O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the last on the science of medicine, by its completion its purpose is achieved, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me appropriate that I complete it for you with this treatise, which is part of the practice with the hand, that is surgery.
For the practice with the hand [surgery] is neglected in our region and in our time completely abandoned, so that its knowledge will soon be lost, and its vestige removed; and nothing will remain of it but a few small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which will mislead the hands and lead those [who practice it] into error and hesitation, so that its meanings are obscured and its strength and art diminished.
And it seems to me appropriate that I revive it [surgery] with the organization of this treatise, according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and that it comes with the forms of cautery instruments and other instruments of the work, as well as the addition of the explanation, and its preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why no good craftsman with his hand [skilled surgeon] is found in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which includes knowing the positions of the limbs and their shapes, and their connection and separation from each other; and have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their origin; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the locations of their exits.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that with one name there is much [said], but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand [surgery].
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy, it is unavoidable that they will fall into error, by which men will die, just as I have seen many of them, because some are instructed in this science and throw themselves into it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and ignorant physician incise a scrofulous abscess on the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed until the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who intended to extract a stone from a man which had developed in his age, and the stone was large, and he came to him and pulled out that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I realized that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened to a black eunuch who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, in his ignorance, prepared the fracture, on the wound, with cushions and splints with strong tightening, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he untied it according to his wishes, and then he re-bandaged it, and then he left it for some days and ordered us to untie the bandage; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot were abscessed, and it resulted in ruin/loss (of the limb).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to loosen the bandage, and tranquility was achieved, and thus his pains lessened.
But however, corruption was already established in the limb, so I could not restrain it, and the corruption in the limb did not cease spreading until the patient perished/died.
And I saw another physician who lanced a cancerous abscess, and it ulcerated for some days, until its malignancy was magnified with its host/patient.
The reason for this is that since cancer is caused by pure melancholic humor, it is not appropriate that it be touched with an iron instrument at all, unless it is in a limb that can be entirely removed.
And therefore, my son, it is necessary for you that the operation with my hand be divided into two parts: that is, into an operation with which health is associated, and into a work with which there is danger in the most serious cases.
And I have already explained this in every place in this book where there is an operation in which there is error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to heed this and abandon that, so that fools do not find a way to criticize and dishonor you.
And so take up your work with care and with God's protection, and [treat] your patients with success and with confidence.
And proceed in the best way, by the grace of God leading to health and laudable success, and leave aside the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and withdraw your efforts from that which makes you afraid, so that hesitation in your faith and in your reputation does not escape you: for more will remain to your glory, and later on high in the world and behind, to your holiness.
As Gualia already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad/severe disease, so that you are not called a bad doctor.
And I have already divided this book according to three chapters.
Here begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter discusses (is arranged/ordered) from the head to the feet, the shapes of the instruments and the cautery irons, and what is necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on drying and perforation, cupping, wounds, and extraction of arrows and similar things, all categorized and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter arranged from the head to the feet, and on the forms of the instruments.
It is fitting that I describe the qualities of the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in which complexion cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discussion of the quality of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a long and lengthy discussion, and subtle in terms of knowledge, and a hidden secret; and a great multitude of wise men have already spoken about it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discussions is enough for me, fearing lengthiness.
And so I say that you may perform cautery on every complexion universally, whether it has matter or not, except for two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
Regarding the hot and dry complexion with matter, they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial to it, and others said the contrary, that cautery is not suitable in an illness which is characterized by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry illness be healed with something that is hot and dry.
And whoever says the opposite of this claims that cautery is beneficial in every hot and dry illness that occurs in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of a man and his humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of a man cold.
And I say, regarding his statement, that experience has already revealed this to me several times.
But still, it is not appropriate for someone to attempt this who has not already been tested and practiced in the chapter on cautery by past practice, and who has not learned about the diversity of men's complexions and the disposition of diseases in themselves, and their causes, and their accompanying symptoms, and the duration of their time.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, for all doctors agree on these, and they are not in disagreement on the benefits of cautery in those cases.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its advantage over cautery with burning medicine, is because fire is simple, the action of which only reaches the part that is cauterized and does not harm the other parts connected to it, except with little harm/side effects.
But the action of burning medicine reaches the other parts distant from it, and very easily brings about an illness in the body that is difficult to heal and occurs very quickly.
But fire, due to its sublime nature and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, but only removes excess.  And it is already known to us through experiment and lengthy service of study in the art concerning the truth of these matters; and therefore I am excused for the length of this discussion. And if it were not forbidden for me to include in this book a secret hidden from you about fire, and the nature of its operation within bodies and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with philosophical demonstration, which would be burdensome to your minds...
And know, sons, that they (doctors/people) disagree about the time in which it is appropriate to perform cautery and suggest the best time is spring.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at all times, because the harm caused by the timing of it is outweighed by the benefit that is obtained with the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and sudden, which do not allow delay because it is feared that from them will come a worse problem, which is greater than the small harm introduced by the timing.
And do not hold in your minds, oh sons, this thing that only the common people and foolish doctors believe, which is that there is not always a recurrence of the illness that is healed by cautery, and they posit this as a consequence.
And the reason is not at all as they think it is, for cautery is nothing but a medicine which alters the constitution and dries the humors which are the cause from which the pains come.
But nevertheless, cautery surpasses medicine by the speed of its effectiveness and the power of its action and the greatness of its master (God/physician).
And it is possible that their illness will return at some point in time, depending on the patient's constitution and the location of their illness, and their strength, and what is prepared in their body from the aggregation of superfluities within them, and what has happened to them in their acquisition of food and similar such causes; but if the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a part with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar things, it is possible that the pain will not return to them, but this happens in only a few cases.
And it is common talk that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a true statement, but not in the way they understand it, for they believe that there is no cure that benefits with medicine or any other thing after cautery.
And the reason is contrary to this; and the understanding that cauteri is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer the methods of treatment in some diseases, and those medicines are not effective, and then at the end of the matter one administers cautery, and it benefits the condition. And from this it appears that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which the common people and many foolish doctors have.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they said this only because of the evenness of gold and the sublimity/purity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not develop an ulcer/inflammation, which is not entirely true, for I have already tested this and have found that it does not do this except in some bodies but not others, and cautery with it (gold) is good and better than with iron, just as they said; but however when you cauterize with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, for it is born/created according to the amount that you want by the redness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you add ignition onto it, the gold melts by the fire, and some flakes fall into the fire, by which the artisan sees through the work/occupation of that.
And therefore cautery is done with iron by us more easily and more appropriately to the correctness of the operation.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have arranged them from the head to the feet, so that the inquirer may find there what he wants.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is applied for coldness and wetness/humidity affecting the brain, from which arise two things:  a runny nose, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain, and generally every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, lethargy and apoplexy, and similar ailments.
The method of this cauterization is as follows: first, order the patient to be purged with a laxative medicine that cleanses the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient and their age and habit.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a square bench and place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the thumb upon the bridge of his nose, between his eyes. But, however, when your middle finger has reached [a certain spot], mark that place with ink, and then burn it with an olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place it upon the spot marked with ink, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand should be constricted little by little, and you should turn it.
And then lift your hand lightly and examine the place, and if you see that some amount of the skull bone, about the size of a chickpea, has been exposed, then raise your hand; and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another one if it has cooled, until you see as much bone exposed as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and leave it for three days.
And then place upon it cotton soaked in pus/discharge and leave it there until the burn scab is destroyed/falls off. And then treat the area with tetrapharmacon ointment until it is healed.
And they already said that when the wound remains open, the pus/discharge drains better, because it is better and more helpful/beneficial.
And some of them said that the nape of the neck should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the surface of the bone is burned.
And then what is burned of the bone should be scraped, and then it should be treated/dressed.
And others said that with the cautery one should reach the back, until it presses strongly on the bone until the bone decays/breaks down, and this is the form of a small incision/cauterization: And they believe that the vapors of the head escape from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated/dressed until it is healed/closed.
But these two types of cautery have not been seen by me at all, except in some men by way of fear/as a frightening method; and leaving that (method), believe me, it is better with health, when it exists; for the head is weakened by the breaking of its continuity in this way as we have testified in other limbs, and even more so when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and I use that one and with that one I work with/for health.
Chapter Two.
Of cauterization of the head. /  Concerning cauterization of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the whole head and this is protracted in the patient, and (he) uses leeches and cupping glasses and head purges and plasters and poultices and puppies and chickens and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we describe and none of those things benefits him, then consider whether the patient's head is of a strong constitution in its nature; and it is not cold, and finds much great cold, then cauterize that (head) with another cautery a little below the first one. And then cauterize that (area) above the crown of his head with a cautery until the skin is broken/split. Incise the skin and uncover the bone to the extent we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery on the back part of the head in the place which is called the vessel/cavity -- and this is the place where cupping glasses are placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient by this finds/experiences very great pain, different from the pain of all other cauterizations of the head.
And I will describe this cautery in its place, for the cautery with which the temples and the occiput are cauterized must be a more subtle/fine/thin cautery than the one with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
On cauterization for a recent migraine (literally: non-ancient migraine).
When a dull pain occurs in a part of the head, or a widespread pain reaching into the eye, let the head be evacuated with cleansing medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on illnesses; and if all is of no avail, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is this: that you take a clove of garlic, peel it, and cut off its ends on both sides. And then incise the site of the pain on the temple with a broad lancet until a space is made there for the clove of garlic, so that it can be hidden there.
And then press on it firmly with a compress for downward restriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place over it a pad soaked in butter until the place scars.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines that I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron is done in this manner: heat with a cautery, of which this is the shape:
and it is called a nail cautery, because its head is shaped like a nail, which has some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small prominence.
And then place it on the area of pain, and hold your hand there - so the bandaging underneath is not burned - and rotate the iron little by little, with speed.
And if the amount burned reaches the depth of the skin, so that half of it [the skin] is [burned], lift your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it there for three days; and then place cotton soaked in pus [likely a typo and should be "butter"], and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife as a cautery, and beware of incising an artery in this migraine, particularly in the ancient way.
Chapter Four.
On cauterization of an ancient migraine.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said about the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the sections on diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see by the illness that what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is not sufficient for him, then it is necessary that you heat the knife-cautery until it is white-hot, then you mark on the place of the pain with a line the length of which is half the length of the finger, or about that; and place your hand down once and press it until you incise the artery [possibly scalp/skin is meant, not artery] and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be aware of the continuation of the mandible, which is moved during chewing, and that the muscle is not damaged nor the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And be on guard and cautious about the flow of blood from the artery that is cut, because there is danger in cutting it, and especially for the one who is ignorant of what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; and ceases the operation there at the beginning.
And I will make a reminder about the management of blood flow arising from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see with this disease that this cautery is not enough and you see the patient's body can tolerate it, cauterize him in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we discussed in the chapter on narrowing of the artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more appropriate.
Chapter 5.
On cauterization for earaches.  (or,  Of the cauterization of ear pains.)
When pain comes to the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatment which I mentioned in the section/chapter, and the pain is not removed, then heat the cautery, and this is the one called "puncta," of which this is the form/shape:
And then, near the ignition/heating, apply the "puncta" around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or around each temple if the pain is in each one. Slowly move the cautery along the root of the ear after you have marked the location with ink/dye. (Or "a probe")
And if you cauterize with the "puncta" ten points on the whole ear and cauterize it, then treat the spots until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of "turtura" (likely a medical condition, perhaps "torticollis" or "stiff neck," given the context).  It could also refer to a "turtledove," potentially suggesting a specific remedy or anatomical analogy.
Turtura," which is treated with cauterization, is simply one of the conditions that comes from phlegm, as we have said in the sections on diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that kind of "turtura" with irons (surgical instruments) and head purges and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: the first behind the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third near the joining of the two lips (of the wound/affected area - context suggests the back of the neck).
And place your cautery opposite the diseased part, because softening (of the tissues/reduction of the condition) will only occur in the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize him with the cautery directly at the extremity of the ear then a little higher above the crown of the head, and another on the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the little finger, and leave your hand with the cautery until an amount half the thickness of a string has been burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of knife, the form of which has been described previously. But however, it is more subtle than that one, a little like you see; and it needs to cut away any excess thickness.
And then treat the area with what has been described previously until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On cauterization for chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment is ineffective and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on all the corners of his head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck/nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And cauterize also above the pit of the stomach; and further back as well; and then let him be treated with the appropriate procedure.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness/loss of memory caused by phlegm/inflammation.
It is fitting that at the beginning the patient be given large electuaries (thick medicinal pastes/drinks) and brain-cleansing pills.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply on the nape of their neck the plaster of citrus described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply this several times, for this is a type of cautery. And do this following the same method which I have already described.
And so, if he is healed by these, good; and if not, then make three cauteries on the back part of his head, which should descend from the highest part of the head down to the lowest part of the neck. And leave a space between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been explained previously.
But if you want an addition and the patient can tolerate it, then make a cautery in the middle area; and if you want a greater addition, then cauterize on the two temples.
Then treat it until it is healed; and the cautery should be olive-shaped according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
On Cauterization for Paralysis and Softening of the Whole Body.
It is fitting that you proceed with cleansing of the head with jars and with what we have said; then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with cauteries on all the temples of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body, when it is excessive and the patients tolerate it and the disease is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from his tailbone a lot, and lift your hand. And then treat him until he is healed, and this should be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter 10.
On Cauterization for Epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And thus you must cleanse his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatments which we have mentioned in the section when the disease is severe and the patient can tolerate the strong medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that cleanse the brain, before this for many days, with beneficial foods.
And then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the preceding method, and with another cautery on the occiput (back of the head), and cauterize on each "horn"/temple of the head. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, for paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped as was previously mentioned/described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a subtle/fine cautery in this manner.
Chapter 11.
On cauterization for melancholia.  (or "Of the cauterization of melancholy")
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries that we have described in the section on paralysis.  (Alternatively, "...in the chapter having to do with paralysis.")
And if the cause of melancholia is superfluity declining into melancholia, and the patient's body is humid/moist, then give him to drink that which cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division/chapter. (The word "division" could also refer to a section or part of the treatise.)
And then shave the patient's head, and then make a decent-sized hole (or "fontanel", or possibly a kind of seton) on him over the crown, and then locate/place it in the middle of the head.  ("tortora" is a tricky word here, context suggests a surgical opening.)
And the patient should be sitting upright and be held on all sides / supported all around.
And then apply an ointment; and old sheep dung is most suitable for this.  (The term "pus digne" literally means "more worthy" but effectively translates to "most suitable" in this context). And warm it over the fire with moderate heat, to a temperature which the said (patient) can tolerate when it is placed on him. And then pour it in the middle of his head, within the circular area (previously prepared), and leave it there until it has cooled.
And do this with the patient once a week, along with the other treatments, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him by making small points according to his illness, but hold your hand with a cautery arranged thusly: this is the kind of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
And then apply this dressing soaked in dung or chicken fat.
Chapter .xii. (or Chapter 12)
On cauterization for water descending into the eye. (or ...for watery eyes.)
When the onset of watery eyes has been revealed to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division (section/chapter), then begin and give the patient to drink that which cleanses his head, and keep him from all humidity/moisture.
And have him sweat in a bath properly for some days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize him with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of a flow of water in both eyes, or on one side if it is the beginning of the water in one eye.
Incise with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the nape of the neck; and let the cauteries be at that distance across the width of the temples.
And beware of blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see blood flow from him, stop it with any medicine that you can.
And I will come with knowledge which is in the extraction of arteries and with their abscission (removal/cutting) and the precaution against blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck below the two bones with two cauteries produced/placed posteriorly/at the back.
Chapter .xiii. (Thirteen)
On the cauterization of old tears/lachrymal ducts (or possibly old wounds/ulcers).
When tears/discharge from the eyes are chronically present, and it is verified that this is due to cold phlegmatic superfluities through the veins and arteries which are in the obvious/exposed place of the head, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the chapter on the commencement of water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery on the middle place of the head and with two cauteries on the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries on the neck below the two bones. And if you need addition (further treatment), then cauterize that on all sides of the corner of the eye on the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter .xiv. (Fourteen)
On the cauterization of foul odor of the nose.
When you treat that with what we have said in the division/section and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient cochia pills to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a middling cautery with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize him with a clove-shaped cautery with two cauterizations above both eyebrows, leaving the hairs a little bit. (i.e., leaving some hair.)
And beware of the artery so that you do not burn it.
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form/shape.
Chapter 15.
On cauterization for softening of the eyelids/eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or by moisture/humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar caustic; and this is its form/shape:
And you, cauterize a little above both eyebrows with two cauteries on each side, and let them be distant from the temples. And let the length of each cauterization be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too hard with the cautery, but only enough so that a third of the eyelid is burned/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
On cauterization of the eyelid when the eyelashes turn inwards and prick the eye.
When the eyelashes turn inwards so that they prick the eye, cauterization is done in two ways: either with iron or with a burning medicine.
If cauterizing with fire, first instruct the patient to let their eyelashes grow, like those who are cleaning them, until they are long and even; and if it pricks him above their origin, then you bind his eyes with a bandage so that he doesn't move until the lashes have grown.
Then when they are grown and even, place the patient's head on your lap, and then mark on his eyelid with ink, a mark in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and the beginning of the mark should be near the lashes.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or psyllium mucilage over the eye.
And then ignite the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, in many passes, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely and visibly burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the lashes raised away from the puncture of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with an ointment on it until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if something returns through the lashes near their roots and the eyelid is softened, then cauterize it again just as you did the first time.
But if the lashes are on the eyelid below, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is levelled and the lashes don't prick the eye.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is this: you instruct the patient to let the hairs grow until they are long and even, and then a shape of a myrtle leaf is made from a piece of cloth.
And then take one ounce of known soap and one ounce of quicklime or something similar, and then mix these things well, and do this quickly so that it doesn't cool; and then spread this on the cloth which is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place it over one or both eyes; and place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And put the said (cotton/cloth) on the medicine and press it a little and don't remove it until the patient feels the medicine's bite, for its bite is like fire.
And when (the patient) perceives this, that he feels the bite, then remove the medicine and wipe it with your finger; when the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then examine if you see the eyelid is already lifted.  If it is lifted, treat the scar with fire or by incision, for then it is well; and if not, return the medicine to the place where the medicine hasn't yet taken effect and don't hesitate until your work is even and the eye is corrected.
And then place cotton with an ointment on it until the burned area's eschar is removed, and then treat it with eye ointment or another (ointment) until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, after your operation, that you watch with vigilant diligence that none of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days softens and some part of it falls into the eye, then repeat your treatment on the softened part of the eyelid, just as you did at the beginning, and then surround it (the eye, presumably with cotton/ointment) until it is healed.
And this is the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And know that people's eyes are diverse in size, both large and small, and therefore your surgical repair should be done accordingly.
And the path of correctness will not be hidden from the one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
On cauterization of a fistula that is in the lacrimal duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the sections on diseases and your treatment is not effective, then you must cauterize it in this manner: order the patient to place their head on your lap, and have an assistant hold their head between your hands in such a way that it is not moved or agitated.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or psyllium mucilage over their eye.
And then heat the cautery, which has this shape:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at one end with which the cauterization is done.
And if you wish, it can be penetrating to the other end. And if you want, it can come out like a tent/probe.  (The word "tenta" could mean a tent, or more likely in this surgical context, a probe or director.)
But however, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw blood from it and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which should be well heated, over it, and hold your hand a little above the cautery from the eye to the area of the nose, so that your hand does not err or the patient be disturbed, and then place the cautery in the fat of the eye socket and cauterize it.
And so, if at the beginning of your cauterization, it reaches the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place cotton with ointment on it and around it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and scraped as appropriate, as described in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some first described: cut into the location of the fistula and split it open, and then insert into that fissure a thin cannula which is shaped to fit; and pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with the cannula firmly, and do not let the patient move so that the molten lead does not reach his eye; and you must place cotton soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye, for lead adheres to the site of the fistula and heals it with wondrous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a passage through the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as will come in its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On cauterization of the fissure of the eyelids. (Or, more literally:  Of the cauterization of the crack/split of the eyelids.)
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures with what we have said in the sections (or divisions) and your treatment is not effective, then heat the small knife-shaped cautery according to this form which follows:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then place it carefully and gently within those fissures until the cautery reaches the depth/bottom of the fissures.
And then treat that with ointments/plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
On cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula or in the palate or in the roots of the teeth an abscess develops, and then forms an arm (sinus tract), and then ruptures, and then becomes chronic, and discharges pus and becomes a fistula, and then you treat it and the treatment is ineffective, then you must heat a cautery according to the size that can be received in the fistula, and then place that heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand steady until the heated iron reaches its bottom, and do this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about the treatment until it is healed.
And so if the diseased tissue is cut out and it heals, good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the area and remove the corrupted bone as it will come in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
On cauterization of the teeth and softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and it is ineffective, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat a cautery, the shape of which will come later, so that you place the cannula over the tooth and insert the heated cautery into it quickly, and hold your hand steady a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cautery a few times as you wish.
And then the patient rinses their mouth with salt water and holds it for an hour, and then spits it out, because the loose tooth is strengthened and the softened gum is tightened and the corrupt moisture is dried.
Chapter 21.
On cauterization for toothache.
When toothache is due to cold, or there is a cold or worm in it, or you cannot effect a cure with medicine, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterize with cow dung, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with dung is done by taking cow dung and boiling it in an iron pot, and then taking cotton and wrapping it around the end of a probe, and then dipping it in the boiled dung, and placing it quickly on the aching tooth before it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in the aforementioned dung, and place it on the aching tooth, and then place a hot iron on it until the iron reaches the depth of the tooth's cavity.
But cauterization with fire is that you should use a small tube of brass or iron, and let it have some thickness in its center so that the sharpness/intensity of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
And then heat the cautery, the shape of which will come [is appropriate - contextually implied], and place it on the tooth and keep your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times, for the pain will be removed either on that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, after the application of the cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and hold it for an hour and then spit it out; and this is the manner of cauterization.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as best you can.
And this is the form of the cannula/tube:
Chapter 22.
On the cauterization of extrophulos/protrusions (likely referring to hemorrhoids).
When protrusions/extrophulos are caused by phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to digestion by medicines, and you desire their gentle digestion, then you should heat a hollow cautery, the form of which is as follows, having two open ends so that the smoke may escape from the cautery through the other end, and place the heated cautery on the swelling/abscess once, and again if needed, until it reaches the core/depth of the swelling/abscess.
And if the swelling/abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the swelling/abscess, and then leave it for three days and place upon it cotton soaked in butter/ointment until what the fire has burned is removed.
And then treat it with ointment, bandages/compresses, and cloths until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
On cauterization of hoarseness of voice and constriction of breath/windpipe.
When humors come upon the canal of the lung, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of complexion, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and afterwards cauterize him/her behind the root of the throat in the depressed/affected area.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn his uvula/throat, but only a little.
And cauterize him/her with another cautery behind the joint of the neck on his/her last vertebra.  (Could also mean lowest vertebra - context unclear) And his/her cautery should be nail-shaped according to the preceding method.
And then treat him/her with what was described previously until he/she is healed.
Chapter 24.
On cauterization for lung disease and cough.
When a cough and lung disease are caused by cold humors, and the patient does not have a fever or phthisis (tuberculosis), and it is a chronic illness, then cauterize him/her with two cauteries above the furcula (collarbone) in the depressed areas along the length and width; and with another cautery in the middle of the feet between the two ankle bones (malleoli); and the cautery should be nail-shaped according to the preceding method.
And if you wish, perform your cauterization by puncturing with the cautery that is called "puncta," the form of which was described previously in the section on cauterization for ear pain.
And the punctures should be between each one (of the cauterizations? Perhaps referring to multiple punctures in the locations already mentioned); and afterwards treat him/her with the same medicine that was previously described until he/she is healed.
And the cautery should have three prongs because the cauterization is done with it: with one application, you can make three cauteries in this manner:
Chapter 25.
On cauterization of the uvula, which is the tip of the little tongue (or soft palate).
When the tip of the uvula is dislocated because of slippery humors and is not stabilized at the time of its return from its dislocation until it is accustomed to returning to its place, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again so that it happens, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned over on their back or on their left side; and then lift the uvula which is on the inner part of the soft palate to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated to the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery having two points; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the uvula until it penetrates to the other side; and the figure of the cauterization will then have four cauterized points.
And sometimes it is arranged according to this count and for that reason there are eight cauterizations.
And then place cooked leeks with salt on the cauterization, and then have the patient lie still, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is strengthened/healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the moisture is removed and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
On cauterization of the stomach.
When coldness and much moisture comes into the stomach, so that it deviates from its normal complexion and those [cold and moist humors] multiply and remain within it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and it is not strengthened, then it is necessary that the patient lie supine on his back, and extend his arms and his thighs, and then cauterize him with three cauteries: with one cautery under the feet by the length of a finger with a nail-shaped cautery, and with two cauteries on both sides of the cautery, so that a triangular cauterization pattern results; and keep them separated so that they are not touching, which would cause putrefaction/infection.
And the depth of the cautery should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And the shape of the cautery should be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the shape of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized by puncturing in someone who is afraid of this cautery [the large circular one]: that is, you mark points on the stomach as many as you want with a probe, and then cauterize it at those points with a cautery; and then treat it with the aforementioned treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On cauterization of a cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver due to coldness or humidity or excessive wind, such that its normal complexion is thrown off by superfluous discharge, and the patient is treated with what we have said and does not recover, then the patient should lie on their back, with their neck raised, and you mark with a probe three cauteries in this form and this same size on the liver below the hypochondria, where one can perceive the lying position of the person.
And let the space between each cautery be the width of a finger, and let the cautery [mark/incision] be the length of a straight body [of a date pit, according to other texts].
And do not press your hand heavily with the cautery.
And if the amount [of burnt flesh] that is adhered is half the size/thickness of a hazelnut shell, then add no more; and if the patient is standing on their feet; and if not standing, then they should be lying down, with their thighs extended and their arms raised.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these [points] with a bladed cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to go too deep in the cauterization, only burn as much as a hazelnut shell’s thickness,  so that the entire area is cauterized and [the heat] reaches the intestines, because the shell is thin there; and know this.
Chapter 28.
On the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When a growth or abscess occurs in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its membrane, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will find [it] heavy and the pain will be sharp.
And if it is in the membrane of the liver, the pain will be very sharp and sometimes it even tires the physicians who treat it.
Therefore, it is necessary that the patient be turned on his neck/back; then probe the location of the abscess with a probe/instrument; then ignite the cautery which is shaped like a radish, the form of which has been shown in the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the diseased matter is burned, until it reaches the diaphragm, until healthy blood comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And this type of cautery should only be used by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated;  but even with experience, one should rarely be bold with this operation, but rather leave it to us [experienced physicians], and that is better.
Chapter 29.
On Cauterization of Pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy should be done in this manner: you take one dried root of long birthwort, as long as can be found, and the thickness of a finger.
Then submerge it in oil and dry it by the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in the place which is between the joining of the collarbone and the neck; and with two small cauteries placed below [the first cautery], angled slightly towards the area below the chin; and with two cauteries in the area sloping between the fifth and sixth ribs, angled slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the foot; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the place between the two shoulders; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the foot, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary to squeeze your hand with the cautery, rather let it be held loosely in the hand.
And some of the ancients have said that some men use an iron cautery resembling a ray, and ignite it, and place it in the area between the ribs, until they reach the abscess with it and pus is drawn out, as we have said concerning abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, for one may die immediately or a fistula may develop in that place which cannot be healed.
Chapter 30.
On cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have said about its treatment in the section [on diseases] and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all of which are direct, one of which is that you make three or four cauterizations in a line along the length of the spleen, following the pattern of the cauterizations of the liver, the figure of which has preceded [this chapter].
And let the space between each cauterization be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization be the same as we have described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and the patient should lie on his back.
And there is another way to cauterize, which is that you ignite the cautery having two openings, as we have described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and lift the rib cage which is next to the spleen where it may be according to the position of the patient on their left side.
And the lifting motion with which you raise the rib cage should be along the length of the body, so that the cauterizations are made along the width of the body.
And then insert the two openings, very hot, into that space, so that with them you penetrate the rib cage from the other side.
And then remove the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three openings.
And then you treat the cauterized area after it has stopped emitting sparks for several days, because this is more important than the other preceding steps in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
On Cauterization for Dropsy (or Edema).
Cauterization is not used in general dropsy/edema, but only in ascites specifically.
When you treat the dropsical/edematous patient with the methods of treatment that we have mentioned in the division/chapter and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you cauterize him/her with four cauteries around the navel and with one cautery on the stomach, and with another cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae/spine of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery on the stomach. And the amount of depth of the cautery should be proportional to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cautery wound open so that it drains fluid for a long time.
And do not stop the patient's treatment soon after the cauterization with the necessary procedures, so that two intentions/purposes can be added to it, and healing will be hastened for him with assistance/help.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped according to the form of that which is described above, and the one with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped according to its preceding form/description.
Chapter 32.
On cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are abscessed/swollen in dropsy and are filled with serous/citrine fluid, it is necessary that you cauterize the feet on the top of the foot in the space between the little toe and the ring toe. And lift your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then lift your hand and do not remove it entirely, because the serous/citrine fluid will drain out.
The cautery is of this shape [likely refers to an accompanying image]: And then make two cauterizations on both thighs, and on each thigh the cauterization should be made with the tip of a knife-shaped cautery. And the cauterization should be along the length of the thigh: one above the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and two cauterizations on each thigh.  (So a total of four, two on each thigh).
And leave the wound open without dressing it for a long time so that any fluid may drain out of it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On cauterization of the abdomen/belly. (Literally "body of the belly")
When the abdomen, due to cold and humidity within, weakens the faculties—namely the digestive and retentive faculties, which are in the stomach and intestines—and this is treated with the usual methods of treatment and they do not help, and sometimes the patient, tolerating cautery, shows improvement, then cauterize him with a large cautery on his stomach as described previously in the cauterization of the stomach, with the circular cautery and with four subtle cauteries around the navel, with a subtle nail-shaped cautery; and with a cautery on the hip over the large trochanter, or with two cauteries.
And if you sometimes see excessive humidity and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize him on the pubis and over the intestines, and very lightly apply two cauteries on the stomach and near the groins, for it is a complete curative treatment with which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When there are, in the posterior, old hemorrhoids of a nature consisting of thick cold humors or corrupted humidities, and they are treated with what we have said in the section [on hemorrhoids] and your treatment is not effective, then cauterize with three cauteries on the bone below the two small vertebrae and the small triangular one, and with a cautery above the navel down two fingers [widths].
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and his face appears pale/sickly, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cautery as described previously; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On cauterization for fistulas after their excision.
When you excise a fistula, then you should heat the cautery that is shaped like a spoke/ray. Then insert that heated [cautery] into the excised fistula, and guide it with your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if there are many such [fistulas], then cauterize each of them as we have described, and then treat them with the ointments you deem appropriate until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery over some area, it is slower in healing.
Chapter 36.
On cauterization of fistulas that are located in the posterior of the body in the lower parts.
When the [usual treatment/nature] doesn't respond to the procedure we have described in its place, and the patient is frightened by this, let it be treated with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the posterior, and it is old, with decaying sanies (pus) and corrupted moisture, then what is most necessary is to probe it with a thin probe, and then determine the extent of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then heat the cautery that is ray-shaped (or like a spoke). And then insert this heated cautery into the fistula, according to the depth to which you inserted the probe.
And repeat [inserting] the cautery into it until the corrupted flesh is burned, either one or two or three times, according to the extent of your need.
And beware of burning a nerve or a vein; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think it is not penetrating [deeply].
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the infected matter is cut away from it, and remains so for a long time, then know that it is already completely healed.
And if the infected matter is not removed from it, then know that it is penetrating either into the depth of the bone where it is corrupted, or that this [condition] is caused by things which will be discussed in their [proper] place.
Chapter 37.
On cauterization of the kidneys/loins. (The word "ronhos" likely refers to the lumbar region, commonly referred to as the kidneys in older medical texts.)
When pain occurs in the loins/kidneys due to cold or excessive flatulence, and because of this the patient's side is numb, then it is necessary that you cauterize him on the loins/kidneys with a cautery on each kidney/loin with a nail-shaped cautery, which was mentioned previously.
And very lightly cauterize with the second one a little below, so that there will be three cauterizations in a line, and thus they will be more helpful in healing.
Chapter 38.
On cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize him in the lowest part above the bladder, where the pubic hair begins, and cauterize with one cautery on his right side, and with another on his left.
And let the length of each cauterization be according to the size of the patient's finger's width; and cauterize him with a cautery on the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped as described previously.
Chapter 39.
On cauterization of the uterus/womb.
When coldness and humidity occur in the uterus, and therefore pregnancy is prevented, and the menses are corrupted, and their onset is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then the area should be cauterized with three cauteries around the navel, as we have said regarding cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the area lowest on the back, or with two cauteries, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On cauterization for dislocation of the hip.
When mucous humidities infiltrate the hip area, and cause it to become dislocated, a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it bulges.
Then it is necessary to cauterize the patient on the area of his hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with ink the circumference of the area as it is swollen, so that the affected part falls in the middle of the circle.
And use the cautery whose form was previously described for cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not available to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And the cauterization should be done according to the thickness of the entire swelling/growth; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On cauterization of the sciatic vein/nerve.
When pain occurs in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and humidity, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, and this treatment is outdated, then you must purge the patient of gross humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial or similar pills; and then cauterize him.
And the method of cauterization in this case is of two kinds: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
It will be according to two known methods, one of which is that you cauterize him on the area of his hip with three triangular cauteries in this way; and make a good depth with them. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes the cautery is placed over the middle part, on the head of the hip, for which there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle, the form of which is described in the cauterization for dislocation of the hip, where the circle should be continuous and burn around the thickness of the entire thigh.
And this is the manner of cautery in sciatica when the pain is located in the hip and does not extend down to the thigh.
But if it extends down to the thigh, then cauterize it with two cauteries on the thigh over the place where the patient feels the pain; and with one cautery on the ankle about four fingers below the outside part.
And if you use a knife-shaped cautery, the depth of the cauterization should be according to the thickness of the thigh.
And if the patient feels that the pain extends down to the area near the toes, then cauterize him there where he feels it with a cautery, making three points or four or more if needed.
And if the patient feels pain below the knee towards the thigh, then cauterize him there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterizations not to go so deep with the cautery that you burn a nerve or a large artery, and thereby cause the patient harm or contraction.
And I have seen some of those who cauterized on the ankle and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg became so swollen that the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then severe diarrhea occurred, and soon after, death.
And if the pain is in two places together, cauterize each one according to this method.
And some wise men have said regarding cauterization of the hip, a cautery of which this is the manner: make a circle of iron, the circumference of which is half a palm; and let its opening be the size of a date pit or a little less.
And inside that circle let there be another third; and let the space between them be the size of a finger joint; and let these circles be open in two places; and let the length of these cases be a joint or two joints, and make in them an iron mark decently fitted within the circles; and this is its form/shape:
Then it should be heated in the fire until it glows red and emits sparks, and then it should be placed on the part of the hip, with the patient lying on their healthy side. And then cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
And then leave it for three days; then plaster it with a poultice and leave the wound open for many days; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Albucasis says: Oh, sons, we have used this type of cautery little because of the fear of its appearance, and because we have found few who look upon it.
But it is among the best cauteries for those who have tolerated it, and it finds its place with them.
But cautery with burning medicines is when you make two circles having a perforated handle in which they hold the bolt of the iron door; let the length of the walls in their height be two nuts or about that.
And let the space between each one be the width of a finger; and let them be open below and above, and let them contain themselves between each other in this manner:
Then place it on the part of the hip [with] the patient lying on his side, and press your hand on the part of the hip; then take hot water and pour it between the two circles, and let its quality be with the fire little by little, and keep it [there] for the amount of one hour, and let the patient endure the biting and the burning of it — for it produces a biting like that of fire — until the biting ceases.
Then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe the hot water from the whole hip with fresh water.
And then leave it for three days and place lint on it until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will run from it; this is latest in improvement/healing.
Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
And if the pain extends into the thigh or the leg, make him a bath in which he soaks in water just as is done in the hip bath likewise.
And this is the method of the hot water bath, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the correction of medicines. Take one part each of alkali and unslaked lime, crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which should be perforated by a small hole, according to the size of the ray (fish?). / according to the amount that fits within the ray.  (This line is tricky -  "ray" could refer to a fish, a beam of light, a measurement, or something else. Context might help clarify.)
And place another glazed pot beneath the bottom of the first pot, and pour fresh water over the alkali and quicklime, covering them by a finger's breadth. Then compress these with your hand, pressing firmly, and let the pot drip the sharp water into the glazed pot below.
Then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for it will then become very strongly acrid, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other limbs, because it acts like fire.
And there are some medicines with which a burn may also be cauterized, such as Armenian wormwood, anacardic honey (or cashew honey), and lime with soap, mixed together.
And Galien recounts the operation and treatment of an ancient man with which pain of the hip and sciatic nerve is cured, and highly praises its effectiveness, saying that no other treatment is needed, and that it is healed by a single application, as he relates, because the man entered a bath very quickly, and thus was already cured of it.
And take fresh spurge, and if fresh is not found, take a fresh fig; and mash it well with a little grease and place it on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bind it.
And leave it for the duration of three hours or for the amount of time that the patient feels relief from the burning sensation.
And then place him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, place him in the sun, because the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use a cautery with these medicines except after the bowels have been evacuated.
But, Escorides [Dioscorides] said that when the styptic vein is cauterized with goat dung, it benefits the patient; and the cautery should be with it in this manner: take wool and soak it in old oil, and place it between the arch of the foot and the ankle.
And then take dry goat dung and ignite it with fire until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. And then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of the burning reaches the hip and the pain subsides.
And this type of cautery is called cautery of cows (or cautery of oxen).
Chapter 42.
On cauterization for back pain.
Sometimes pain is in the back from several causes, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and from similar causes, by the influx of cold and moist matter.
And cautery is not appropriate except in this one type, which is caused by an outpouring of cold material (or humor).
Therefore, after purging the patient with fetid pills and the like, it is necessary to make three lines along the width of the back where the pain is located, then mark with ink where to apply five or more cauteries on each line, according to the patient's tolerance and strength.
And the cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries of a medium nail shape, in this way:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of kyphosis/humpback. (Gilbositat likely refers to a hump or curvature of the spine.)
This disease often happens to young children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs with tightness in the spine along with elevation and movement (of the spine/back).
And you will find on the last vertebra of the back and its joints that it already begins to protrude over the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery according to this shape: The cautery should cover the entire part of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize two or three lines in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But be careful that cautery is not administered on a swelling caused by a nerve spasm.
Chapter 44.
On cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pains are caused by cold humors, which infiltrate every part of the body, then when pain comes to the feet, it is customary for doctors to call this gout specifically/properly.
And so when gout is treated as a cold ailment with the appropriate methods and the pains do not subside, then cautery remains (as a treatment option).
And you should cauterize (the patient) near the evacuation point in the area around the foot joint with many cauterizations.
And it should be an olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that points be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise up to the knees or other joints, and this happens many times, then cauterize the patient on each painful spot with three or four cauterizations on all sides with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if you need many cauterizations, then cauterize the area but don't press your hand too hard with the cautery; rather, [the burn] should only be about the thickness of a string/cord.
And if the pains rise up into one or two knees, then administer the cautery which we have discussed in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called chiragra/gout of the hand, then puncture around the wrists as they turn, puncture in those two lines.
And if pain remains in the said fingers, then puncture around the wrists on each knuckle and on the back of the hand.
And if the pains rise after a few days to the elbows or the muscles, then cauterize each of these with the aid of assistance [i.e., helpers/instruments].
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and the taking of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and phlegm is evacuated with this cautery, they will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On cauterization of ruptures/hernias. (The word "rompeduras" suggests something broken or ruptured, thus hernias fits the surgical context.)
When a rupture occurs in the groins, with the intestines descending before the bandage to the genitals, and this is a recent onset, then you must order the patient to stop eating on that day and to use softening/laxative things so that the tear/opening in their belly descends.
And then hold the patient between your hands, supporting them on their back, and tell them to hold their breath until it is completely exhaled or the bandage or intestines appear.
And then return/replace it (the hernia/intestines) with your fingers and mark with ink the rupture above the pubic bone with a mark whose extremity should be similar to a half-circle on the upper parts of the body.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is its shape:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. And then return the intestine or the hernia to its cavity, and then place your hand over the place so that the intestine does not come out.
And you have already opened (the space) between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion there, and another assistant should be on his legs, and another on his chest, who holds his hands. (Or potentially "on his feet," depending on the precise meaning of "pietz," which could mean feet or chest in some medieval contexts).
And then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, with the cautery held straight, and keep it there until you reach the bone of the pubis to the middle of the finger; and repeat this another time if you have not reached the bone on the first try.
And carefully watch the protruding intestine at the time of your cauterization, so that you do not burn that intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that when you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation will not be successful / you will not have achieved your goal.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small according to their size and dimensions.
And then treat the cauterized area for three days with ointment until the burn scab is removed/falls off.
And then treat it with the other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and the wound are sealed/closed.
And it is necessary that you adjust his nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which cleanses his bowels, and so that the intestine does not protrude due to the strain of healing.
And then when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And he should be spared from work and from excess of food and drink, and from loud speech, for when he follows this regimen, he will be completely healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in the other parts of the belly, and this at the beginning, and if you wish them not to recur, then cauterize that rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is excised from the swelling be about a third of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it will not return.
And some earlier [physicians] said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this manner, after it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed across the width on the highest side of the rupture, and another line above; and place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion/testicle pain. (Torcio can refer to twisting, which could relate to a hernia or testicular torsion.)
When pain occurs in any limb due to a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and you cannot achieve a cure with medicines, then it is necessary that you cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery should be applied to the whole limb according to its size, its smallness, its strength, its weakness, the location of the torsion/pain, and the degree of pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary in these pains that the limb be punctured, from that limb to the one that is near it, so it is necessary that you follow these with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes [treated] with cautery with very great benefit, and especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so when you want to cauterize that one, consider if the leprosy is advanced and you have treated it with what we have said in the division and it doesn't diminish, nor stop, and you fear that the corruption will take over the patient's whole body and their entire constitution, then cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second a little below it, near the forehead at the hairline; and two on the temples; and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, press your hand with the cautery a little so that it makes a small impression on the bone, and the scabs are removed from it, so that the expiration of thick vapors is relieved from him; and cauterize over the spleen according to what is needed/prescribed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and appears manifestly, then you must cauterize him with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheeks, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with a large one on the vertebrae near the tailbone, and one on the tailbone, and with two on the hips, and with one on each hip, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the elbows, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the palms of his feet; and cauterize him on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of his feet, and on the wrists of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are on the heel so that you don't burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and with another on the pit of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as healthy people do, because his body is already numb.
And the cauteries must be large or small depending on the limbs and joints according to what was previously described regarding the types of instruments.
And then treat the cauterized area with cooked chickpea flour mixed with honey and the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On Cauterization for Numbness/Stupor.  (Or, Cauterization in the Case of Stupor)
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small.
And let your cautery penetrate a little into the thickness of the skin. (The word "codena" likely refers to skin or flesh) And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
