And sometimes numbness, which occurs in the hands and feet, and in the vertebrae of the back near the exits of the nerves which move that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
But no one should presume to do this except someone who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the paths of the nerves moving throughout the entire body.
Chapter 49.
On cauterization of morphea, which is albaras (likely a skin condition, possibly vitiligo or another form of discoloration).
When albaras (morphea) is old and you can't treat it with any medicinal ointments, then you cauterize it with a pointed cautery, lightly, according to the size of the affected skin area, until the whiteness and discoloration are removed, and its color is changed.
And then treat it with a mixture of chickpea and lentil flour, rose oil, arnica leaves, and either pigeon blood or swallow blood, one part of each all mixed together.  Then spread this on a cloth and place it on the affected area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
On the cauterization of cancer.
When a cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its circumference, just as it is circumscribed.
And the scholars said that it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle; but I have not seen this, for I fear it may ulcerate, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more correct that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51. (Roman numerals LI)
On the cauterization of swelling/tumor. (The word "adubilati" likely refers to a swelling, tumor, or possibly an abscess.  The "-ati" ending suggests a plural form, but the context implies a singular concept.)
When a swelling is slow in ripening/maturing, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or due to the patient's age, because he is old and decrepit, with little blood, or because of the weather/season, and you want to hasten the ripening/maturing of the swelling; then cauterize around its circumference with many small pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten its maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form/shape: and place it in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin/membrane. (The word "codena" likely means skin, membrane, or a layer of tissue.)
And if the cautery [is applied] in the place that is lower/dependent, so that the arm's body may be relieved/drained; then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On Cauterization with Corrosives/Caustics.
A corrosive/caustic is an ulcer spreading in the limb, which corrodes/eats away at it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive ulcer in a place that tolerates cauterization with fire, then you should ignite small pointed cauteries, as many as is appropriate for the place where the corrosive ulcer is located.
And then cauterize it all around until you eradicate all of the corrosion and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place over the cauterized areas a dressing soaked in mixed oil until the scabs are all removed/have fallen off along with all the infection/corrosion.
And then treat it with ointments that promote flesh/tissue growth.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh grows where there is no more infection, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on what remains in the infected places.
And a corrosive agent is sometimes medicated with a sharp/strong medicine, because that is used in place of a cautery. But nevertheless cautery with fire provides the easiest/quickest relief.
And I have already described the treatment with sharp/strong medicine in the section/chapter (on it), so take that when you need it for him/her.
Chapter 53.
On cauterization of nails and other similar (things/ailments).
Many times an ailment called a corn/nail occurs on the parts under the foot, and it is a large, deep-seated cause which hurts the foot; and cautery on these is of two kinds, that is, cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is that you heat a concave cautery similar to a vulture's feather, which should be made of iron according to the size which entirely contains the nail/corn, and it should have thin edges/margins.
And then place that heated (cautery) on the nail/corn, and then turn your hand with the cautery around the nail/corn until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail/corn. And leave that for three days until it begins to throw off/expel pus. And then plaster it with wild mallows crushed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for it will be eradicated from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it doesn't heal, and many times this happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you must administer in those (places) cautery with water, and that is that you take a small tube of copper or iron or a vulture's feather, and place it on the nail/corn; and then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube, and keep your hand (there), and you turn it with the pressure of your hand completely, and let the edge of the tube be pressed into the root of the nail/corn, and the hot water will find a way to the depth of the root of the nail/corn; and let the patient wait a bit, for the burning of the hot water for an hour, and then remove it, for the nail/corn with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those, and then with the next one until you come to all of them that are in the body.
And then treat them, after the roots of the nails/corns are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On cauterization of tremor/shivering/shaking.  (Likely referring to a treatment for tremors or shivering)
When someone experiences tremors due to cold in the nerves or from a fever caused by cold, or for other similar reasons, then you must cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cauterie; and with a cauterie on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cauterie.
And their tremor will subside and their recovery from the cold illness will hasten.
Chapter 55.
On cauterization for white spots/patches appearing on the body. ( *Albati* likely refers to some kind of skin discoloration or lesion perceived as white.)
When white spots/patches appear on the body, and there are cracked pustules which are made of gross corrupted matter; then it is necessary at the onset of their appearance, that each head of the fistula be cauterized with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, the tip of which is ignited with fire; or with the roots of long birthwort (*Aristolochia longa*); or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes cautery is also appropriate at the beginning of its expulsion in this manner; and they [the pustules/lesions?] are not believed [understood?], and the superfluity making them is broken up, and heals from them.  (This sentence is a little unclear; the meaning of "cregudas" in this context is uncertain.)
But it is necessary that this be done after evacuation by phlebotomy (blood-letting).
Chapter 56.
On cauterization of blood flow occurring from the incision of an artery.
Too often there occurs a flow from an artery that is incised below the wound which happens deeply, or from the incision of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and other similar things, and it is difficult to remedy this.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you should quickly cover the opening of the artery with your hand, and place your finger on it, pressing it strongly many times until the flow of blood is stopped beneath your finger, and so that it does not come out.
And then put in the fire several small and large olive-shaped cauteries, in great number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of them, small or large depending on the location and the wound in which the artery is ruptured, and quickly, after you have removed your finger, apply the cautery on that vein until the blood is burned and stopped.
And if the blood spurts from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire, and continue to do this one after the other until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another injury comes upon the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to stop the bleeding in those, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or by extraction when it is not extracted, because when it is extracted its ends are better cauterized and the blood is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by tight constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop blood be placed on it; and they should be pressed with a cushion with decent constriction.
But he who administers proper constriction either with ligature and tight bandages or by applying burning substances and similar things, he won't do anything but cause delay.
But if this happens to someone and the doctor is not present nor medicine, then gently press with the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply great pressure until the bleeding stops from the upper part of the wound, and do not remove the finger from over the artery; and sprinkle very cold water on the artery until the blood congeals and thickens; and it is better to sew than needing cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping glasses and of phlebotomy (blood-letting) and of wounds and similar things.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every disease in which cauterization with fire and with burning medicine is appropriate, and the occasions and causes of it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the top of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one seeking this may be helped, then, concerning that which he has asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that what is in this chapter is, by means of terror, that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because great caution is required, for in the operations which are in this chapter many times there is evacuation which is with blood, with which is life, either through the opening of a vein or incision over an abscess or opening of a fistula or cleaning of a wound or extraction of an arrow or incision for a stone and things similar to them. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in the hands of many of them [i.e., practitioners], death.
And I warn you that what leads to hesitation does not fall upon you, for sometimes you will encounter people of many temperaments with many kinds of illnesses. And of these there are some who become angry about their illness, and death is insignificant to them; and this is because of the intensity of what they find from their illness, and because of the length of their illness, and with the illness there is fear, which signifies death.
And of these there is the one who gives you his possessions and says, you hoping for his recovery, and his disease is pernicious/fatal.
And therefore it is not fitting that you agree [to treat] the one who comes to you in this manner; and let your caution be greater than greed and your rapacity; and do not presume anything about this except with certain knowledge which verifies it before you, and to which follows praiseworthy success.
And in the treatment of all your patients, make use of prognosis and indications of him whose health is restored, for in that is your aid in acquiring fame and renown, praise and glory.
Oh, sons, may God inspire his understanding in you and not remove from you direction and protection, for this is in his hand.
And I have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by sections as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the treatment of the head down to the feet, so that what you ask about and what you need may be made easier for you.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water which is contained in the heads of children. (This likely refers to hydrocephalus.)
Know that this illness often comes to children after childbirth, and when the nurse squeezes the child's head carelessly/without gentleness. (This probably refers to birth trauma).
And sometimes it happens due to a hidden cause which is not known.
But I have not seen this illness except in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it seems to me a wasted effort in those cases. (Meaning the author found the condition untreatable)
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always so enlarged that he could not sit up by himself because of the size of his head, and further, the fluid increased until he died.
And this fluid is either collected between the skin and the bone, or it is collected below the bone, above/on the nape (back of the neck).
For the operation in this case, if the fluid is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you must cut in the middle of the head with an incision latitudinally, and let the length of the incision be almost two fingers so that the fluid may drain, and this is the shape of the spatum (bandage/compress):
And then if the fluid is more abundant and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions made in this manner:
And if the fluid is below the bone, a sign of it is that you will see the sutures of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inside parts, and this is not hidden from you. You must cut in the middle of the head with three incisions in this manner:
and after the incision, extract all the fluid; and then bind the incisions with cloths and soft lint; and then soak it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and feed the patient with dry food with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, which considers where the size of the abscess and the collection of fluid appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore pay attention with the incision where the abscess and the fullness of the fluid appear, and do this as best you can.
And take care not to cut the artery and consequently cause bleeding and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of fluid.
Second Chapter (This is a repetition of the previous line, likely a stylistic choice).
When someone develops cataracts or rheum (discharge) in the eyes or feet, and it is chronic, and medical treatments are not effective, then the last resort is the incision of those two arteries.
Therefore, when you want to incise them, have the patient shave their head, then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then tighten the patient's neck with a cloth; then consider where the vein pulsates; the locations of each are on the areas near the depressed parts close to the ears, and they are so hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with ink, and then incise each one with a scalpel, sharpened on each side, with an incision reaching to the bone, and let this be across the width of the head (or perhaps "along the side of the head," depending on the precise meaning of "latitut").
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and cut it at the incisions on the upper parts.
And let the length of the incision be about the width of two fingers joined together, because when a vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery doesn't appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers, and then mark with ink, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let is six ounces, and very soon you let out more or less, and all that depends on the apparent strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any part of the pericranium remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess doesn't develop afterward.
And then pack the wound with soft feathers and linen bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from a chronic migraine or acute catarrh, due to harmful moisture and [poor] quality in the tissues of the temples, or ancient vehement dizziness similar to these, and is treated with medical cures and it doesn't help, then we have proven in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or cauterization of them as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and pay attention to the artery visible in the temple for it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden only in a few men due to the vehemence of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or steam the place with hot water until the artery appears manifestly to you. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you peel the skin with it [the spatula] gently until you reach the artery; and then make an incision there and extract it [the artery] at the upper parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics/membranes which are beneath it, completely.
But if the artery is thin/small, then wrap it with the tip of the hook/lancet, and then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its extremities are elongated and constricted, and no flow of blood will occur, for if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then drain from six ounces of blood up to three.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with long cords, and not fear putrefaction before the healing of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then cut the excess which is between the two ligatures/ties; and do this at that time or shortly thereafter.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery down to the bone with a knife-like cautery until its extremities are incised, and it will serve in place of that same procedure.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a warm complexion; for cautery is among those things that promote the resolution of humors, and therefore it is better in aiding recovery.
And after the extraction of the arteries, you must fill the place with old cotton, and powders suitably made should be placed over it, and then it should be treated with drying medications that promote flesh growth and with others until it is healed.
And if while you are operating a second time bleeding occurs from the artery, then you should hurry to stop that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with cotton, and press your hand on it until the bleeding is stopped/reduced. And if none of these things are available to you, then place your finger over the area until the blood clots, and wash the area with very cold water until the sharpness/pain subsides, and it is constricted as necessary.
And the easiest thing in extracting an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and apply each one, heated by fire, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between its ends is lengthened, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form/shape of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be similar/equal. But however, if it is less sharp than two knives, it is better, because if it is as sharp as two knives it will stick to any wound, and it will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, as there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut easily.
And this work/method is better in every operation and easier.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the size/width of a finger's thickness.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of acute and acidic tears of the eyes. (Alternatively:  Of the body in regards to the treatment of sharp and acidic tears from the eyes).
When there are acidic tears, and the source of these is the veins which are on the front of the head, and you find no other cause in this for the medical treatment, and you see the face of the patient redden and feel on their forehead a crawling sensation as if it were the movement of ants, and their eyes are weakened, moist so that their lashes are stuck together, and the eyelids are irritated by the sharpness of the tears, the ancient doctors have often used this operation for their treatment, and I will describe this treatment briefly.
And you instruct the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then make three incisions on the forehead equally spaced along the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers: the first incision should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the tear duct, and the third on the other side.
And beware of cutting the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the joint of the two jaws (mandibles). And let the length of each incision be the measure of three fingers placed together.
And have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which you dry the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two pointed ends, of which this is the form of the incision: which is from the area of the temples down to the second incision. Scrape with that (instrument) all the tissue which is between the two incisions, down to the pericranium (membrane) which is over the bone, and then do the same from the middle incision down to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert another time into the first incision another instrument which is called a knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp side of it towards the flesh adjacent to the tissue, and the smooth side of it inwards toward the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure/crack, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But ensure that the incision is continuous with that separation of the tissue, and then do the same in the middle fissure down to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient amount of blood has flowed, press out (clear) the area of the pieces of clotted blood.
And then place in each fissure some old cotton, and place over these compresses soaked in wine and oil and sour wine, and watch that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, undo the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm water or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
On the treatment of excessive tearing (or rheum) caused by cataracts and to the eyes descending from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences acute and persistent cataracts, and you see the patient's eyes thinned and small, and their sight is weakened, and their eyelids are ulcerated and their eyelashes are falling out, and there is a sharp, throbbing pain in the forehead accompanied by emaciation, then know by these symptoms that the matter and cataracts come from many veins originating from the inner parts.
And the best treatment for this is the following: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in it in the middle of the forehead, a little wide (and start with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be down to the bone; and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles which are moved by chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or with a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two edges of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then tighten over it with small cushions soaked in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you untie this and see that the swelling has diminished, then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow in it, and then treat it with a drying treatment which makes the flesh grow, such as if you take two parts wheat flour and four parts rosin and mix them, and make a plaster from them and apply it to these wounds for the generation of flesh.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment of things that fall into the ears.
Everything that falls into the ears is one of four kinds, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant such as grain or a bean, or it is a liquid substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And so when a stone or another similar thing falls into the ear, of those things that do not swell or grow in the ear, then place the ear in the sun, and if you see the stone, then pour into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then try to extract it, and make the person sneeze with pepper, and block the nostrils just before the sneeze comes. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and stretch the ear upwards, for many times it is thrown out by these means.
And if it is not thrown out, then extract it with the instrument called a subtle ear probe, of which this is the form:
And if it is removed with the probe, good; and if not, then extract it with a subtle hook of small bend/double curve.
And so if it is not removed by this, then make a tube of brass and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open but the tube. And then extract it with your breath by strong suction, for many times it comes out with this that we have recounted.
But if it is not removed in this manner, then take a small quantity of burnt alum or prepared glass, with which birds are caught, and place that on the end of a stick/rod, and then wrap cotton decently placed over it. And then insert that into the opening of the ear gently, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And thus if it is not removed with all the things that we have recounted, then you must consider cutting it before a hot abscess or spasm develops.
And the way to cut it is that you at first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to their strength, and then have them sit between your hands and turn their ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear behind their cheek in the most prominent location. And the incision should be moon-shaped until you reach the stone, and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then sew the incision at that time quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the thing fallen into the ear is one of the growths that increase and swells, then administer treatment and extract it; but if it doesn't respond to such treatment, then you take thin, dyed linen according to this method: and administer with it the removal of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And don't do this when you think that it is already moistened by the moisture/vapor of the ear, but put it in many pieces, and then remove them with hooks or with a fine gesture/movement, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is facilitated.
But for water that has entered the ear, it is necessary that the patient sneeze with seasoning at the beginning, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie on that ear in which the water is, and if by this it is expelled, good; and if not, then take many dyed stones the length of the finger, and heat them in the fire a little, and the patient inserts one of these into the hole of his ear and hops on the one foot of that side and strikes with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Then don't stop this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Then don't stop this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And the water will be expelled thus:  take paper or cloth and put one end of it in the ear and light the other end with fire until most of it is burned.
And then put in another piece of cloth and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is expelled, and draw it out with a tube just as was said above about the stone for extraction.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small-bodied, such as a flea or similar, treat it as we have said in the section; but if it is large-bodied, which is apparent to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with movements and hooks, and its case is easier than all the other cases that happen in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the section and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you examine the ear in the sunlight.
And then if some sort of worm appears there, extract it with movements or with fine hooks, but if nothing appears, then take a tube, narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear as far as the patient can bear; and then suck strongly with it, and do this many times until all the worms are driven out.
But if it does not obey you thus, then you block and close the circumference of the tube with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already tested in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument of which this is the form: And make it of silver or brass, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and with a flat top above.
And thus it will be as a pusher for what is in the cavity of the cannula to constantly [move it to] where it is needed. And if you wish, take the ray (rod/probe) and wrap cotton decently on its end, and then pour oil into the cavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the tube — and that is in the ear — and then insert the ray with cotton on top and press it with your hand with moderate compression until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that the substance which you instill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of blockage of the ears.
Sometimes some children are born from their mothers' wombs without ear openings, and this also happens in some men, a blockage in the ear canal due to a growth or excess flesh there; and this blockage is sometimes located deep in the ear canal, which impedes hearing, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs deep within, which affects hearing, is among the most difficult conditions to heal.
And in the case where hearing is affected, you should place the patient's ear in the sunlight and examine it. And then, if you see an apparent blockage, open it with a thin spatula, of which this is the shape:
Its end should have a small width, one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be smooth on both sides so that it does not injure the ear.
And so, if the blockage is due to flesh growing in the ear or in its canal, then take it with thin hooks and cut it with great care until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is in the inner part of the ear, then take a thin, smooth root and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And so if you have found a blockage with this that the patient feels as a difficulty in hearing, gently force it out; and be careful not to damage the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a piece of linen, according to the size of the opening, which you have covered in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are certain of the healing of the wound; or take some alum, dissolve it in rosewater, pulverize it, sprinkle it over ground seeds, and use this; and if you see that the ear has become abscessed, then you must remove the alum and replace it with other alum that you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the heat of the abscess has subsided; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
On the treatment of a sty which occurs on the eyelids.
Styes which occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore, you must hold them with forceps or a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots, and then place ground sedge on the areas.
But if pain with a flow of blood occurs, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and they often return when some part of their roots remains; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that occurs in the eyelids.
Often a condition occurs in the eyelids which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and therefore it is called swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick humor in the upper and lower eyelid.
Therefore, the treatment for them is that you consider then if the swelling originates in the eyelid itself and is movable in every direction; and its cause is mild.
Then make a longitudinal incision on it, and then carefully peel it off in every direction with ease until it is detached, and then lift it with a hook and cut it off.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid below the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and raise the growth with a hook, so that you don’t need an incision, and press it on all sides, and if the eyelid is stretched by the incision, that doesn't matter at all.
And after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing substance until it is healthy.
Chapter 10.
About the treatment of fleshy growths (axcirnah - likely a type of growth or cyst) on the eyelid above the eye.
Axcirnath is a growth which develops in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and occurs mostly in children, affecting their eyes and often causing cataracts in them; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of this, for tears flow from their eyes.
And so you see them lying on their faces all the time, and they sleep on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are moist, covered with swelling, and a noticeable prominence of the axcirnath is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the axcirnath within.
And the method of operation is this: the patient rests their head on your lap, then place a cloth on them and create a frame/border, make a circle according to the size which contains the axcirnath completely, then place this upon it and press with your fingers all around so that the axcirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then make an incision in the middle of that moist area with a lancet, making a cut along its width, and the cut should not be larger than the incision made in phlebotomy (blood-letting).
But in the beginning, you must split the skin until you reach the axcirnath, because in later stages the axcirnath develops through the incision like a white fatty substance.
Then extract it with cloths that you have wrapped around your fingers externally and roll it between your right and left hand until it is removed.
And be careful not to go too deep in the incision so that with the instrument you do not reach the eye and damage it.
But if you don't see the axcirnath at the beginning of the incision, then you should carefully go a little deeper in the incision until the axcirnath appears; then extract it as we have said; then submerge cloths in water and wine vinegar and place them on the area and bind them with bandages.
And there are some who grind salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it absorbs what remains of the moisture/humidity.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess develops in the area, then treat it with sedative plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the methods of examining/palpating the eye.
When hairs grow on the eyelids, close under the natural hairs, and become hardened, they irritate the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, softening of the eyelids, and inflammation, which can lead to the destruction of the eye, which is called a sty (tactinir) of the eye.  It is treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or with a sharp medicine as described previously in the chapter on cautery; or the sty (atactini) is removed by incision and suture; or with tubes, as I will explain.
And therefore, you must place the patient's head on your lap.  Then turn the eyelid with your left hand, and if it is turned, good; and if not, insert a needle into it, which has thread attached to the lower part of the eyelid, and push the needle with the thread upwards, and this should be near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow — close to the hairs — and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the thread.
Then make an incision on the inner part of the eyelid, below the ingrown hairs, with a lancet, from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. Then remove the thread and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bandage over the eyelid with a dressing, and it should be shaped like a myrtle leaf.
But the shape should be according to the size of the swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people; for in some, a large amount of the eyelid needs to be incised according to how much the eyelid is swollen.
Then make incisions with the lancet along the two lines that you have marked, and start from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. And make your incision near the natural eyelashes about the thickness of a hair.
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the swelling/growth (codena), and then excise it completely, and then stitch the two edges of the incision with silk thread or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and cause what is superfluous from the threads to stick to the eyebrow with some sticky substance if you want to do this, and if not, don't bother.
Then leave the stitching of the threads for three or four days, and then take care of it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitching, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, for the eyelid is lifted/raised by the sealing of the wound and its joining/healing; but stitching is better. And thus said the ancients regarding styes/hordeolum (taxanir).
But however, take care with the patient, as it is a delicate operation, and there is risk involved.
And there is another method for styes (taxmir), which is to make a mark on the eyelid shaped like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three hooks separated and arranged in this form:
But if it's not possible to hold the hooks, and this is not leveled, then take a needle threaded with a suture and place it in the middle of the figure, then place another suture near the larger lacrimal duct, and the second suture near the smaller lacrimal duct, and adjust the sutures between your fingers evenly, and then with these lift your hand evenly, lifting the entire fold which is marked as we have described.
And then join the two lips of the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess develops near your incision or laceration, soothe that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted, then you should use on it what we have said about incisions; but stitching in each case is more skillful/refined.
Splinting with reeds is done in the following manner: You should turn the eyelid and cut the figure that is inside as I have described; and then skillfully make two reeds or two thin splints whose length corresponds to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the probe/spatula. And you have already made incisions at the edges of each part, where you are holding the threads.
Then carefully add each [splint] subtly over the excess of the eyelid's skin, and bind the reeds on each side with firm pressure, and leave it like that for a few days, because the flesh constricted by the pressure will die and be denied [nourishment] and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it has solidified/healed, the eyelid is lifted and the eyelashes do not prick/irritate (the eye).
And this is the form of two reeds which you will make according to this shape: and it should have this quantity of length and width, neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
On the lifting/removal of irritating eyelashes in the eye with a needle when the eyelash is hard/ingrown.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough and irritating eyelashes in the eyes, then the procedure is that you take a fine needle and place in it a fine thread of silk and linen, and then you adjust its ends and tie each in a very subtle way/with a very small knot. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of one palm (handspan).
And then you arrange another fine thread beneath it, short, and knot the ends of that if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head on your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is well-lit, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending eyelash which needs removal, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull it out above the natural eyelash.
And then pull the hair out and insert it into a depilatory (or plaster/salve), and let the hairs be one or two or three and no more.
And then pull your hand with it [the hair and depilatory/plaster] until the hair is ejected with the depilatory from the eyelid along with the natural eyelash.
But if the depilatory is pulled and the hair is not ejected, you will pull the depilatory downwards with the bile which you have mixed with it, until the depilatory is ejected from the upper hole.
And watch it [the process] with the sight of the eye, for then you will remove the depilatory and the bile, and close each eye and cover it up, and leave it covered for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is too short, then you should attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it [the new hair] is lifted with it [the attached natural hair], or you can leave it until it grows somewhat longer, and then you lift it.
Chapter 13
On the treatment of ectropion, which occurs in the upper eyelid.
When ectropion occurs in the eye, and there is a contraction of the eyelid when it is pulled upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a plague sore, a cut or cauterization, and it is called "hare eye," and this ectropion is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of plague sores or cuts or cauterizations, or similar poorly treated conditions.
But the way to operate on it is that you cut that hardened tissue and separate its edges/lips, and place between them a linen cloth wick, and bind them until they are healed.
And you should not use drying, constricting treatments on it; because if you do this, the ectropion will return worse than it ever was.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and foment with water in which marshmallow and linseed have been cooked, or diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oils, and soak the wick in this, and treat with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you completely prevent it from hardening/scarring again in the way it did at the beginning.
And this operation that we have described is only a partial correction of ectropion which doesn't restore the area to the shape it had before.
Chapter 14.
On the treatment of ectropion which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This ectropion, which occurs in the lower eyelid, is what is truly called ectropion; and there is one type that is natural, another that is accidental.
But accidental ectropion is caused by a wound or a tear or by cauterization or other similar things.
And the way to operate is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it pass from the left lacrimal point to the right lacrimal point, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
And then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and make an incision with a wide spatula/instrument. And then if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good; and if not, take the thread and place it in the split area, and with it turn the eyelid, and make two incisions on the side of the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two incisions be at the two angles of the larger incision which you have made, so that they meet, and form an angle between them, so that when they come together, their shape is made.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
And then remove/cut away that flesh for the width of the side of it sticking out below, that is, the eyelid.
And then join the separated parts and sew them with woolen thread; and then treat them with what we have said about softening medicines and ointments until it is healed.
And if an ulcer develops because of an incision or suture or cauterization, then you need to make a clean incision on the skin of the eyelid as described previously. And then separate [the two edges of the wound] with the ointment between the two lips [of the wound] as we have said.
In summary of the discussion on the treatment of ulcers, whether above or below, the operation should proceed according to the preparation for the type of ulcer, which occurs in the form of diverse swellings/lumps.
And the skilled practitioner always diligently prepares in every way possible to restore the [affected area] to its natural form, or close to it, and [treats] that patient as is appropriate with the operation and instruments in the best arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men, such that the upper eyelid is adhered to the white of the eye, and is hindered from easy movement and its natural functions, then you should insert the end of the probe under the eyelid, and lift it upwards, or extend it with a hook.
And then you incise the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not have sharp incisions, having a slightly blunt [edge] so that the patient is not distressed during the operation, and position the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And make your incision in such a way that it excises that adhesion easily until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and all the adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye, with which you wash it; or instill in it egg yolk dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid and the eye with some lint, and place wool infused with egg white over the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative agents (medications that promote healing) until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On incision of the nail and of fleshy excrescence of the tear ducts (lacrimal caruncle).
Know that a nail is of two kinds, for either it is sinewy/fibrous, and is similar to a hard, thin film/membrane; or it is non-sinewy/non-fibrous, resembling a frozen white humidity, which when an iron touches it, or it is forced to be grasped with a hook, is cut off and the hook is not stuck in it.
And both types begin only at the larger lacrimal duct/caruncle from where it goes little by little until it covers the sight and obstructs the light and the movement of the eye.
But the way of operating in its incision is that the patient places their head on your knee, and then opens their eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with a hook with a shallow bend/curve and extend it upwards. And then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair or ox hair or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or bend the end of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it upwards, and scrape with the hair the sides of the nail which follows the pupil. And thus you follow it with the thread/hair to its back/rear.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the lacrimal caruncle with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the flesh of the natural lacrimal caruncle so that a continuous flow of tears does not occur due to its incision.
And sometimes the nail, extended with the hook alone or with thread, as we have said, and then be scraped with a lion's spatula of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and cause a rupture in it, and that the pupil be perforated.
Then, into the incision/wound put a little ground salt or wound-powder made from pig fat, and leave that in until the next day; and then treat it with what is needed until it is healed.
And if the nail bed is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is the hook inserted in it, then often this type of nail ailment is treated with medicines, and is healed just as I have recounted in the chapter/section.
And if you want to incise it, then you open the patient's wound and take a thin, sharp steel lion's spatula of this form: with thinness/finely. And if you see that it (presumably the wound/affected area) is thinned and damaged and eaten away, then instill into that place oil of your choice made from pig fat, or with red oil or with ground salt, and cover the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the procedure on it until everything that is there is removed, unless a hot, opposing abscess is in the wound, for then you leave it, and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your procedure on it reasonably/methodically until it is healed.
If the protruding lacrimal caruncle causes great harm in the wound, it should be lifted with a hook; and incise a part of it, but do not complete the incision so that you do not cut into the flow of tears.
And then instill red oil made of pig fat into the tear duct until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
On the incision of an abscess and of that which is born of flesh attached/formed in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some people a red flesh grows, and it spreads until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses on the eyelids and very quickly compresses the eyelids on the outer parts and is similar to a pomegranate flower.
But the operation for it is that the patient places his head in your lap; and then open his eyes and gather all that flesh with hooks suitable for this, and hold it with forceps or with these; and then cut the flesh at the beginning until it is all finished with the scalpel, and beware of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And your incision, with which you cut those, may be with a spatula which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which a sliver is removed, according to the form of it near this chapter.
Then when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with powdered salt, or sprinkle on it red powder and similar corrosive medicines, and place on the eye externally cotton with egg white until it is safe from hot swelling/inflammation.
And if some cause of swelling remains and hot blood dominates, and you fear a hot swelling/inflammation, then leave the eye and treat that swelling with what reduces that swelling, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do with the excess flesh which you find in the eye of this kind.
But however you must avoid all treatment of a cold eye by natural means or by accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is able to tolerate that operation.
Chapter 18.
About/Concerning the collection of sand in the eye.
Sand (or Gravel) means red veins that are stretched/spread over the eye and obstruct its vision/function, and weaken the eye over a long period of time.
Therefore it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which the sand is, is strong and there is no other disease in it other than the sand, then you may remove that sand.
And you should instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and then lift/raise those veins with a hook or with two according to your judgment; and let that hook be of fine/thin double-pronged form/make according to this shape:
or it is two hooks/prongs gradually merging into one body in this manner/shape:
And then those veins should be grasped with fine forceps, gently, and wipe/clean the blood from time to time, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and turned/folded back.
And take care that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula/instrument; and do your operation at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation that you incise/cut only those veins.
And when you have finished, then you instill in the eye green or red onion juice so that with its sharpness it corrodes what is remaining of the sand/grit/foreign body.
But if this is not possible for you to remove it all at that time, then you apply a plaster with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for some days until his pain subsides and he is safe from a hot abscess/inflammation.
And then repeat on him according to the same method until he is healed.
And this is the form/shape of the scissors/forceps:
Chapter 19.
On the reduction of nasal polyps/swellings.  (Arizati likely refers to some kind of nasal growth or inflammation.)
When you treat nasal swellings with a cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine as previously described and it is not healed, then there is no other option but to cut open the abscess upon its maturation, and to extract all the moisture which is in it and the pus until the bone is exposed/uncovered.
And then when the bone is uncovered/exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form/shape:
And it is called a rasp, and it should be made of Indian steel, and its head should be round like a hazelnut, which should be made pointed/rough by filing or scraping with fine points (as shown in the following image/diagram).
And place it on the corrupted area of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away/removed.
And this should be done several times, and then treat/dress the area with drying and astringent substances/remedies.
Then if the area is consolidated/healed and good flesh has grown there, and the flow of pus has stopped and remains so for forty days, and it is not renewed and does not become abscessed and no complications/accidents occur in it, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other solution but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and in this manner: you must expose the bone underneath with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is exposed, then you take a drill/trephine in this way/shape:
Let the tip of the iron be triangular, and its shaft be thinly drawn out to the tip as you see here. (likely accompanied by a diagram)
And then place it on the bone, and let it be near the tear duct; and move your hand slightly away from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the drill/trephine until it penetrates all the way through the bone, and the patient will feel it because he finds wind coming out from under his hand.
Then afterwards treat the area with styptic and drying medicines as I have taught you.
Then when the flesh grows and the area is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outer parts returns to the nose, and this is less burdensome for the patient.
Chapter 20.
On reducing the swelling of the eye.
When the entire eye swells and there is no damage or diminishment of vision, then you should bleed the patient, and then purge him, and then apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suck with good suction; and then apply a liniment made of acacia, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla to the eye, and then tighten a plaster over the eye with a soft cloth.
And the way to make the cloths is to take soft linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four layers; and make compresses from these.
And then place them one on top of the other according to the amount that the eye needs. And then tighten over them with a hard cloth, binding it firmly; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
But if in the meantime the swelling has softened, then it should be bound less tightly.  (Implying a looser bandage).
And then remove the bandage during the day and night, as we have said.
And if you see that the eye is already reduced (back to normal), good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping glasses until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
On the incision of the prominence of the uveal tunic (of the eye).
When a rupture occurs in the uveal tunic (of the eye), and it protrudes and projects beyond the eyelids like a grape, and a person's appearance is marred by this, and you want to incise it, then the operation on it is as we have recounted: that you insert the needle into the roots of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then put in another needle which has a double thread on the side of the tear duct, and make that one penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the location of the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea around the needle, and tighten it a lot.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and leave it until the thread falls off with the (protruding part of the) uvea.
And then treat the eye with something that strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes it gathers from all sides with a circle of cloth inside the eye near the large protrusion, and then it is pierced with a fine spatula that reaches the front of the eye, and the albuminous fluid runs, and the eye shrinks and goes in immediately.
And then tighten (it/bandage it) until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of elbows (or possibly *alconati*, a specific elbow condition).  It could also mean "of those with elbow problems.
This disease, which is called alconati, is nothing but blood which has collected in the eye, resembling dripping water, but is not (water).
But the way to operate on it is as follows: have the patient sit on a prepared and upright saddle, then take their head in your two hands, on both sides, and move it until you see the blood reach the lower parts, then it should be dried and not descend.  And the light should be blocked, and the patient will see things as they saw before.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water (and not blood).
But if the descent of the blood is not prepared by what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands and take a thin spatula, and with this work on the thin membrane [panniculus, perhaps a membrane of the eye], pushing it a little bit down where the conjunctiva meets the cornea (in the corona), into the empty space, until the blood is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you should instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of watery discharge from the eye. /  About the treatment of tearing/watery eyes.
We have already discussed the types of watery discharge in the classification and which one requires perforation, explaining it clearly; therefore, [treat] that one according to the remedy. / We have already described the types of watery discharges in the classification and which one needs surgical opening, explaining it in detail. Now, regarding the healing/treatment [of that].
Then you should have the patient sit between your legs on a square and tie his healthy eye very tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his healthy eye, if the eye that has the discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand if the eye [with the discharge] is the right.
And then with your right hand take a probe, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the tip of the probe near the edge of the iris, about the thickness of a hair away, in the white of the eye towards the smaller tear duct, and then push the probe in firmly and rotate your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye until you feel that the probe has reached an empty space.
And the length of the probe's path should be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the edge of the colored part of the eye, which is the iris; for you will see through it the pupil and the vision of the eye because of the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the probe into the area where the fluid is, and then you draw it downwards repeatedly, and if the fluid descends immediately, then at once the patient will regain their sight and [will see] the probe in their eye.
And then rest a little; if the fluid has descended, then you repeat the procedure before removing the probe.
And when it is dry and does not descend, then you carefully remove the probe, turning your hand little by little, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in water and wash the inside of the eye with it; and then cover the outside of the eye with cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white, and bandage the eye well with this.
But we and those of our time place on it cumin crushed with egg white.
And if the probe does not yield to you upon entering the eye due to its hardness—for there are some men whose eyes are very hard—then you must take the instrument which is called alberid, of which this is the form:
And then pierce that conjunctiva with it, and do not remove your hand from the instrument, and do nothing but make a subtle path for the probe.
And then insert the probe as we have said.
And after completing your work, a suitably prepared sleeping area should be ready for the patient, in which they sleep on their back in a darkened room, and are forbidden from all movement.
And place things in their natural position so that their nature is soothed, and they don't move their head to the right or left at all.
And the bandaging should be according to his condition until the third day.
And then untie it in that same dark room and prohibit cold sights, and make them see some pleasant things, and then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And you should not do this during the time of treatments or immediately after the infusion/application (of medicine). It is fitting that you remove this because the water can easily increase due to forceful and excessive urination/defecation (This translation is uncertain, the meaning of "reguardament" is unclear.  It could relate to something being looked at, urination, or a bowel movement. Given the context, a bodily function seems likely).
But if a hot abscess/swelling occurs, then you should untie the bandage before the seventh day, and treat it with something that reduces the abscess/swelling.
Then you should cover his sight and place a cloth over his face through which his breathing can be directed, leaving it there for a few days, while he remains in the dark room.
And afterwards, he should be gradually taken out of that room and spoken to about his affairs/condition.
And know that in surgery the student is not excused from sometimes watching, and so you should be bold in the operation.
And it has come to our knowledge from some of those who are in Artach, and some say that fire is made in a perforated clay vessel (earthenware pot?) in Ayrac with which the water is sucked out.  (This likely refers to some sort of suction device or possibly a primitive still.)
But however, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is a new thing.
And these are the forms of the types of earthenware so that those who don't know them may see them: (This likely refers to illustrations of the pottery.)
And they should be made of bronze or iron specifically, and their ends should be subtly triangular, the tip sharp, just as this is the shape. (Likely referring to surgical instruments.)
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of nasal polyps (literally, "born flesh in the nose").
When various fleshy growths develop in the nose, some of which resemble the many-footed scorpion or the many-footed polyp (octopus); and this malady is called polypus because it resembles that creature. And some of it (the growth) is cancerous flesh, flabby, of a dark color; and some of it is lion-like flesh, not of a dark color.  (Note: "lion-like" likely refers to a specific appearance or texture.)
Therefore, for the kind that is of this lion-like flesh, not fraudulent nor cancerous, you must have the patient sit between your hands and place him in the sun, and open his nostrils, and pick at those fleshy growths with your fingernail. And then pull it outwards, and then cut what you have grasped of it with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; if any of it remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape it with one of the subtle instruments with ease until none of it remains. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot abscess occurs, then you apply what is necessary to it; or if it is of the malignant abscesses, then you burn and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
And after the incision, sour wine and water or just wine should be put into the nose. But if the nose is open and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that inside there is proud flesh growing on the bones above the nostrils, to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, then it is necessary that you insert ox gall, to which some thickness is added, and mix into it many ground nuts, and place between each application the said amount or less, and lay the patient down and insert one end of the ox gall into the nostrils with a ray or other means possible.
And after this has been done, let him inhale its scent until it reaches the nasal passages, and let it come out onto his throat, for children at school often do similar things, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it.
And then place the two ends of the ox gall [bladder], one end coming out over the nose and the other remaining in the nose, and then apply ground meat with the nuts that are in the ox gall, and do this until you know that the flesh is [mixed] with the nuts of the ox gall.
And then remove the ox gall, and immediately after the bloodletting place a plug in the nose, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
And then at the end of the treatment, place a lead tube in the nose for some days until it is healed.
But if you need a drying treatment, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument for cleansing the head with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make it of silver or brass, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small shell, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube like a reed, and the tube of the head-cleansing instrument should be flat and uncovered; to which there should be a handle at its back, with which you hold it when you heat oil or water in it, or whatever you want of juices and absorbable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of boils born at the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil arises on the tip of the nose, and it grows and swells over several days until it is very ugly to look at.
And therefore you must incise it at the beginning of its appearance and remove all of it. And then apply to the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that will stay in its place.
But if its incision is delayed until it has grown large, then consider if it is hardened, firm, dark-colored and with little feeling. Then do not touch it with an iron instrument, for it is a cancerous sore; for many times, great harm comes upon the one who incises this sore, just like what happened to the one who had it.
And if the sore is somewhat soft to the touch and not of a dark color, and you see that incision is possible throughout, then have the patient vomit (or purge) and incise it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On suturing the nose and lips and the ear when the continuity is broken by a wound or similar things.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you will rarely achieve success in that operation except in some men.
Therefore, when such a case happens to someone, you must consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood, and then you bring the two edges of the wound together with sutures, and treat it accordingly until it is healed.
And if the edges of the broken continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must excoriate each part of its apparent skin until blood comes out. And then bring the two edges together with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them dragon's blood and ground incense, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other consolidative plaster, and leave it bandaged for two or three days, and then untie it and change that plaster and leave it until the threads [sutures] are cut by themselves, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the method of suturing is that you either join the broken continuity with needles as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or join the broken continuity with thread as has been made known to you.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of those things that come into the two lips [edges of a wound] in the inner parts.
Many times it happens to many men in the inner parts of their labia [lips, edges of a wound] some hard abscesses resembling pig fat, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn out the lips [edges of the wound] and cut each one of those hardnesses completely, and after the excision you fill the area with crushed sage until the blood is stanched, and afterwards it should be washed with sour wine and the place treated with something that is styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
On the incision of the flesh attached to the gums.
Many times there grows on the gums attached flesh which the ancients called albulus, therefore it is necessary that you lift it with a hook or that you hold it with forceps and cut it down to its root, and let the pus or the blood run.
And afterwards place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it, for after the cautery it will not return.
Chapter 29.
About extracting teeth with iron (instruments).
When on the surface of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums, rough, thin scabs accumulate, and sometimes they become blackened and turn yellowish or green until the gum is affected by this corruption, and the teeth become loose.
And then you must have the patient sit between your knees and rest their head on your chest, and scrape the teeth on which these scabs and sand-like things appear until nothing of them remains, and do this with the blackness and the yellowness and the others until they are cleaned.
Then if what was there at the beginning of the procedure has been removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the procedure on them the next day and the second and the third until you reach the final stage of what you want.
And know that teeth require many scrapers of different shapes and many figures according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inside is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth according to other shapes, and these shapes of scrapers you should have prepared, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
It is appropriate that you treat the tooth for its pain with all care and be slow in all its extraction, because there is nothing that replaces it once it is extracted, for it is a noble substance so that it is excusable in its extraction.
But it is fitting, when the patient is forced to extract that tooth, that you be firmly certain which is the aching tooth, because many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks that it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the work of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which is the aching tooth, and you know it well, then it is fitting that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the tooth and the gum on all sides, and then pull it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at the beginning little by little until you extract it.
And then apply strong large tongs with good leverage or a strong grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well so that it doesn't move, and then pull the tooth straight out in such a way that you don't break it, nor cause the patient more pain.
And if it is not thrown out, then take one of those instruments, and put it under it on all sides with ease and force its removal in the same way as it is done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth and tighten it with the end of a thin root so that it is not broken at the time of your squeezing it with tongs.
And it is necessary that you work carefully with scarification around the entire gum and watch your strength so that you do not break it and a piece of it remains, and returns upon the patient with a worse affliction than his first pain.
And beware of doing what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity concerning their eradication, without administering what we have recounted; for many times they cause great illnesses to come upon men which are more difficult to cure, and the roots of them remain entirely, or some of them, or that you extract it with some bones of the jaw as we have witnessed many times.
And after its eradication, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if blood flow occurs because of this, as often happens, then grind some amount of vitriol and sprinkle it on the wound, and if the vitriol is not effective, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps with which the tooth is moved at the beginning should be of long extremity, with a short and thick handle, so that they are not bent when you must work with them.
And they should be like you see with a thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent, and have short extremities.
And they should be made of Indian steel, suitably made with straight extremities, and at the ends of these should be teeth, some of which fit into the others and tighten by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of these are in the shape of the beak of a bird which is named stork, that is crane, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
On the extraction of teeth and the removal of broken jaw bones.
When you must extract a tooth which is broken and some part remains, then you need to place cotton with butter on the area for a day or two until the place is softened; and then insert forceps or tongs whose extremities are similar to the beak of a bird called stork or crane, the shape of the forceps:
Their extremities should be made with teeth like a file or saw.

And let it have a short end, a little thick, and not tapered so that it is not broken.

The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and with a more subtle/finer extremity/point/tip.

of the triangular extremity, twisted or short, which has some thickness, and is not filed down/sharpened so that it is not broken/brittle.
And also know that the instruments for the teeth are many and similarly the other instruments.
