But if there is a small amount of decay in the bone, then scrape that decay and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that stand out above the others.
When the teeth are prominent, not according to their natural position, their shape is therefore ugly, and especially when it happens to women, and especially to venerable young ladies; therefore you must consider: if a tooth has grown next to another tooth, and closing or filing it is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you incise it with this instrument, of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a pig's snout, and let it be made of Indian steel with a very sharp point.
And make your incision with which you incise it over several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that it does not harm the other teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth suitable for filing, then file it with a file of Indian steel of which this is the shape:
And let it be entirely of Indian steel and the handle as well; and let it have very fine points like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth may be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then polish it at the end, and scrape it with sharp razors.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that break is removed and smoothed, and it is made even so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver foil.
When front teeth are struck and loosened by a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot clench them to chew what they eat, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, it is wise that they be tightened with gold foil and not silver, because silver turns green and then rots after a few days, but gold is never altered.
And let the foil be of such thinness and thickness according to the amount that can enter between the teeth; and the method of application is that you take the foil and take twice as much of it between two healthy teeth, and then you press with two twists of the foil between the two damaged ones, or if it's one or many, until the sheet reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the side from which you began, and tighten it with your hand gently and skillfully so that it is not damaged.
And let your tightening with which you tighten the foil be towards the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the foil with forceps or scissors, and tuck these in each place with a tool, and turn them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then leave it as tight as it is.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another foil in the same manner at any time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, as you see them bound:
And when one or two teeth are returned near their setting in their places, and they have regrown as we have recounted, and they become damaged again.
And only a good and wise artisan can do this.
And sometimes some sort of device is prepared from cow bones, and made from them in the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and is tightened as we have said, and it remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
On the incision of the ligament which comes in the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament, which is found under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental, and occurs due to a plague/wound/sore which has already been consolidated/healed.
And the operation for it is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being placed in your lap; and lift their tongue, and then incise that sinewy ligament along its width until the tongue is released from its restriction.
But if there is any hardness or knotting in it, and this has occurred due to the healing of a plague/wound/sore, then you cast hooks/grasping tools and cut that across its width until you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved/untied/released.
And take care that no fissure is made in the underlying flesh and that you cut an artery here and bleeding occurs.
And then the site of the incision should be washed with rosewater or with sour wine or with cold water; and then place lozenges/medicated tablets under the tongue; that is, a wick/plug which the patient holds under the tongue every night so that it is not consolidated/healed closed again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed/powdered vitriol on the area; but if the blood continues to flow, then cauterize the area with a lenticular/lens-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula [cyst] from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small lump occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it quickly grows until it fills the mouth.
And the procedure for it is that you open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is a dark or black color, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it for it is cancerous.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then put your hook into it, and incise it with a subtle spatula, and cut it on all sides; and if blood comes to you at the time of your operation, then you place crushed vitriol (copper sulfate) on it, until the blood is stopped.
And then return to your work until you have completely removed it; then wash it with sour wine and salt; and treat it with the other treatment appropriate to this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
On the extraction of an abscess from both ears and of that which grows in the throat and of other abscesses.
When swelling occurs in the throat, that is, glands that are clustered together, which protrude outwards and are called tonsils, which when you treat them as we have said in the (previous) section and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of a dark color, with little feeling, then do not touch it with an instrument (iron/knife).
And if it is of a red color and the root is large, then do not touch it with an instrument because of the blood flow; rather leave it until it is matured (ripened), and then either you will lance it, or it will rupture by itself.
And if it is of white color, round, and its root is thin, then that is the one that should be incised.
And the procedure for it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the swelling has subsided from its heat with complete rest or if it has diminished with some decrease; and then have the patient sit before the sun, and their head be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the assistant taking an appropriate instrument between their hands presses down the patient's tongue with it, of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be thin like a knife.
Then when you press the tongue with it and the swelling is shown to you, and you direct your gaze upon it, then take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Besides this, pull some of the membrane [or: one of the layers/coatings] and then incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Besides this, pull some of the membrane [or: one of the layers/coatings] and then incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to forceps, however its extremities are curved, so that the opening of each one is towards the other, very sharp, and which should be made of Indian steel, tempered.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one slit, incise the other in this same manner of incision, and after the incision the patient should gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and things similar to those with styptic properties until the flow of blood is restricted, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat besides those behind the two ears, and incise these as I have said in the incision of two slits, similarly.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess which had arisen in her throat, which abscess tended towards a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing with a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her from eating and drinking water, and she would have died if it had continued for a day or two more.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had pierced two branches until it came out through two holes/openings.
Then I took care of it as if it were a fistula and inserted a hook into one of them [the holes], and pulled it [the hook], and a large portion was drawn out, and then I incised it where I understood it connected to the other hole, and then I opened her mouth, and held down her tongue, and then I inserted the hook into that abscess.
And then I cut away a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Then the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took food, then I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and an abscess formed in its place, which I incised until it was gone.
Then I used a cautery and cauterized his throat, then there was scarring; and then I left and didn't know what God did with them/him after that.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of the uvula when it is abscessed.
When a catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called buba/buboe.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the section/chapter, and your treatment is not effective, and you similarly see that pus is indeed gathering there and it is subtle, then you must incise the one that is aggregated and round; and if it has that length, and is sanguine, of a dark or black color, and there is no feeling in it, then you must consider/be careful with its incision, because in that there is danger to the patient.
And when you see it in the manner that I have described, regarding its whiteness and length, you should have the patient sit and depress their tongue with an instrument, of which I will describe the form, and then place the hook in the uvula, and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are mentioned in the incision of the tonsils.
And you must not incise it beyond what is added above the natural boundary, because if you incise more of it, you will injure their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by its incision, you should use ingenuity in its cauterization without fear, and the method of cautery in this case is only with sharp/corrosive water, which is to say that you place the patient's head in your lap, and then depress their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take some of the water which we have described in the chapter on cauterization, make a burning coal with it, and apply it neither thickly nor thinly, and with it fill the cavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
Let its tip be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a radish spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and have the patient lie on their side so that saliva or mucus runs from their mouth because of the internal medicine, so that none of it descends onto their throat and harms it.
And then hold your hand with the medicine, and press it onto the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see it blackened and dried by the biting action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the tip of a radish, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and insert the radish with the cotton into a cannula until the cotton adheres to that cannula, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it, for it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you deem it necessary to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and then wipe around the cauterized uvula with cotton soaked in egg white, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine, and let it be washed with cold water, and be treated externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less fearsome.
Sometimes the uvula is treated with something more subtle than cautery and incision, for it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take calamine, hyssop, origan, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, borage and similar herbs, and combine all these things, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in strong wine, and boil; and let the pot be well sealed with lute, and let there be in the middle of the pot a hole, over which is placed a concave instrument of brass in this manner; let it be made of silver or brass.
And let the tip be inserted into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is very much obscured/darkened (likely meaning: until the swelling reduces).
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and beware not to do this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses, for it would aggravate the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but you should wait for the clear manifestation/development of the abscess before cauterizing.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then take a reed and place an eggshell at the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected from burns, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good and healthy/safe.
Chapter 38.
On the extraction of thorns and of that which adheres to the throat/esophagus and of other things.
Many times a fish bone or some other thing gets stuck in the throat/esophagus.
Then you must extract what is apparent from these, on which your sight falls, after you depress the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that what is in the throat can be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat/esophagus, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because very easily the thing attached to the throat/esophagus will be expelled by vomit; or have the patient swallow a piece of turnip or a lettuce stem or a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of dry sea sponge, and tie it tightly with thread, and then have the patient swallow it, because when it comes to the place in which the thorns are, and it is pulled out, then they are expelled. And further, take an eel, tie it with thread, and insert it inside and suddenly pull it out; or take a leek, insert it suddenly, and suddenly withdraw it.
But if they are not expelled by these things which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form/shape:
And let it be a little thicker than a ray (fish), and at its end let there be a concavity which you insert into their throat with ease.
And let him/her hold his/her head elevated upwards, and be careful that you touch his/her throat bone, lest he/she be harmed, and with that you push the bone or the thorn, or the physician places it with his hand.
But it is better that the patient insert it themselves, because of their knowledge of the location of the attached object, and push it down, and pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the prepared quantity until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat/gullet.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the section on healing with medicines and it does not come off, then observe the patient's throat/gullet in the sunlight, after you have compressed their tongue with the instrument that we have recommended to you.
Then, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, appropriate movements/gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat/gullet, and then place a heated iron in the cavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat/gullet) and the patient should abstain from water all day.
And then take a glass full of cold water, and have him open his mouth over it, and wash his mouth with it; and do not swallow any of it, and pour the water with your hand, hour after hour, because the leech falls off as soon as it senses the water.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with zedoary or with asafoetida using the instrument that we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, and it will fall out.
And there is another method of operating with fumigation for this: you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then the fumigation should be added on top; and have the patient put his mouth at the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall out.
And if it does not fall out, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and have the patient abstain from drinking, and have him eat salty things and garlic, and not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps as you see, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and the end of it is similar to a stork's beak, which is toothed, and roughened with a file, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discussion on opening abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many types, in many ways, as mentioned for each of them in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, one of which is by the type of abscess itself; and by the humors it contains.
And the second way is due to the locations in the body in which they occur, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttock; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy area is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own treatment by surgery.
And further, there are some abscesses which should not be perforated except near the maturation of the pus within them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be lanced when they are raw, and not matured to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess occurs near a joint, its being is prolonged until it can putrefy what is around it, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, which causes contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay lancing it until it is matured it will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, then lance it immature; and you should not lance it raw, of incomplete digestion, except the one that is near the anus, so that its core does not putrefy and be elongated to the parts inside the anus, and become a fistula, or that it result in a condition that is not healed. But you should know the time to lance the abscess, and that is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when the pain and fever cease, and near the decrease of redness and throbbing, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know the perforation should fall in the lowest place of the abscess and where it is most conducive to healing from the swelling.
And the lancing should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the area of the muscles, tendons, or arteries; and, lastly, in all similar places where there is no bending; because in those that are bent, use lancing appropriate to that limb.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, it is better to leave it unlanced until its digestion is fully confirmed, as we have said; for if you lance it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and its edges harden very easily, as does its depth.
But some abscesses are lanced across the width of the flesh or body out of necessity, or according to the needs of the limb.
And you should make a small incision for small abscesses, and a large and wide incision or multiple incisions for large abscesses according to the variation in the size of the abscesses.
And there are other abscesses in which the skin must be held concave and incised, when it is made like bread and made at the point that it already protrudes, as happens with many furuncles and buboes.
And some of these are incised with a three-angled incision; and of these there are some which are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, such as abscesses in the corners (of the eye/mouth?).
And of them there are some in which a round or crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones, is used.
But one which is not like these superficial erysipelas abscesses, you should incise with only a superficial, or flat, incision.
And it is necessary when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, that you lance it and do not worry about extracting all the pus at that time; having extracted some of it, then cover the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger amount, and then do this many times gradually until it is all drained.
And especially if the patient is of a cold nature, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old decrepit person, because the vital spirit is often dissolved with the outflow of the pus, and the patient dies very easily, and you are to blame.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great care, and then the incision in which you lance these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and then consider if the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then you apply lint or old cotton; and if the abscess is large, and the openings of the incision are many, then you must place a wick in each hole until they join together.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin is cut off or you have hollowed it out in the surrounding area, then you must fill it with old cotton or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted bits of cloth or roots, without moisture; and bind it until the third day; and then proceed and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then you apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and place it over the site of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer the regimen and treatment and powders which I have described in many places of this our book and section.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should soak powders in warm wine and oil, and place them over the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must place powders soaked with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all of this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should loosen the abscess and cleanse it and apply in its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses generally, but following the method of distinction I have already spoken about each abscess in what manner is the way of its treatment by individual chapters.
Chapter 41.
On the incision of abscesses which occur in the back of the head.
On the back of the head, small abscesses, specifically nodes, which contain tunics that are made for them; and their types are many.
And among them are pimples, and of those there are some that contain a moisture resembling file dust.  (This likely refers to a gritty or viscous pus). And among them are some which contain a moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and among them are some that are stone-like, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of these there is no danger, as long as the removal of these does not go against an artery.  (i.e., as long as you don't cut an artery).
And the operation in incising these is that you probe them with an instrument, the shape of which is seen here, which is called a probe/lancet, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is moisture, then you cut it with an incision along its length by a superficial cut according to this shape/manner:
And begin the incision from the line B to the line G. Then when the moisture is evacuated, you excoriate/scrape that skin which contained the moisture, and leave nothing of it at all, for it returns too often, if anything of it remains.
And then submerge/soak cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with this and leave it until the next day, for with this you will corrode all that remains of that pellicle/membrane/thin skin.
And then repeat with the cotton and Egyptian ointment a second and third time if you need to, until you are confident that nothing of that pellicle/membrane remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess/boil contains fatty lumps/nodes, then you incise/cut it in this manner/shape: with an incision having the shape of a cross, and place hooks between the two lips/edges of the incision and peel/strip it from all sides, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic/membrane which contains it.
But if it offers resistance to you, then do what I told you first/initially.
And similarly, do the same for the hardened/calcified abscess regarding incision and treatment as we have told you; and incision on a hardened/calcified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture/fluid.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in some men, in which there is no moisture at all; which is why I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone were to be struck with it, it would cause injury.
But in other abscesses occurring in the head, other than this one, this chapter does not apply, such as the abscesses that come on the heads of children behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And place the perforation of these on their lowest part so that the body may be relieved of the corrupted blood/pus at the lowest points.
And then treat them with that which is appropriate for their healing/treatment.
Chapter 42.
On the incision of scrofula which occurs in the neck.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin/elbows; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, there are many that are generated among them, and every scrofulous tumor of these is within its own tunic/membrane, just like in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stone-like, and some of them contain humors/fluids, and some of them are malignant which do not respond to treatment/cure.
Therefore, in the one that you find of good disposition by touch, whose apparent color is close to the color of skin, and is movable in every part, and is not attached to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the trachea/windpipe, and is not deep, it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial cut from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line G between the line B, and flay/peel it on all sides, and extend the lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks if you need them, just as we have said about the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more prudent that the vein or nerve not be incised/cut.
And let the scalpel/blade not be too sharp, lest you injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the patient, and cut what is not necessary to cut; for if you incise a vein or artery and are hindered from your work, then place on the wound ground vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and constrict the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to suppurate/drain pus. When the blood is cut off/stopped.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your finger/probe whether there remains any matter from the other small scrofula/swollen glands, then incise them and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, it is necessary then that you do not incise that scrofula at its root. First, it is necessary that you tie it with doubled thread tightly and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm/injury.
And then fill the wound with old cotton which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it/dress it.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound and sew them immediately as long as there is no superfluity/excess remaining.
But if you see that some superfluity/excess remains due to the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you try to incise the root of it, and extract it and administer sutures as has been said/described.
And those scrofulas that contain humors, perforate them with a superficial perforation where it appears to you to be the place of its maturation, and place the perforation in the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
And then administer perforation, cauterize with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption/infection. And then when you see the wound is already cleansed, then you treat it with flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
On the incision of the hair of the abscess occurring/incident within the throat/gullet.  (The "hair" likely refers to a thin surgical instrument or a type of thread/suture.)
The ancients discussed this incision in the epiglottis/uvula, but no one in our land has seen it done.
And this is the writing/transcription of their discourse/words.
But in men having quinsy/tonsillitis, the removal/extraction of the epiglottis/uvula must be avoided, as no benefit is derived from it because all the veins, including the major ones of the patient, are located there.
But in those who have hot abscesses in the mouth or throat or both ears, when the illness is not in the trachea/windpipe, the incision of the epiglottis/uvula should be used to avoid death, which would be caused by suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should incise the epiglottis/uvula below three or four of its rings/circular ridges, with a small incision, widthwise, between two rings, so that the incision is superficial, not in the cartilage, and this place is suitable for incision, because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it.
And if the physician is timid/fearful, then he should stretch the neck/throat skin with a hook, and then cut the skin until he reaches the canal/trachea, but avoid the blood vessels if he sees any sign of them.
And then incise superficially as we have described; and the incision of the trachea/windpipe is indicated by the breath which comes out of it along with what is broken off from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then when the swelling/crisis has subsided, at which time perforation is feared, rejoin the two edges of the skin of the wound, and sew only that without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote flesh growth until it is healed.
And Albucasis said that the summary of the discussion we have recounted is simply that you should see that some of these abscesses/swellings have already obstructed the patient's throat, and the patient is near death, and their breathing is cut off, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient can breathe through the site of the wound, somewhat, and be saved from death.
And for this reason they recommend leaving the wound open until the harmful matter of the disease has been consumed/expelled; and its harmfulness lasts for three days; and then they recommend sewing up the wound and treating it until it is healed.
And what I myself have seen is that a maidservant took a knife and put it to her throat and cut with it a part of the trachea/windpipe, and I was called to treat her, and I found her as silent as the dead, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood that had come out of the wound was minimal/slow.
Then I was assured that she had not cut a vein or artery, and the air was coming out of the wound, and I treated her until she was healed.
And nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice, and after a few days she returned to a better state / recovered.
And now I say that in the cutting/incision of the epiglottis there is no danger/fear.
Chapter 44.
On incision/cutting upon a swelling/abscess which occurs on the throat externally, and is called a hernia of the throat/goiter.
This swelling/abscess called a hernia of the throat/goiter is a large swelling similar to the color of the body, and is common in women.
And it is of two kinds, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural kind is not appropriate/necessary to treat/cure.
And the accidental kind is of two types, of which one is similar to fatty lumps/nodes, and the other type is similar to a swelling which is caused by a knotting/dilation of an artery, and in that one there is danger/cause for concern. And it is not appropriate to treat/operate on it with iron/a knife except in the one that is small. And you probe it and examine it with your finger, and if you find it similar to fatty nodes, and it is not attached to any of the veins, then you incise it just like the nodes, and remove it with what it contains from the skin and sac/cyst; and if not, then its entire nature is examined, and then treat the area with the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the incision/cutting of the types of lumps/nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division/classification.
And it is necessary that I recount the difference between a node and a growth/protuberance, as it may cause doubt/confusion.
Therefore, I say that with a growth/protuberance there is heat and fever and vehement pains until the boiling/inflammation of the superfluity/excess is calmed; and when putrefaction is complete, then the fever, the pain, and the heat cease.
But with a node there is no heat nor fever nor pain, and take as an example the lipoma/fatty cyst which is in its own sac/cyst.
And it is the same color as the body, and its beginning is like a pea and it forms like a blister, be it larger or smaller, and they [nodes] are of two types, for either they are fatty and contain moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, as I said in the classification.
Therefore, it is necessary, when you come to the treatment of a node, that you examine/explore it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called a probe/sound, according as its form will appear in the chapter which is next/follows this one.
And the manner of examining abscesses and all nodes is this: that you take this instrument and insert it in the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will turn your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin/membrane.
And then spread out your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then remove the probe and consider what has come out in its track/groove.
And if the moisture is ejected in a stream/flowing, whatever color it may be, then you incise it with a superficial incision as I have said about other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes from the track of the probe, then know that it is fat/a fatty tumor.
And cut on it with an incision in the shape of a cross like this: as I have taught you, and lift it with hooks, and peel the skin on all sides with ease, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and draw out that infected blood with the knot.
But if it is ruptured during the operation, it is not possible to extract that infected blood, and this happens many times, and then you remove it in pieces until nothing of it remains.
Because if any of it remains, little or much, the node will return according to the most/the larger part (that remains).
And if you see that some small part of it remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound, and place over it something to absorb the discharge/pus, and treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large node/growth, then you sew its two edges/lips and treat it with something that promotes flesh growth.
But if there is a pulsating vein in the opposite direction, or a non-pulsating one, then you must burn and fill the place with ground vitriol and bind it tightly for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise what remains of the node.

Chapter 47.
Of the treatment of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the nipples of some men are swollen by the fulfillment of sleep when they begin to be hairy, and then their nipples are swollen, and become similar to the breasts of women, and remain apostematous (infected with pus), ugly.
And to him who has this, it is necessary to incise them with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner, from the line B to the line G, and then excise all the thickness/growth, and then fill the wound with incarnative (flesh-forming) medicine, and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the nipple hangs down and is softened by its size as happens in women, then it should be split on both sides above, with two incisions similar to a crescent moon shape, each of which is contained within the other after the end of each one, so that the larger line contains the smaller one in this way, from line B and line G.  And then excise the flesh which is between the two edges of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and the powders that are necessary in the treatment until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what should be incised because of the restlessness of the patient, and because of blood flow, then it is necessary that you fill the wound with cotton submerged in corrosive ointment and leave it there until it corrodes what remains of the fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.

Chapter 49.
On the cutting/lancing of abscesses that occur because of an artery or vein.
When an artery is ruptured, and the skin/tissue over it has healed/closed, then many times an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to the artery or vein are these: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, aggregated in the depth of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a sort of whistling/creaking/crackling sound in it.
And that which is due to a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And cutting/lancing this abscess is a mistake, especially in those that are under the armpits, and in the elbows, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron (surgical instruments) must be avoided, and that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should be avoided.
But on that which is there because of swelling of the opening of the artery, you will make a long incision in the skin/tissue; and then open the incision with hooks and [find] the artery, and free it from the membranes/tunics until it is exposed/uncovered.
And then put a needle on it and make it penetrate through to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places,—which procedure I have shown you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a spatula/blade until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the abscess is dissolved/drained.
And then administer a treatment in which pepper is generated/used until the ligatures fall off; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to a cut vein, then you must hold with your hand as much as possible of the abscess along with the skin, and then insert the needle under the area which you have held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it until you pull it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature as I have told you in the tying of a grape in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you are afraid that the thread will come out, then insert the thread with the needle under the whole abscess near the incision of the first needle, and tie your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in its middle area so that when what is in it has come out, you then cut the excess skin, and leave that which is tied.
And then put on it a powder which you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is made with socks/bandages and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter L (50) of the abscess which occurs because of a twisted/sprained nerve.
Just as an abscess occurs in an artery and in a vein, similarly an abscess occurs in the nerves when there is an impact on them or occurs through excessive work, and similar things.
And it is done most often in the areas of the joints or the heel, or in any place in which the joints are strained/moved.
And it is a serious abscess, and it is similar to the color of the other flesh, and it is without notable features/manifestations most of the time, and when it is pressed forcefully, the patient feels a sort of numbness in it.
And the abscess is not joined/fixed to the surrounding flesh, and because of this it is beneath the skin, and it can be moved in every direction and it does not go to the areas in front or behind.
Therefore, those [abscesses] that are in the joints should not be invaded with iron (i.e., surgically treated), for contraction would very easily follow.
But [for] that which is on the head or on the forehead, split the skin with a spatula/spatula-shaped knife. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and cut it out by the roots.
And if it is large, then you lift it with a hook, and peel/excise it, and then stop/stanch [the bleeding]; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51 (or 50 if using a zero-based numbering system).
On the incision of an abscess which occurs from a hernia that occurs in the belly/abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies that growths appear which are called fungals due to their similarity to fungi/mushrooms, whose roots are thin and the head/top of which is large; and sometimes their edges are inverted/turned inward, and there are those that are small, and there are those that are very large/magnified.

Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical hernia/protrusion. (Literally: Of the treatment of eminence of the navel)
An umbilical hernia is caused by many reasons; either by fissures of the peritoneum (ciphhac) which is over the abdomen, and from there the tissues or intestines emerge as occurs in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously stated, or by trapped wind/gas.
But if it is due to a fissure of the peritoneum and protrusion of the tissue, then the color of the swelling is similar to the body's color; and it is smooth without pain, and appears in different locations. And if it is due to a protrusion of the intestine, then the appearance of that is, compared to what we have described, more vehemently different, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and then returns; and it is very sensitive to the touch, and it becomes much larger after bathing and strenuous activity/work.
But if it is caused by fluid/humidity, then it is smooth and is not hidden when pressed with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it make a gurgling sound (crepitus).
And if it is caused by blood, then with these signs the swelling appears, tending toward black.
But if it is caused by dead flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is fixed in one spot/position/size.
But if it is caused by flatulence, then its touch is smooth.
And the procedure for it is that you consider then if the protrusion of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or from a vein, or from flatulence; then you must refrain from treating it, because in that case there is danger and fear, just as I taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings/abscesses of arteries and veins.
And if the protrusion of the navel is due to the intestine or the membrane/tissue, then you must order the patient to hold his breath, and stand upright/extended; then probe with a  probe around the entire navel, and then order him to stand facing you between your hands upon his feet, and then wrap broad bandages around the navel over the place you probed; and then extend/pull out half of the swelling upwards with a large hook; and then tie/bind it around with strong thread or silk, and tie it up properly.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling above the binding and insert your finger into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found it or secured it with the binding, then you must pull softly/carefully and push the intestine back into the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane/tissue, then take it with a hook; and cut away its excess/superfluous parts.
But if you encounter an artery or vein, then avoid/go around that area, and then return to your procedure; and take two needles and thread them with strong thread and insert the two needles around the area that you have made around the swelling in the form of a cross, making them penetrate; and then constrict the swelling in four places over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the stretched flesh rots and falls off by itself; or cut it when it has thinned and rotted, and then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the swelling of the navel is from flesh growing there or from moisture/fluid, then you must drain the swelling as we have said and remove the blood or fluid, which you find there, and then treat it with what is necessary and what will strengthen/heal the area.

And I have tried all these methods and I did not find better cleansing with forceps, with binding with bile than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times it avoids the skin underneath, since the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is very easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with the cleansing which is done by a cautery, and is finger-shaped, which cobblers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not safe, because it easily enters its opening.

And then put your hand to his penis and blow into the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis has emerged, and then cleanse it of any dirt that has accumulated there.

But the error in cleansing is that you might too easily remove the inner foreskin, completely, or part of it near the incision.

On the extraction of a stone (likely a bladder stone/kidney stone).

And know that some stones have angles and edges, which makes their extraction difficult.

But if there is more than one stone, then at the beginning you push your hand to the opening of the bladder and then incise upon it; then push it a little toward this (opening); and similarly do if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is ignorance to incise it with a large incision, for one of two things will happen to the patient: either he will die or he will suffer a continuous dripping of urine, because the area is not completely healed.
But continue to push it out until it is expelled, or work at breaking it up with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with powder of aloe and dragon's blood, and bind it. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that hot swelling may be reduced.
Then lay the patient on their back, and do not undo the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied it, wash the area with water and a little oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazlicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burning, and corrosive swelling, and it appears as if the blood were congealed in the bladder, and because of this urination is impeded (and this is known by the discharge of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it provokes corruption and putrefaction of the bladder.
And then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with an appropriate remedy for every type, with that which suits it for healing until it is healthy.
And it is necessary during the whole treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines that are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is stuck there, and thus urination is blocked, then treat it with what I recommended before you resort to incision, for too many times incision has been blamed for this condition; and I have already tested this, and that is that you take a perforator of polished iron, of which this is its form:
with a triangular tip, sharp, fixed in wood.
And then take bile, and with that tie the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
And then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches that stone. And turn the perforator with your hand in that stone little by little, and you enlarge its perforation until you make it penetrate to the other side; for the urine is immediately released.
And then press your hand over what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, for it is broken up, and is cast out with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you for any reason that prevents it, then tie one thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. And then cut down on the stone in the penis between the two ligatures. And then remove it. And then untie the ligature and cleanse the congealed blood which is found in the wound.
And the thread ligature below the stone is not necessary, except so that it doesn't return to the bladder, and the other ligature above [is necessary so that] when the thread is untied [after] the removal of the stone, the penis returns to its place and the wound closes.
And for this reason, it is necessary for you, when you tie the thread above, to lift the penis to the upper parts, or that it returns towards your abdomen, and the wound closes as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of stone from women.
Stone is rarely generated in women.
But if a stone appears in any of them, then its treatment is difficult, and prohibited in many ways.
One of them is that the woman is easily ashamed/modest.  (Or possibly "easily embarrassed")
And the second is that you don't find a well-bred woman who will reveal herself to the physician when she is chaste, or is one of the women having husbands.
The third is that you will not find a woman well-versed in this art, especially the operation by hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone, therefore you need a deep incision, and in that there is danger/risk.
But if necessity compels you to it, then you must take a well-learned female physician, and she is rarely found.
But if you are deprived of her, then seek a chaste and subtle physician, or employ a well-trained matron skilled in women's matters, or a woman who has learned part of this art through training.
Then have her present, and command her to do everything that you tell her, from the examination for the stone at the beginning; which is that if the woman is a virgin, then she should put her finger in her rectum and search for the stone.
Then if she finds it, she should hold it under her finger, and then you order her to incise over it.
But if she is not a virgin, then instruct the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after she has placed her left hand over the bladder, and compresses it well.
Then if she finds it, she should gradually move it away from the opening of the bladder downwards with the tip of her finger using her utmost skill until it reaches the root of the thigh.
And then incise over that location halfway between the vagina and the root of the thigh on whichever side it is stopped, and feel for the stone in that area, and her finger must not be moved from the stone, holding it underneath.
And the incision should be small at the beginning; and then insert the probe into that incision, and if she feels the stone, then enlarge the incision according to the size that she knows the stone will be extracted from.
And know that there are many kinds of stones: for some of them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and having branches.
Therefore, you should know how many kinds there are so that you can indicate what you want.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the area and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is extracted.
And make sure you prepare the instruments that are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may be helped by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that is incised, then you apply powder to the area and compress it with bandages and a tight dressing, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will very quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the acuteness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the area can bear it, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62. (Capitol is likely a variant spelling of "Capítol," meaning "Chapter")
On the incision/cutting over an inguinal hernia.  (Hernia "acoza" likely refers to a specific type, possibly inguinal given its location near the "coyssas" (thighs) in the previous section.)
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of fluid in the white tunic, which is under the chain/cord of the genitals containing the testicle, and is called a tunic (or membrane).
And sometimes it is in the very membrane which nature has prepared for it in the area of the testicle, so that it looks like there is another testicle; and the spermatic cord is between the white tunic, which we have mentioned, and it is nothing but a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is by a weakness which occurs in the genitals, because of which this matter is poured into them, and sometimes occurs from a blow made on the genitals.
And this fluid has many colors, for its color either tends towards yellow/citrine, or bloody/sanguine, or red, or is black and fecal-like, or is watery white, and this is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white tunic, which we have mentioned, then the abscess/swelling is round, inclining a little to length like the shape of an egg, and surrounds the testicle, for the fluid contains it on all sides.
And if the fluid is in its own membrane, then the swelling is round with part of the testicle and therefore one thinks that it is another testicle.
And if the fluid is between the testicle's cord and the white tunic, then one feels a lump/mass underneath.  (Literally: "then you feel underneath the feeling/lump")
And when you want to know the color of the fluid, then you test the color of the fluid in the swelling with a square instrument, the form of which has been described before; and according to what comes out on the mark of the instrument, judge what is inside it.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron (a surgical instrument), then it is necessary that we order the patient to be phlebotomized (bled) if this is possible for you, and see that his body is full (of blood - i.e., not anemic).
And then he should be laid on his back on something a little high/raised, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit on his left side, and order the assistant to be on his right, who should extend his staff (rod/instrument) on either side at the testicle's cord and at the area of the abdomen's navel.
And then take a broad spatula and cut the spermatic cord in the middle lengthwise nearly to the pubic bone.
And the incision should be made straight and equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the surrounding white tunic (membrane).
And peel/scrape it, and observe, and be careful that you do not incise it; and let your scraping, with which you scrape it partially, be suspended from the egg more and subtly rubbed with the scraper according to the amount possible for you.
And then pierce the tunic full of water with a wide perforation/opening, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips/edges of the incision with hooks, and extend the membrane/tunic upwards; and do not touch the spermatic cord contained within, and incise/cut the membrane/tunic in the manner that is possible for you. And incise/cut it completely or in pieces, and especially its thin side, because if you do not investigate its incision thoroughly, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
But if the testicle proceeds from that outside of its cord at the time of your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the membrane, return it inside, and then join the two edges of the spermatic cord with a suture, and then treat it like other wounds, until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another disease, then you must tie the vessels which are above the suspensory muscle/ligament, and extract the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the water is collected in two separate places, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part as you have done in the other likewise; and if it seems to you to be one operation, then you do it (as one).
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over both testicles and the area of the abdomen; and place on top folded cloths and cushions, and bind these over with a bandage which is of this form:
and then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is pressed, of cotton or dampened wool, and they should be fastened tightly in the waistband of the trousers/pants, and one of the ends should take from the buttock to the back, and the second should take to the part of the calf, and two ends under both thighs and two other ends over both groins, and all the fastening should be joined in the waistband of the trousers.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with a cautery instead of incision with iron; and you should take a thin, knife-like cautery and incise with it the scrotum of the testicles, and it is heated as we have described until the white membrane containing the fluid is exposed, and then you take a cautery of this form: and it is similar to a Greek letter.
And then pierce with it the membrane, and it is heated until all the moisture is expelled.
And then stretch the membrane with hooks, and excoriate it with hooks, and excoriate it with a thin, heated cautery; and incise it as much as you can until you eradicate all of it; and be careful of the testicle that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this operation is safer and further from blood flow, and better than operating with an incision.
But if you encounter a problem like a burn during the incision or cauterization, or bleeding, then you must treat whichever problem arises appropriately, with the treatment that suits it as described.
But if the patient is afraid and does not tolerate the second operation because of its dreadfulness, then administer perforation as I have described; and it is when you are certain, by what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery/serous.
Then you must have the patient sit on a raised seat/chair, then pierce the abscess at its lowest point with a broad spatula/instrument, and make the perforation along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the fluid/water flows out.
Then place cotton over the area and bandage it and leave it, so that the remaining fluid drains. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the drainage of the fluid when you pierce it: and this only happens because the white membrane in the opening of the wound creates resistance and prevents the flow of fluid, then you must insert into the wound a feather or instrument with which fluid is extracted from dropsies, the form of which has been described previously; or insert a small tube into the opening of the wound.
And know that the fluid will return, and will collect again in about six months or thereabouts; and when it has collected, then it should be pierced in the same manner, for thus the patient will spend his days.
Chapter 63.
On the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment/cure.
Know that incising this hernia is dangerously risky in most cases; therefore, it seems best to me to leave it alone and for the patient to be saved by this (method).
And I am recalling the operation on it and the types of abscesses/swellings.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the bodies from which the genitals are composed, and this happens for many reasons: either from an acute, severe excess (of humor), which spreads to the genitals, or from an injury/impact.
And the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body, and there is no pain found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And it easily becomes a hardened/stone-like abscess, the color of which is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by knotting/obstruction of the arteries, and by their swelling, as has already been mentioned, or by swelling of the veins.
But that which is caused by swelling of the arteries is recognized by the dispersal of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers; and it must not be touched (further/otherwise).
But that which is caused by the swelling of a vein is not dispersed at all by compression with the fingers.
And it is indeed possible that an incision can be made upon this type as upon other flesh; which is that you incise the spermatic cord, and then extend the testicle to the upper parts, and extract it from the white membrane (tunica albuginea?), and free the suspensory ligament of the vessels, and tie them off, and then incise the suspensory ligament after you have freed it completely from all parts of the testicle.
Then, if the testicle is consolidated with this fleshy growth, it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the membrane, or between that which is among the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all of that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a fleshy growth in the place of the adhesion, which is behind it, then it is necessary that you incise all of that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia. (Literally: Of the treatment of hernia that is of the intestines.)
A hernia is a swelling surrounded by a kind of wrapping similar to new growth with softening of the testicles, and it makes exercise and walking difficult for the patient.
And the treatment of this disease is dangerous for the reason previously mentioned.  (Implying it refers to a previously mentioned danger/difficulty).
However, I must describe the procedure as performed by earlier practitioners. (Or "the ancients/predecessors").
And I say that you must have the patient sit on a raised chair/seat; and then you push the suspensory [muscles/ligaments] of the testicles downwards; and then you hold the penis with your fingers at the base, which is next to the groin; and let another assistant, besides yourself, hold it and extend it forcefully.
And then you incise with a sharp, broad scalpel/knife via a transverse incision to the right of the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then peel/remove the skin from all sides as we have described in the removal of arteries, which are in the temples.
And then insert into them a needle with a double thread, and tie it [the thread] at the front of the location where you made the incision, and tie it again at the back of them [the vessels]. And then incise them [the vessels] in the middle with a straight incision along the length of the body, and remove what is collected of the putrid and corrupt humors/fluids.
And then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds in which you want healthy flesh to be generated so that the vessels that nourish one of the two genitals fall away, for there is no harm in this.
But if you happen to cut all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed, or that the testicles consequently dry up/wither along with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels and are shriveled up, or that they dry up so that there is no function/use with them.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a tear that happens in the peritoneum stretched over the abdomen on the side near the groin/angles of the abdominal wall,  through which the intestines descend from that rupture towards the scrotum.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or from its distension, and these two types occur for many reasons: either from a blow/impact, or from jumps/leaps, or from shouting, or from lifting something heavy, or similar things.
And the sign of this, when it is from distension of the peritoneum, is that it happens little by little over a long time, and doesn't happen suddenly, and there is swelling around the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is from a tear of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and there is a distinct swelling apparent below the groin/ligament appearing suddenly, and this is due to the exit of the intestine, and its mass appearing outside of the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal zirbal (likely referring to a specific type of hernia involving both the intestine and omentum).
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear/fissure occurs in the intestine, and here it is retained, and with this there is great danger/peril for the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of the types of this disease with iron there is danger/risk, therefore the use of it in that (treatment) should be avoided.
And the method of operating on it is that you instruct the patient to return the intestine with his hand to the area within its cavity if it obeys his manipulation, and then it should be rolled/massaged on its neck between your hands, and elevate his legs.
And then tighten the cord, which follows the spermatic cord to the upper parts, and incise the spermatic cord completely lengthwise; and then insert hooks into the two lips of the incision as needed to open the incision with them.
And make the incision as large as possible so that the testicle can be expelled/removed from it.
And then peel/remove the membranes which are under the spermatic cord, until the hard white membrane is exposed on each side.
Then insert your finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white membrane, which is under the spermatic cord, and between the second membrane, and release with it the adhesion that is at the back part of the testicle.
And then pull the spermatic cord outward with your hand, and with this extend the white membrane upward with your left hand, and lift the testicle with the membrane toward the incision; and order the assistant to extend the testicle upwards and you dissolve the adhesion that is behind with complete loosening/separation.
And search with your fingers that there is no part of the intestine wrapped/caught in the hard white membrane (tunica albuginea). But if you find any part of it there, then you push it back into the abdomen below.
And then make an incision in the part below the spermatic cord through which blood will flow and will thus be purged, as we have related in what we have said.
And then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it on the wound; and apply the bandage that we have described (and sometimes the white membrane, which we mentioned, is cauterized near the incision for fear of blood flow, because it often happens) and leave the bandage until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then it should be fomented with warm water, and [the water] placed on it until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatment for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of wind hernia / Of pneumatocele /  Of gas hernia (A pneumatocele is a hernia filled with air or gas).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone be bold with its treatment using iron (instruments/surgery).
And the first ones (physicians/authors) said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with veins/vessels; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision upon it with ease; and then dry the middle area around the abscess with that which opens it, until the vessels fall off.
And treat the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture/hernia that occurs in the groins.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groins as we have said, and elevate the area.
And do not allow any part of the intestines to descend to the genitals; and if they do, it is a small amount; and it returns in all hours/frequently.
But if the time is prolonged with this, a fissure is added in the scrotum until the intestine descends into the scrotum; and this happens because of the extension of the scrotum which is in the groins as we have said, the reason being that the scrotum is extended, and then softened and the area becomes abscessed and swells.
And its treatment is with a cautery as was described in the preceding narrative.
And sometimes it is treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on their back between your hands, and then dry the location of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with a cut along its width to the size of three fingers.
And then tighten the white tunic (membrane) that is under the skin until it is exposed/uncovered.
And then take the root (or possibly a ray/beam - context needed) and place it on the protruding part of the scrotum and press it against the front of the abdomen/belly.
And then sew the two protruding parts on the edge of the root/ray of the tunic/membrane, joining one to the other with the suture.
And then remove the end of the root/ray, and do not cut the tunic/membrane completely, nor extend the testicle, or anything else as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment of wounds, and as you would incise hemorrhoids, clean it and restore the wound until it is healed; because the tunic/membrane is contracted and the protrusion of the rupture is not adjusted/reduced.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to a cure/healing.
Chapter 68.
On softening the hardness/swelling of the testicles.
Many times the hardness/swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh between them is softened, and his appearance is made ugly/unsightly.
Therefore it is necessary for the one who is asked to treat him to have the patient lie on his back, and then cut out all the hardened/swollen part which is softened above the natural testicle; and then join the two edges/lips with sutures.
And if you wish, sew in the beginning the excess of the softened hardened/swollen part with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then cut between the sutures; and then treat it by the method of treating other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
On castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law, but for a reason it is permitted to me that I mention it in this book; and the mention of it is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that this may be in the knowledge of the physician when he is asked about it, that he may know the treatment of the one to whom it happens; and the other way is that we often have need of castration of other beasts for our benefit from them, such as monkeys, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And so I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by cutting with an incision.
But in that which is done by crushing, the way of the operation is that we make the beast sit in hot water until its genitals are softened and loose and hanging down. And then take them with your hand until they are dissolved and no longer apparent to the touch.
But in castration done by incision and cutting, it is necessary that you hold the beast and squeeze the cord of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory muscle, and make an incision over each testicle with a single cut until both testicles come out.
And then cut them after you have skinned each one. And don't leave anything on the surface of the wound, except the thin membrane which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than the one done by crushing, because crushing very easily leaves some testicular tissue, which causes the beast to still desire mating.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
About the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when there appears in what would be the space in the scrotum, in the area between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva, in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from this [opening] which is in the scrotum.
But in women, there is one type: the woman's vulva, above the pubic bone, is like two testicles of a man, somewhat diminished all around the outside, resembling the foreskin of a man and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three kinds: two for men and one kind for women. And it is necessary that you excise those excess/joined  fleshes, so that the swelling of the area disappears, and then treat them with the treatment for other wounds/sores.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the scrotum, there is no treatment or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
About swelling and protruding flesh caused by women.
Swelling occurs easily due to natural causes until it is stretched and becomes light in appearance; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much so that it spreads just as it does in men and prevents intercourse.
Therefore, you must hold the excess swelling with your hand or with a hook and incise it.
And do not go deep in the incision, especially near the root, so that it does not cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
Excess flesh is flesh that grows in the mother's womb, filling it up, and easily protrudes outwards in the shape of a tail (and because of this some early writers called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it just as we incise swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of arthritis/gout (alratica seems to be a variant spelling referring to a condition related to joint pain).
Arthritis/gout (alratica) occurs when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is too small. And it is either natural or accidental/acquired.
Accidental/acquired is due to a preceding illness, and this is caused either by excess flesh growth or by a thin or thick membrane/film.
And it is located either in the mother's vulva, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or in its lower part, and it prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and very easily obstructs menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examination by a midwife, if the blockage is visible, it's near the entrance; but if it is not visible, then you search for it with your fingers or with a probe/instrument.
But if the blockage is caused by a thin membrane/film and is close to the labia, then you cut and rupture it.
And you should apply powders to both labia, and then place your index fingers from both hands together/inside.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then spread the labia forcefully until that thin membrane/film is ruptured and the blockage is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and lie with a man daily so that the incised area does not close/heal over.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you need to incise it with a broad-bladed instrument or with a myrtle leaf.  (Note: "spatum lat" likely refers to some kind of surgical instrument, perhaps a spatula-like knife.)
But if the blockage is due to flesh/excess tissue, then lift it with a hook, and incise/cut it.
And you should have medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting/cauterizing, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and incense, mixed with egg white.
And then insert a wide lead cannula so that the wound isn't closed prematurely/easily.
And keep it (the cannula) in for a few days and administer some drying medications.
And then it should be treated with another healing treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the afterbirth of other flesh (tissues), and then it is necessary to cut in this manner. But if this is due to cancerous abscess, the cancerous abscess which is in the afterbirth should not be opposed with iron (surgical instruments) in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of fissures, and of red swelling that occurs in the vulva of women.
