Hemorrhoids are swellings of the orifices of many veins from which much blood flows continuously; and when they are old hemorrhoids, they become hard. (lit: "they are made/become hard.")
And I have already described in the division [earlier section] the types of these and their signs, and I will [now] say here which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore, I say that when hemorrhoids and hard lumps are in the anus and are not noticeable (lit: "do not appear to the feeling/sense"), then these should not be treated with iron (surgery).
But those which are at the opening of the anus, which are felt/palpable, are those that are treated.
And you need to put the woman in a cold place and then stretch out the pile with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and cut them out at the roots.
And then sprinkle on them some blood-stopping powder, one of the non-corrosive/biting powders, such as acacia and similar ones.
And then wrap her feet for an hour, and then place her in cold water.
And if the bleeding continues, then have her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate rinds and gallnuts and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this on his/her back.
And if the bleeding is stopped, good; and if not, apply two suction cups, and to the breasts without scarification/incision.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in plantain juice or in clary juice with spoiled wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
On perforation/fistula of an abscess/ulcer occurring in the breast.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the breast, such as cancer and hardened abscess and ulcerations and painful swellings and herpes and fistulas, hemorrhoids and fissures and styes and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these diseases and their types and their signs, and their treatments in the (relevant) section/chapter.
And it is fitting that I discuss in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the breast when it is one of those abscesses that collect pus, and if you want to know how its operation is performed with an instrument.
Therefore, you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and you see it redden, and if you feel a sensation of heat on it, then do not attempt its perforation, and treat it with that which helps its maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then you must have the woman sit on a chair that has two cushions; and she should recline on her back and bring her feet closer to the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms below her buttocks, and tie each one with a suitable bandage for this purpose.
And then the midwife should sit on the right side and administer the instrument with which the opening of the breast is opened, the form of which will be described in those things that will be done later/next.
And it is necessary when you want to use this instrument that you insert the nipple of the woman's breast with the tip, and that which of the instrument has entered the breast should not be more than the nipple of the breast and [should not] hurt the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the nipple of the breast, then you must place cushions/pads on the surface of the nipple of the breast so that the entry of the whole instrument into the nipple of the breast is prevented.
And you must place the screw that runs in the instrument on the upper side and the [one who] administers it should hold the instrument and turn the screw until the opening of the breast/nipple is opened/dilated.
And when the discharge appears, and is touched with the hand and is watery and soft, then you must wipe it from around the patient's head with a soft cloth/sponge.
And when the blood is evacuated, then you must place in the wound a wick soaked in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the compress/bandage on the incision in the nipple of the breast.
And place outside the breast and over the dressing clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for about two days with an ointment that is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the breast and the wound are washed, as if baptized, with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or long birthwort has been boiled, and then return to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the breast itself, then you must refrain from treating it with iron (surgical instruments), treating it instead with what we have said in the section/chapter.
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives how to treat living embryos when they are in an unnatural position/presentation.
It is necessary for the midwife to recognize at the outset the natural presentation of the birth by its signs.  And that is when you see the woman pushing downwards, and desiring to breathe fresh air, and moreover is relieved by what is on top of her (presumably pressure from the fetus), and hastens the exit of the embryo, then you should know that this birth is in the natural position. This is when the exit is head first, and the afterbirth with it, either attached or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then the belly should be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, for when it descends head first, the afterbirth comes with it, and she (the mother) is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification/cleansing.
But any other presentation than this is unnatural and undesirable. For when the embryo comes out feet first, and its body has come out before its head and its feet, or one of its hands is by its foot, or its head has come out and its hand, or its belly, and it is quickly turned onto its neck, such and similar presentations are undesirable.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and skilled in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing every presentation of these, and what the trick is in these, so that it may be understood by this and be acted upon accordingly.
When the fetus comes out head first by a natural birth, and it is difficult for the woman and the birth is hard for her, and you see her strength weakened, then have her sit, or have the women hold her, and bathe her vulva with cooked fenugreek and with moistening oils.
And then the midwife should insert a small spatula between her fingers and cut the afterbirth with it, or tear it with her fingernail until the fluid that is in it flows out. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it doesn't descend, then the woman needs to be given an enema of psyllium mucilage and fenugreek mucilage with sesame.
And after the enema, tell her to strain and make her sneeze with condiments (or spices); and cover her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if both hands of the fetus are presented first, then you should gently push them back little by little. But if they do not go back in, then place the woman on a chair and raise her feet, and then shake the chair on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she doesn't fall during the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then cut them off and extract the rest of the fetus or tie the body to his hand with cloth and extract it evenly, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus comes feet first, then you must push them upward, and then turn the fetus slightly, and then straighten it, and then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
But when it has come out up to the thighs, then you order her to push, and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have described, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in the normal position, for it will then be expelled with an easy delivery.
And if it is still obstructed after all that we have described, then take mucilage of marshmallow and dissolved gum, and pound them together in the mortar with good pounding, and then anoint the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen with it. And then make her sit in warm water, up to her hypochondria (ribs/upper abdomen).
And when you see that her lower parts are softened, then make a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to her. And when you have applied the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and close her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her abdomen with light compression, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
Likewise for a fetus lying sideways, hanging by one of its hands. Be ingenious in reducing its hand, but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then have her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with a vehement shaking, and then administer the remedies which I have recounted, made of mucilages.
And if the birth has started, then make her sit on a birthing stool, and then focus on the reduction of the hand, and rock the fetus according to the natural position, and make the woman sneeze and tell her to push until it is delivered.
Likewise for the fetus lying on its neck with its hands spread out, and its face is towards its mother's back. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and deliver it with ease.
And when it is rocking/in motion, then apply to its vulva what we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then tell her to push little by little, and when you have done this, then have her sit on a birthing stool and tell her to lean on her right side, and then tell her to bear down/push; and make her sneeze with spices/a sneezing agent. Delivery of the prepared fetus onto its side.
When they come out horrifically from the mother in this way, examine (it), and then you incise the afterbirth with your fingernail until the fluid is evacuated.
And if the fetus is wrapped around its face and neck, then you cut it from the navel so that it is not constricted and dies.
And then push the fetus to the inside parts until you position it as it should be, and tell the woman to push/bear down several times, and it will be expelled with such ease, God willing.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born, but this is strange and rarely happens; but they are usually stillborn, not alive.
And within these, be it twins or more, is a single afterbirth (placenta), and they are separated by a membrane, which separates the ligaments in the navel of each of them.  And sometimes they are delivered just as easily as a single fetus, by such a natural ease, and they are sometimes born in a more problematic way, as we have described.
And if any of them are difficult to deliver and come in what we have called unnatural presentations, then you treat them as we have described.
And let the midwife be subtle and do this with ease and subtly, and with patience, and be ingenious according to each presentation, according to what is prepared for her by her wit, leading to a successful outcome.
Remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is miscarried.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman miscarried and delivered six, and another ten, all formed, which fact was established by the measurement of the glorious learned men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, for very easily such a thing might appear to her, and then she should address it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with what we have said in the section on medical treatment and it has not come out, and you are forced to use a manual operation, then you must consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and not suffering from any of the conditions for which loss is feared.
Then the woman should be turned over on the bed onto her back, and her head should be inclined downwards and her thighs raised.
And the assistant should hold her on both sides or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend itself during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with humid oils and mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the womb, and then search with her hand for a place in which to fix the hooks into the fetus.
And finding a suitable place for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned head-down, and then you fix the hooks in its eyes, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or under the chin, or in its collarbone, or in the places near the ribs, or under the hypochondria (area below the ribs).
But if the descent is feet-first, then in the groin, or in the middle of the ribs.
And it is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand, and place its curve in the place which is between the fingers of the left hand, and insert the hand gently and fix the hook in any of those places which we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty space (presumably the uterus).
And then place and fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third if necessary, so that the pull is equal, and there is no extension along its straight line only.
But rather it should be moved in every direction, so that its extraction is eased just as is done with a tooth during its removal/extraction.
And it is necessary in this that the tension between them be softened/reduced; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert them cautiously and rotate/turn with them that which is retained.
Then if part of the fetus is expelled, then the hooks should be moved to a place that is higher up; and do this until all of the fetus is expelled.
But if one hand has come out before the other, and it is not possible to return it due to its constriction/contraction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until it is completely expelled, and cut it either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.
And thus you should do with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or it suffers constriction in the birth canal, or there is fluid accumulated in its head, then it is necessary to insert a sharp spatula between the said bones and split the head and extract the fluid, or to crush it with an instrument which is called "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is next to this one.
And similarly do so if the fetus is naturally large-headed: and then it is necessary to incise it with a pointed instrument, or take it with a hook as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has come out and is constricted below the wishbone/clavicle, then let it be cut so that the fluid which is in the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey extraction, then you shall immediately incise the head as best you can.
And if the lower part of the belly is infected with an abscess or has dropsy, then it is necessary that you pierce it so that the fluid within it drains.
But if the fetus is positioned feet first, then its extraction is easy and its direction to the mouth of the womb is easy/straightforward.
But if it is constricted below the belly and the foot, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you cut the belly and the foot so that what is in them drains out.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mouth of the womb is open, then she inserts her hand into the birth canal, and searches with it for the head, and draws it with the fingers to the mouth of the womb, and then inserts into it one hook, or two hooks, and pulls it with these.
And if the mouth of the womb is narrowed by a hot swelling which occurs in it, then it is necessary that it not be worked upon; rather, it is necessary then to administer an infusion of moist, fatty substances. And administer poultices/plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned: and what we have said about the live fetus should be administered.
And if this is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything inside of the afterbirth (or placenta).
And when you have completed all of your work, then administer what we have said about the treatment of hot abscesses which occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then you should use styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a dead fetus was, and then on top of that fetus she became pregnant again, and then the other fetus also died. It happened to someone that after a long time an abscess formed in their navel; and it swelled until it opened, and it began to discharge pus (or matter from the arm - less likely given the context).
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied on him some strongly drawing plasters, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones in the belly (in that area).
And then I thought that they were the bones of the dead fetus.
So I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones from it, the woman being in thousands of difficult situations [positions/childbirth pains?], and yet lived in such a manner for a long time, a little pus still running from it.
And I do not affirm this to be strange, but rather that in this there is science and help in what the skillful physician administers with his hand for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of hook with which the mother [womb/uterus] is opened:
This is the form of a clamp in which the labia are held, to which holding are two hooks at two ends of two shafts. But this hook must be more subtle than a constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each shaft be two fingers and the edge of each one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two shafts are two fittings of the same wood that are already fixed, the length of which is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it may be opened with them when you turn the two prescribed hooks.
Another similar form, more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood shafts in the likeness of forceps. But however, at its extremity there are two additions as you see, and the length of these additions is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the womb with these, then have the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging, [with] the space between her thighs open. And then open your hand with the instrument just as you do evenly with forceps, according to the amount you want to open the mouth of the womb until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo, which the ancients described:
A method of impulsion with which the fetus is pushed:
A method of reduction with which a large head is constrained/reduced.
And it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like a saw's teeth, with which it is incised and constrained/held. A method of slight/small reduction:
Form of a hook having a spine/point:
Form of a hook having two spines/points:
Form of a spatula having two extremities with which the head is cut off.
Form of two broad spatulas.
All these instruments are practiced/used, and the more the types of these are multiplied and prepared by the artificers/craftsmen, the easier it is for their operation and the more highly they are regarded by men due to their quantity/availability.
And do not neglect to prepare any of these instruments, for the need for them is unavoidable/inexcusable (i.e., you must have them).
On the extraction of the afterbirth (or placenta).
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you must order the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with pepper, and tightly close your hand over her mouth and over her nostrils, and if by this means it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mother's womb, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, gum arabic, cassia bark, and centaury. All these herbs or some of them should be submerged in water in the pot, and place it on the fire.
And then place a reed pipe in the patient's womb, and hold it there until the steam comes to the womb's cavity.  (Note: "mother" here likely means womb, as in referring to the source of the issue)
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains and it (the afterbirth) hasn't come out, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow mucilage, and then insert it into the vulva and search with it for the afterbirth, and when she has found it, then take it and draw it out little by little.  But if it is attached to the lining of the womb, then insert her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it bit by bit as instructed so that the womb doesn't tear due to forceful pulling. Before, it is necessary that it be moved to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the opening of the womb is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and the medicinal application of herbal oil.
But if it is not expelled with all the methods that we have described, then take care not to exert yourself with repeated attempts at extraction, but rather it is necessary that what has come out of it to the opening of the womb be tied to the woman's thigh, and then anoint it with ointment and tetrapharmacum; for this will cause it to rot within a few days and it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it rots, a bad odor rises from it to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and therefore suitable fumigations should be administered for this.
But some of the Ancients have also tried fumigation with dead nettle and dried figs.
Form of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or bronze.
And the narrow end should be placed in the vulva and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation material placed on the embers of the fire.
And keep it there until the fumigation material is consumed, and then repeat with another.
Treatment of an imperforate anus in an infant.
Many children are born without an anal opening, and their anus is thin and closed/sealed. Then the midwife must perforate that membrane with her finger; or else, perforate it with a sharp instrument, taking care not to touch the surrounding tissue/muscle.  (The word "lacert" likely refers to muscle or flesh near the anus that should be avoided).
Then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will close up again, then place a cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child needs to defecate.
And sometimes blockage of the anus occurs due to the hardening/scarring of a plague sore or abscess; then it is necessary that this hardened tissue be incised, and then be treated as we have said regarding the treatment and lead (treatment).  (The "lead" here might refer to a lead instrument or a lead-based ointment, details are unclear from this sentence alone.)
On the treatment of fistulas that occur near the anus. (Or perhaps more broadly, "below/in the lower body")
Fistulas that occur in the lowermost parts are swellings and lumps which occur near the anus externally or in the perineal space.
And it is one fistula or many.
And when this swelling is old, and a watery white fluid or thin pus flows from it, some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate (deeper tissues).
And these penetrating ones are recognized because there is discharge and flatulence from them; and sometimes the patient suffers from the discharge, and worms often emerge from them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the bladder in the perineum, and into the urethra; and some of them penetrate into the hip joint and into the hollow of the buttock.
And how a fistula penetrating into the anus is distinguished from a non-penetrating one is that you put your index finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity (or winding path).
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger (in the rectum).
And if you do not feel it at all and no discharge, worms, or flatulence comes out of the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, the sign of it is a discharge of urine from it and the prevention of this so that the area may be healed with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the hip joint or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then the sign of it is that the probe goes in there if there is no tortuosity to the bone, and (there is) increased pain in the area surrounding the part; and that pus constantly comes out of it; and that medication is of no use in it, nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is work and vanity for the foolish physicians who are fixated on it/them.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for according to what I will explain to you, and what I have already tried.
And the patient lies between your hands on their back and raises their legs up, their thighs bent towards their stomach, and then insert a lead probe, or of bronze if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe reaches.
And if the patient feels it around the anus/rectum, then you must insert your index finger into the anus/rectum.
And if you feel in your finger the now uncovered probe penetrating by itself, other than what you feel between your finger and that siphac (membrane/tissue) or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
But however, some say that it is healed in some men late/slowly.
And the medication by which healing is hoped for in him is that you ignite a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been described before, and insert this ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus/rectum; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fistulous attached tissues, which resemble the shaft of a bird's feather, have been cauterized.
And then treat it with compresses soaked in butter until all those cauterized tissues come out, and then treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate all the way to your finger which is in the anus/rectum, and between your finger and the probe there is a thick veil of flesh or membrane, and you see the fistula in such a way that the surface of the intestine is dry, then dry the intestine from the beginning of the fistula, and dry it with the probe, letting it be in the fistula until the dried area reaches the place where the end of the probe reaches, and through the probe, it (the fistula) is freed and evacuated/drained.
And then clean those fistulous tissues, which are similar to the shaft of a bird's feather, and let nothing of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood that comes between you and your work when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterizing with fire combines two good properties: that is, cauterization of those attached tissues, and stopping of the blood [and] drying of the humors.
And then treat it with compresses soaked in butter or crushed cabbage until the area produces pus, and then at that time treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these are medicines in which there are astringent and drying properties; when it is thus restored, it is healed and you do not fear recurrence/relapse.
But if the fistula already reaches into the depth/rectum of the anus and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it with that.
And then if you feel the probe and between you and it there is a thin membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, at that time there is no treatment for him except by way of faith and hope.
This can be administered in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert the necessary medicines and the appropriate treatment; and do not proceed much with cutting so that you do not incise the muscle containing the anus/rectum, and thus involuntary defecation happens to the patient; and then treat it with what we have said for it will be healed very quickly; or stretch that membrane and clean it after you dry it near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another sharp-ended instrument, and then clean those fistulous tissues that are in the incision as far as you can by scraping; and then endeavor to make the restoration of the whole wound by cutting, and if the upper orifice is not consolidated/closed, and the fistula remains open in the area of the anus, it will be easier afterwards for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is treated in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is at the side of the anus, around the surface of the body with the buttock [or perhaps *along* the surface of the body towards the buttock] and at the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing leather/hides [cuirs/skins] in this manner:
and insert into it thread wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity/depth.
But if it penetrates the margin of the anus from the inside, then you pull the thread out of that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and tie the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and then repeatedly cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues that are between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall off.
And then treat the wound until it is closed and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it be deep in the perineum, because it is not up to you to do this so that you do not cut the muscle; and then continue with that until it is healed.
The form of a thorn-shaped spatula with which the fistula is opened: the curvature of which is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as you sometimes do.
On suturing hemorrhoids from which blood flows in their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are located between the buttocks resembling embers/coals, or they are like grape seeds. And of these, some are small and large, and blood flows from them constantly, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its edges.
But however those that are outside the anus are according to the limbs of little humidity, from which flows a light yellow water or a little blood with a continuous scab/crust, and are according to the color of the body/skin.
And the treatment for the one that is inside the anus is that you order the patient to sit down and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then cover yourself and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then cut them down to their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their moisture and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth or with cotton or with a woolen bandage, and extract them with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of a cautery; or cauterize them as described previously in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey and protrude, then give the patient an enema with a small bite (irritant), and let it be washed with that water which is in the anus so that it obeys and protrudes easily when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is minor, and you should take them with your fingernail, or take them with a hook, and cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And the one that you have incised, you must administer sutures to them in this manner: take wrapped thread and insert it into a needle and then draw the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lowest part of the needle, and laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong tightening, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them and do not sew it, so that the excess blood flows from it.
And then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or ointment on the anus; and place it on the backside and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat them with ointments or other treatment until it is healed.
And when they are old and medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are made moist and the pus crust on top is removed, which prevents them from healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you scrape them more vehemently than the first time until they bleed a lot and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a whitlow, that is a nail abscess, is nothing but a round lump, the color of flesh, resembling the head of a small nail, and it occurs throughout the body, and mostly on the parts under the feet and on the fingers, and therefore causes excruciating pain.
And then it must be incised around the nail, and held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it as described in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, round, and callous eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it comes on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to stretch and incise it, or to constrict it with a thread or skin until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or a sharp medicine.
But a "formica" (ant bite/boil) is a small, felted or callous, large eminence on the surface of the body, occurring frequently on the penis, and when the cold encounters it, it is felt there like the bite of an ant, and is found around the body.
And its treatment is that you take a tube of a goose feather or a vulture feather, and let it be strong, and place it on the formica so that the circle of the tube contains its edges; and then turn your hand with the tube until you incise what is within the circumference of the formica, and place it within the substance of the flesh, and eradicate the formica from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of copper or iron according to this method:
Let the upper part of the tube be drawn to a point, blunt (that is, not perforated), enveloped so that the apprentice's grip on it is facilitated, as is its rotation.
And if you wish, cauterize the formica as was previously described in the cauterization of a "atheil" in the chapter on cautery.
On the form of the instruments that are used to give an enema. (Cristeri likely refers to an enema or clyster)
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or brass, or cast or hammered copper; and sometimes it is made from these instruments small and large according to those to whom they are administered, for the one that is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose rectums are fragile or painful, their enemas should be small.
The form of a large and small enema:
The head of the cannula above, on which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and it should have a distinction where the bag is tied, as you see.
And its lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) but smooth, tapering to a point; and on one of its sides there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the width of the hole should be according to the thickness of the liquid, and a little larger.
And the bag in which the medicines are placed should be made of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its root (or neck) should be one palm (long).
And it should be perforated with many holes as shown; and let the space between the holes be the width of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong raw gall from about ten gallbladders, or thereabouts; and attach the bag with it; and when the bag is placed in position, let it be tightened at the top of the syringe between two distinctive marks with strong constriction, and then cauterize each hole: But the way to cauterize with it is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient should lie supine on their back, and raise their legs up and place under them a leather or linen sheet; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or similar; and then insert it into the anus gently, and then the assistant should take the bag and squeeze it strongly with both hands at the same time until everything in the syringe is evacuated into the intestine; and then remove the syringe and the patient should raise their legs against the wall.
And if the medicine forces him to defecate, let him hold it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it for a whole night, it is better for his recovery.
But if you cauterize him for diarrhea and his defecation pains him, then you repeat the cautery until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or harmful abscesses in the anus, then the syringe should be very subtle and smooth, like the syringes with which children are treated.
On the treatment of wounds/sores/plagues. (The word "plagues" here is likely referring to a wider range of skin afflictions than the modern meaning.)
I have already said in the section on the treatment of wounds what was pertinent to the book, and thus I describe the treatment of these with my hand and with medicines according to their complexity/completion/perfection (depending on the exact nuance intended by "conpliment").
Therefore, I say that wounds are categorized according to how the wounds are made and according to the location where the wounds occur.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are like the throwing of a stone, or incision by sword or knife, or striking with a lance, or with wood, or with an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are like a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the foot, or the belly, or the liver, and similar ones on the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with the simple wounds of the head, for the discussion of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from the throwing of a stone or something similar, and it has only broken the skin and it is a large wound, and you fear the development of a hot abscess on that patient, then you should immediately phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw blood from it according to the strength of the patient if there is no cause preventing phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded area; and the patient should beware of repletion and wine and coarse foods.
But if some reason prevents bloodletting, then his food should be reduced and he should not go near meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess develops on it, cotton infused in rose oil alone or in wine in which a styptic has been steeped.
But if you are sure of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent, his own blood and, provided the air has not changed it, the powder which I will describe shortly.
And if the air has already changed it (the blood/wound), then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or made by incision with a sword or something similar, and its edges are not joined by stitching, then join them with sutures, as I will explain regarding their suturing.
But if the wound is already exposed to the bone and hangs, and there is little holding it except for a small tendon, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh back in place like a chain, and order the patient to avoid foods that are thick: head cheese and soft cheese and things like them, until he is recovered.
And if you find putrefaction in the sinew/tendon and it is not adhering to the bone, then you incise all that is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood might occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you examine the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it off; and if necessity compels you and you cannot do what we have said, then cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is insignificant; so you sprinkle this powder on them, for it strengthens them before the air changes them/infects them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part incense oil, two parts dragon's blood, three parts unslaked lime; let it all be very finely ground and sifted, and fill the wound with it, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it on top with pillows/cushions/compresses and leave it, and do not untie it while it is like this, that is not abscessed/inflamed, for the medicine must not be removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes use only lime, when incense and dragon's blood are not available to you.
And also perform this same procedure on large wounds when it is appropriate that they be covered and bandaged, and these are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered/infected.
And if the air has altered/infected them, and especially when it is in the summertime, then the application of this powder is not intended, but then treat them with what we have said, so that you place some digestive ointments on them, or that you put barley flour powder with water and honey on them until they expel pus; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a bone fracture occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you treat it with plaster.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of neck wounds/injuries. (Literally: "Of the wounds/plagues of the neck.")
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple head wound.
But if the nerve or artery of the neck is incised, then, if it is a nerve, there is no treatment for it except to avoid treating it with something that causes strong constriction, such as cinnabar and vitriol (which is like copper sulfate) and similar things, because they harm the nerves and constrict them. And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerves is cold and it is continuous with the brain, which is the highest of all the members/organs.
And if this [the wound] is treated with mild medications, such as washing it with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and red ochre when washed with fresh water, and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or bring the edges of it together, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or you find putrefaction in the lowest part of it, then pierce it in the most depressed place.
But if an artery has been incised in the wound and there is a flow of blood, then separate or dry it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if you have incised the vertebrae of the neck, or all of it, and the veins of the neck are saved, then you should join the edges of the skin with a suture over the neck bone and do not touch it; rather, smooth it and return it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with a proper bandage, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments that have styptic and stimulating properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has already caused damage to the bone, then you should clean it, and if there are bone fragments in it, remove all those that you can; and what you are not prepared to remove, leave it until the wound putrefies, and it becomes separated and its discharge is eased.
And if the wound is recent then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be mentioned later in its own chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then further consider if air comes out of the wound when the patient breathes; then you should know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then in the first hour you apply powder, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depths and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply a drawing ointment, and loosen it twice a day.
And if you do not have the ointment at hand, then place old cotton in the opening of the wound so that it absorbs the fluids that come out; and have the patient sleep on the wound so that what is collected in it will drain.
But if three or more days have already passed since the injury and the patient does not experience spasms, nor heart tremors, nor difficulty breathing, and you see that his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is not life-threatening; and then treat it with baths and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is hindered, and it remains open, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is by incision of sword or knife, and it is simple on the surface of the foot or the back, then treat it with what has been described previously regarding sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already damaged the bone, and pieces of it have been cut out, then you should enlarge the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are detached. But if they are not detached, then leave them until the wound becomes putrid, as their extraction will be easier.
And for other accidental wounds in the other limbs, the treatment and cure is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds of the abdomen/belly.
The disorder/illness which occurs in the abdomen is either great, or small, or middling.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and so it is that their reduction is more difficult if you do not take care to insert them into their place immediately, for it opens and swells making their entry difficult.
And therefore the best rupture is the middling one, because the reduction of the intestine is not difficult in that [one], as it is difficult in the two aforementioned types.
And know that suturing of the abdomen is done according to four methods, but two sutures are common, which are suitable for sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other unique sutures are common to the other wounds, however they are more specific to the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will explain each one to you with exposition and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has exited through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And so, when this is the case, you must warm it by submerging a sponge or wet cloth in plain warm water, or boil it in water with squinanth, cyperus, and spikenard, and foment the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes do this with wine, which has astringency, and is better than plain water in resolving inflammation.
And so, when you see the inflammation is resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which althea and mallows have been boiled, for by this its entry is eased with little effort.
But if its entry is hindered despite this treatment, then enlarge the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is suitable and there is no incision, and there is no need to resort to drying due to necessity and failure.
Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it curved and sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the tip slender, but not with the slenderness of a spatula, rather let it be slightly blunt.
And then when the wound is enlarged and the intestine comes out, then its reduction must be according to its natural form, into its proper place, if it is possible to do so, for it is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you must make the patient lie on their back, and place their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you place their head and feet higher than the parts below.
And similarly if the rupture is on one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to this, so that the part where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you must manage this in large wounds and in medium wounds.
And in small wounds, place that which is prepared by you, and then have the assistant, who is positioned between your compassionate hands, hold the entire rupture with his hand and bring the edges together, and then uncover from that area, next to the one who is sewing the suture, any nearby foreign matter.
And this is the common method of suturing: take the needle, or count the needle's length according to the size of the wound; and then leave [a space] from the edge of the rupture the width of a ring finger, and push the needle through, but do not include flesh at the two ends of the stitch, with the two ends of the thread which is below the stitch on the inside parts, until you make it penetrate that part; and you have already joined two ends of the stitch and two ends of the thread, making four strands.
And then tightly grasp [the flesh] with a double thickness around the circumference of the needle, and sometimes on both sides together, and then join the two edges and the wound by appropriate approximation; and then leave the width of a finger and insert another needle there, and then tie it with thread just as you did with the first needle; and do not cease doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completely closed the wound.
And the needles should be medium between thick and thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the stitch, and therefore they must be medium in thickness and thinness.
And the needle must not be inserted too close to the two edges of the wound, so that the flesh is not easily cut and the wound reopens before it heals.
And the stitches should not be too long so that they do not hinder the healing of the wound.
And then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient while they sleep; and you will place spread-out cushions all around the suture, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them there until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of stitching with needles is in every way more suitable for small wounds, because one needle or two, or thereabouts, is sufficient for closing them.
But the second suture is common: and that is that you close with stitches the four edges of the wound and the two edges of the tear, all at once, with a needle on which is wrapped thread of equal thinness and thickness.
And then when you have penetrated these four edges with the needle, bring the needle back out on the same side where you began, so that the drawn thread closes from the most superficial part of the wound downwards, so that the stitching is like the stitching of cloth with which clothes are tightened/sewn.
And leave between each stitch the width of the little finger.
And this stitching men know.
And with such stitching I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was greater than a palm, and about two palms of intestine from the middle part had come out and the rupture was in the middle of the limb; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been out of the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was healed in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I treated him and he is healthy; and after this he lived for many years, and is restored to his normal activities/health.
And the other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by a miracle it was, for I did not treat him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old cotton on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two specific kinds of stitching according to Paul(us)'s account/narration.
And you should begin with a chain stitch, and insert the needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And then when the needle penetrates the flesh and into the same laceration lengthwise and along the length of the whole body, leave the end of the thread where you have inserted the needle; and push the needle through the other end from the inside part to the outside part in the other extremity of the wound.
And then when you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate again in the same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate the thread in the extremity of the wound which is on its side until it penetrates completely.
And then begin on this same side and sew it with the end of the thread that is on the other side, and pull the needle from the flesh which it penetrates, and then put it back into that flesh and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this extremity of the wound; and pull it from the flesh which is on its side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew all that is outside according to this example.
And the summary of this manner of suture is that you sew with a furrier's stitch with which one sews furs, so that you sew the thread once from the side of the extremity of the flesh, and that you leave it the other time until you have completed it.
But the second type of proper suture, which is likewise called "Gauls" - this is from his speech and recounted by him - is that you sew it according to the likeness with which some doctors sew it, so that they join each part to its corresponding part fitting to it by nature; and they apply the extremity of the other part of it and the extremity of the wound to the other extremity of it, and this is done according to what I have told you.
It is necessary that you stick the needle into the edge of the wound nearest to you on the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that place, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread.
And then turn them each, and make each one penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in the two edges of the other wound that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common simple suture, and it is the suture of which there was previous mention.
This is the speech of Gaul. (Or, this is what Gaul said.)
And moreover some say, suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, you insert a thread into each of them, and begin by inserting the needle on your side, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew hides/leather in the same way.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than [those of] other places of the belly.
But the treatment of plague is [like/the same as] the treatment of other plagues/infectious diseases.
This is because when you treat the fresh plague/wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the swelling and sew it and dress it properly, then you apply incarnative (flesh-generating) powder over it.
And if the air has already altered the plague/wound, then apply some ointments, which are available to you, until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out, and [the edges] are consolidated/joined together; and then treat it as you would treat other wounds/sores until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then apply old cotton over it as soon as it begins to produce pus/discharge, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you will not need any other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the plague/infection will spread and harm the principal members/organs, then you should soak wool fleece in oil of moderate quality, or in rose oil, and place it around the areas between the elbows and under the armpits.
And if he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, for this often happens, then administer an enema with astringent black hellebore wine, especially if the decay has reached the intestine and a passage has formed penetrating into its cavity.
And know that an issue with the large intestine is easier to heal than one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not fully recover from a wound that occurs in it; and this is because of the large number of veins that are in it and because of the size of the vessels within them, and because of the thinness of its wall, and because of its proximity to the nerves.
But if it is the omentum that protrudes from the wound, and you find it fresh/newly protruded, reduce/replace it according to your method with which you similarly replace the intestines.
But if some time has passed and it has already turned green or black, then you must constrict it with bile on the area that is blackened, so that a flow of blood comes, because there are veins and arteries in the omentum.
And then incise what is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the bile hanging from the part below the wound outside it, so that the pulling of it towards the decay of the omentum and discharge from the opening of the wound will be eased for you.
Remembrance/Reminder of the wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it be restored in some men, because of this I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with the thrust of a lance and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and from it issued excrement and flatulence/gas.
Therefore I continued the treatment of him even though I wasn't confident in his healing, but I persisted diligently in treating it until he was healed and the place was consolidated/closed.
And after I saw the place already healed, I feared that some bad accident might befall the patient in his belly; but no bad accident happened to him for that, and he remained in his best condition eating and drinking, and then he entered the bath and exercised his usual/said routines.
And some experienced [physicians] have said that when a wound happens in the intestine and it is small, then it must be bound in this way: you take ants with large heads; and then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one of these ants, having its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then when it bites down on them and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and will not be dislodged.
And then place another ant near the first, and don't stop doing this with ants one next to the other along the length of the entire wound.
And then return/reduce it and sew the wound; and those heads will remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be sewn with fine thread which is made from animal intestine attached to it, after it has been threaded onto a needle; and you take the end of this intestinal thread, and it should be very clean/thinned, and then tie the end of it with thread from it, wrapped around, and then thread this thread onto needles and sew the intestine with it, and replace it in the rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only by way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, for then there is no remedy but alleviation, and it cannot be completely healed.
On the treatment of scrofula and fistula.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it becomes chronic and old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and constantly emits pus and is not cut out, then, in whatever limb it may be, is universally called a fistula and we call that scrofula.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, felted lump, which is not painful and which has a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather; and for this reason it is called a feathery ulcer, that is, a quill.
And in some men it is moist, which emits pus; and the moisture is very easily cut away in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and it is scant, and it is coarse, and it is thin.
I have already shown all these causes, and their signs, and their treatment with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you know the treatment of a fistula which is done by means of an operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the limbs of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the skull, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints composed by composition such as those of the hand and foot; and there are those that cause damage to the nearby principal limb and similar ones.
And so those of these fistulas that are in this manner are among the illnesses difficult to heal, and do not receive treatment, and occupying oneself with them is labor and ignorance.
And if there are any fistulas whose depth does not reach these places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its treatment, and you want to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of brass or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, you will explore it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a thin lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent and curved along the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has many horrific movements/openings, it is not possible for you to explore it with a probe.
Then inject the opening through its orifices, for the liquid with which you inject it will go towards the other orifices and run out of them.
And then inquire carefully, according to whatever way it is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves there, and if the cavity of the fistula is deep or shallow, or if it is a fistula that has many orifices. And be about all this with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of pain through your compression with your hand on the place, and indications of the symptoms and of the causes incidental to the abscess/fistula.
And when you have considered all these matters with true attention, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and you should consider then if the fistula is clearly superficial or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula according to what was said before in its description, and remove that which is in it, the discharge and corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the opening of the flesh, or of skin and the like, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of deep profundity, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it deeply as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then administer bandages soaked in sharp/caustic medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this a few times until the sharp/caustic medicine corrodes everything remaining in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you will examine it and dry it as we have recommended, unless you are prevented by some vein or nerve or principal member, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is any corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will make it flesh again until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and emits discharge as it did before, then know that you have not reached all of the corrupted part; and then you uncover it again and carefully carry out its scraping and cleaning with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And then if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then know that the corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not been able to reach, and then you have no choice but to leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or you are able to extract it, then extract it with fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully extract all of them, and leave nothing behind using all your strength.
But if some sort of humidity obstructs you, and there is no danger or risk of error in cutting them, then cut those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply to the wound something that will make the wound rot, and leave it until the flesh and ligaments around those bones have decayed, and make sure the wound does not heal, and keep the incision you made during your operation open.
Meanwhile, if you are worried, place on it cotton soaked in ground soap with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in some of the green ointments, because when those ligaments rot, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction will be easier.
But if it is a large bone such as the bone of the leg or thigh or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only on the surface until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then heal the wound.
But if a large part of it is corrupted, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut it open and completely incise it up to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I will tell you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you can use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts [the pain] moved on the matter within the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and he had no external cause.
And then the time was prolonged with this due to the error of the physicians until the abscess opened, and many substances rushed out of it, and its treatment was then applied until the leg became fistulous, and many orifices were made in it, all of which emitted putrefaction and bodily fluids.
And then a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise hand among them in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the substances that flowed from those orifices in large quantities; and the man's body was already quite weakened and his color was jaundiced/yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I quickly examined all the orifices and found them reaching the bone from all parts of the leg.
And then I took a knife and incised over one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy and corrupted and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted so that it had penetrated into the marrow.
And then I scraped what was uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone for me. (Alternatively: And then I scraped the exposed and prepared part of the bone that was uncovered for me.)
And I thought that the corruption, which I excised and scraped, was not in the bone, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied a restorative treatment to the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it did not heal/consolidate.
And then I reiterated and uncovered the bone a second time, on top of the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I scraped away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I endeavoured/tried hard to treat and restore it, but it was not restored/healed.
And then I uncovered it again and I didn't stop incising the bone little by little, piece by piece, and I forced its healing, but it was not healed until I had incised around a palm's width of it, and I extracted that with its marrow, and then I treated that with medicines, and it healed/closed easily and was cured.
And there was no need for repetition in its operation and cutting except for the disposition of its fragility and because of its low tolerance and fear of death, for he suffered at all times from an excess of evacuation and a bad syncope/fainting.
And thus he was healed by a complete healing, and there is no hard flesh in place of bone; and his bodily disposition became good and his strength returned; and he goes about his business and suffers no adverse effects in walking nor harm in any matter.
But if there is a protruding bone in the body already broken/fractured, then you must cut it in this way: take a bandage and tie it tightly at the extremity of that protruding bone, and order someone to pull it upwards; and place a woolen bandage thicker than the first bandage; and then tie it over the flesh that is below the bone, and extend its end so that you draw the flesh to the lower parts, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you want to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board suitably under the bone, for when you do this the saw is prevented from cutting the healthy flesh.
And the sawing must be done over the corrupted place little by little, for if there is corruption in the cavity of the bone and it does not appear to the senses, you will be obliged to saw it again.
And if the bone is corrupted and not protruding, rather one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh completely on all sides; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is located until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And the sawing should be done a little at a time, according to the location of the corruption, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is connected to the other part.
And if the corruption lies in the area between two joints, then there is no set rule, but rather ingenuity is required. (Implies the surgeon must use their judgement/skill).
And if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you, and that you open it, and clean it according to every means possible to you, and with every bit of ingenuity you can muster, so long as you do not encounter a vein or nerve.
And know that the incisions and the saws for incising these bones are numerous according to the location of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their thinness and their size, both large and small, and their hardness and their porousness; therefore, it is necessary that you prepare for each type of operation the instrument appropriate to that operation.
And know that this operation instructs you about the kind of instrument you should use; when you have great study and long experience and knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience with many kinds of illnesses, for then you will have for yourself that which is fitting, the instruments for every disease.
And I am teaching you, at the end of this chapter, the number of instruments that you will set out simply, with which you will proceed to your goal, which you will test on others. The shape of a saw:

On the incision/cutting of extremities and the sawing of the bones.
The extremities may rot either due to an internal cause or an external cause, or when you have treated that corruption/infection by applying those medicines and the treatment is not effective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then it is necessary that you incise/cut that limb up to the point where the corruption reaches, so that the patient may escape death or a worse affliction/damage of the limb through this.
And the sign of the one to whom this happens is that the limb becomes black as if burned by fire, or if near the blackness the rot spreads within the limb, consuming it and spreading throughout the body; and then attend to it with an incision/cut.
And similarly, if the cause of the corruption/infection is a bite or sting from some venomous creatures such as a sea scorpion, viper, or a spider (rutela - likely a type of venomous spider) and similar creatures.
And if the corruption/infection or bite is at the tip of the finger, then you should incise/cut the finger and not give space for the corruption to spread into the hand.
And similarly if it happens to the hand, then you should incise/cut it at the wrist joint, and not give space for the corruption/infection to spread into the forearm.
And if it affects that (the forearm), then you should incise/cut the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption spreads further and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is likely; and to administer other treatment beyond this point is a waste of effort/resources (literally: quantity of virtue/power).
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption affects a toe, then you should incise/cut it back to one of the ankle bones/joints.
And if it takes hold in the arch of the foot, then you should incise/cut the whole foot. But if it progresses into the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no remedy but resignation, and leaving the patient to death.
And the manner of incising the limb or stitching it is that you tighten a ligature on the part below which you want to cut, and tighten another ligature above the location; and have an assistant stretch a ligature on the lower part, and have the assistant stretch another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures with a flat spatula until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or stitched.
And it is necessary that linen cloth be placed around it on all sides so that it doesn't touch the area itself, and so that further injury and a hot abscess don't afflict the patient.
But if there is a flow of blood when you operate, then you cauterize the affected area carefully, or place on it some powders that restrict the blood; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a bandage in a suitable place, and care for it until it is healed.
And I will recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, after seeing the treatment go to the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome with pain and burning, and he himself cut off that foot at the joint, and was healed.
For a long time he had the same corruption in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I endeavoured to drive back that excess with what I applied on his hand, of the medicines, after cleansing his body; and the excess was not driven back and began to spread to the other finger until it began in the hand.
And he asked me to cut off his hand, and I did not want to do this, hoping for the reduction of that excess, and more because I feared his death following the cutting off of his hand, for the patient's constitution was declining.
And after he despaired of me, he returned to his land and it reached me that that man prepared himself and cut off his entire hand, and was healed.
And I have not added this account except so that it may be helpful concerning what befalls this illness, and that it may be a sign by which it is signified and according to which one should act.
On the treatment of sinuses and the method of administering enemas with medicines.
