When an abscess occurs in some fleshy member and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus, and then it ruptures and is perforated, and the pus comes out of it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is above it is like thinned cloth, and it has not reached a further state of decay, and it does not affect the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and is not called a fistula.
But however, if the space is prolonged until the decay presses upon some member, then it is called a fistula and not zukem (possibly an ulcer or similar affliction).
And the treatment for it is that you incise that skin entirely as it is rolled up, especially when it is thinned and has become like cloth, and take care that it doesn't adhere to the site due to its decay/infection.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and that that decay/infection will not have spread further, and on the surface the skin looks healthy, then you treat that with an enema/clyster.
And consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the discharge that comes from it has a foul odor, then administer an enema with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, sour wine, and ginger in equal parts, and they are put together in a vessel, and cooked over fire until the medicine becomes red and reaches the thickness of honey.
And then take of it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and administer an enema with that into the sinus, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the duration of two hours.
And then extract it by pressing/squeezing; and do this for as many days as necessary until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is treated with an enema of ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and you should take the ash of vines, and pour water over it; and then strain it and administer it as an enema until you are confident that the sinus has then been cleaned.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the ash water, then administer an enema with water and honey and ground ginger mixed in, or administer an enema with wine and honey mixed, because it is a property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes the sinus contract, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this a few times and you are sure that the sinus has been removed, then administer an enema with that which makes flesh grow in it.
And you should take diachylon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or you can administer an enema with other medicines and ointments that have the properties that I have already described in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow so that the enema cannula cannot be inserted, then you should gradually widen it with a small iron tool, and place lint wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment in it until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is too wide, then you should bring the edges of it together with sutures and leave an opening just large enough for the enema to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the discharge flows is elevated in its upper parts, then dry it in the lowest place that is there so that the discharge flows downwards, because when the discharge is held back in the depth of the sinus, flesh will be generated in it.
And if you cannot dry the sinus around the lowest bone as you wish, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation so that the discharge will flow easily, as is suitable for you, and that no part of the discharge be retained in it.
But [change] the one that is over the opening of the sinus constantly so that what accumulates there from the discharge comes out. And so that you know whether that which flows from it, of the discharge, is much or little, or is healthy [digested], or unhealthy [undigested].
And in this way you examine the area of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And when your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter that is released upon the opening of the sinus is sticking, or is close to sticking, then you may loosen it on the second or third day, and when you loosen the wound, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if a subtle/thin virus comes out of the sinus near the area, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is near, because there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are those in whom the opposite is true.
And if for many days undigested discharge has come out of the sinus, then know that the sinus is not closing/healing; and if the closure of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you adjust by drying the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dried thus as diachylon ointment, when a surplus of the addition of wax is included in it.
And the last thing with which it is treated is that you take diachylon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and old wine equal in quantity to it is poured over it, and then you mix that with it, and use that.
And it acts similarly, when there is no discharge, diachylon ointment, honey that is cooked until it is thickened, and use that; or that you take myrrh and aloe and incense, and grind everything and sprinkle it on the honey when it is on the fire, and then spread this on cloth and tighten it over the sinus; or spread it with the honey which you have cooked until it is thickened, and sprinkle it on cloth and tighten it.
And sometimes long birthwort and roots of lily of the valley are administered with it (and it is lily of the valley) and orris-root powder and centaury, and use these simples either by themselves or mixed, grind these and sieve and sprinkle on the honey which I have mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is deep within it, and already makes an inquest into the bones or into the other hard members or to the ligaments; then you treat it with fistula treatment, as is said above.
On the treatment of panaritium (whitlow) and ingrown nail and addition of finger incision and cutting.
A panaritium is an abscess which arises under the nail of the thumb or the big toe, and easily arises on the other fingers or toes, and when it is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, or it festers from a hot abscess, or is corrupted, and it emits discharge until it corrodes the roots of the nail, and easily corrupts it entirely, and easily spreads its corruption to the bone until it has a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger or toe is damaged, and its color becomes dark.
And so when you have that with what we have said in the division and do not complete your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the excess that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound immediately, because a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions (edges) of the nail now compress the flesh on the inner parts, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin sliver under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and lift it up and incise that flesh with ease, and place upon what remains of the flesh corrosive burning medications until it is all removed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for it will never be healed while the corrupted bone remains there.
And if you see the corrosion and the corruption spreading into the finger, then you should incise it at one of the joints according to the aforesaid procedure, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers a blow or abrasion, and severe pain arises in it, then you must first phlebotomize the patient, and then immediately lance the nail with a sharp knife with a transverse cut from top to bottom, and be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient severe pain, and it will also lead to the growth of excess proud flesh in the area; and then treat the place with something that reduces pain.
But an extra finger, which is born on the hands of some men, very easily becomes fleshy, and very easily bones grow in it, or very easily a nail grows in it.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or they originate at some of the joints of the fingers. But the one that is born at some joint of the finger is not bad, and the one that is born at the roots of the joints is very easily made bad/infected.  (Or perhaps, "easily becomes worse.")
And of those that are born fleshy, the removal is easy, and that is that you cut it off at its base with a broad knife.
But of the one that is born at the root of the joint, the treatment is difficult; and so refrain from cutting it off.
But in the one that is born on the finger near the joints, it is necessary that you first cut the flesh around it with a circular incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
But [regarding] consolidation, which occurs to the fingers amongst themselves many times, either by a birth defect of the man who is born thus, or by consolidation of a wound or by burning with fire, or similar things, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not consolidated/joined together easily/prematurely, and separate them, or place between them a thin sheet of lead until it is healed as it should be.
And similarly if consolidation/joining occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation/joining as is prepared and appropriate to the shape of the limb.
Regarding the incision of varicose veins, these are varices (varicose veins).
Varices are varicose veins, and they are convoluted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body, and their most frequent occurrence is in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile sometimes with strong expulsive medicine, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But their treatment with iron (surgical instruments) is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be lifted and extracted completely.
The incision of it (the varicose vein) is according to this method: first foment the leg with hot water until the thick blood, which is foul, is dissolved from the leg, and then constrict the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one place, or in two, or in three with a wide incision, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you want and see fit has come out, and which the patient's strength will tolerate.
And then bandage it (the leg), and order him (the patient) to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy (blood-letting) when the veins are full and the patient notices it.
But their elevation (vein removal) is according to this method: you who shave the patient's leg if there is a lot of hair on it, and then put him in a bath and foment his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are swollen; or let him be exerted by strong exercise, if a bath is not available to you, until the limb is heated.
And then cut the skin on the right of the vein by an incision lengthwise or behind it below the knee, or at the lowest part of it, below the heel.
And then open the skin with hooks and scrape the vein all around until it appears to the touch, and it is from its appearance that you see it red declining to black.
And then when you pull back and away from the skin you see it white, like a cord.
And then you put the thread underneath it until it is lifted and pulled away from the skin, and suspend it with a hook or thread.
And then make another cut near that cut the space of three fingers, and then scrape the skin of the part above the vein until it appears.
And then lift it and cut it in the second cut along the length of the cut, then pull it out and lift it until it comes out through the second cut, and then pull it out of the cut that is above it.
And do this until you pull it out of the cut that is highest or most above all the cuts, until it is all out; and then cut it.
And if it doesn't obey you in pulling and lifting, then insert a needle with strong, doubled thread and tie it and pull it out, or insert the thread under it and turn your hand in every direction until it is pulled out. And be careful that it is not cut; because if it is broken, it is very difficult for you to lift it and harm will have come to the patient because of it.
And when you have completely removed it, then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil and regular oil on the area of the wound, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is contracted, having tortuosity, to which there is wrapping around the parts, and it is not according to straightness as we have said, then it is necessary that you cut above it at every point of tortuosity and at the places of wrapping, and then suspend it with hooks until you lift the whole thing.
And beware in your cutting that you do not incise the vein and that you do not wound it, because it will be difficult for you to lift it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not sharp like the hooks nor is it of pointed extremity so that it does not wound the vein; and it has the substantial flexibility of a lion, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; having superfluous thickness as we have said.
On the extraction of a leg vein. (Or: Concerning the removal of a vein from the leg.)
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries such as in al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India with little moisture and in which neither grass nor tree grows.
And it is very easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its formation is from putrefaction that comes under the skin, like how serpents and worms and parasites and maggots arise within the body, generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the approaching arrival/development of this vein is that vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and then the area blisters; and then the vein begins to emerge from the location of that blister, which looks like a plant root.
And then when the end of it appears, then you must wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachma to two drachmas, and tie it and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
And as many times as something comes out of it to the outside parts, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if it is very long/extended, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise its root until it has come out, because if you incise it, it retracts and goes into the flesh and causes an abscess and rotting in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is thrown out from the behind/base of it and no part of it remains in the body.
And of this vein, there has come out in some men what is in its length five palms and fingers, and it has even happened to me that one came out of twenty palms.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then insert the root into its hole and perforate it with a long hole through the body, until everything that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices occur in it, and a branch comes out of each orifice.
And so treat it as we have said in the division and with what is said above.
On the incision over worms generated under the skin, and the illness is called bovine (or "of cattle").
This disease is called in some lands among us "bovine disease," because it often happens to oxen, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the whole body up and down, and it is revealed to the senses by its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by putrefaction of certain humors just as worms and snakes and earthworms and intestinal worms occur in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, because when it travels in the body and rises to the head, and reaches the eye, then very quickly it opens into it and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
And so when you want to cure this and extract it, then it cannot be done except by following its movement and the appearance of it to the senses, and then it is necessary that you squeeze what is above it and what is below it with a strong tying and constriction, and then cut over it and extract it.
And if it is deep in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire over the place until it dies, and the greatest danger that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place just as we have said.
But if you see it having reached the head near the eye, then squeeze above the eyebrow with strong pressure, and then cut over it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient uses purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and refrains from foods generating putrefaction.
On cutting/incision for the sickness/disease of nafir.  (Nafir is likely a specific disease or condition.  It's unknown to me, and requires further research to identify).
This disease is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen it according to what I will recount to you. I was called to a sick woman in a town, and uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the cephalic vein of the arm, and after about an hour, I saw that swelling move as a worm climbs upward, but more lightly than could be, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and settled in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran through the other side of the body until it arrived at the other arm.
And then she recounted to me that it revolved through her entire body, as I saw; and I was very astonished by the moving of it and the speed from limb to limb. And I had already seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, but I have seen many who find that pain is transferred from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes like in the woman. / But I did not see it so clearly as in the woman.
And I did not think it to be anything but this, because the woman was old, with a thin body and prominent veins, and in this way the moving wind appeared to the senses.  / ... and thus the movement of this flatulence became apparent.
And it cannot appear according to this returning treatment in those who are overweight and have moist bodies with hidden veins. / ...in those who are of delicate constitution and have a moist body with concealed veins.
And when you want to treat him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on it and then release the pressure gently and dry over it until that compressed wind has escaped, and cauterize the area.
But if you don't see it with your eyes, then you treat him with shaking of the body and with that which expels winds, and purge these with fetid pills and with pills of aloe and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows. /  Concerning the removal of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the locations of the bodies into which they fall, for their diversity is that of those that have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, that is, solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have barbs; and of those that have shafts/fletchings.
And of the signs of the brain, it is when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and has exited from the skull which is above the brain, for there occurs through it sudden vehemence, and blindness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and swelling and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of understanding and vomiting of bile, and speech is very quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and a white humidity resembling dust comes out of the place of the wound, and it appears similar to the serosity of flesh.
And so if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the treatment and from the extraction of the arrow, unless it is superficial/external.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and it feels with it as if it were stuck in a hard object, and not a hollow object; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood runs from the wound, and coldness of the extremities follows, and a cold sweat, and syncope/fainting; and then know that death descends without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are a frothy/spumous emission/coughing of blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed/suppurating, and the color of the patient is changed, and he exhales a great high breath, and seeks the drawing in of cold air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm, which is in the chest, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and the breath is made large with intense pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs can be moved/are usable/function.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the belly/abdomen, and is stuck in it, some tissue comes out of the wound, or the omentum, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope in the healing of him by extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes out, and some matter proceeds from him to the outer parts, and very quickly there is pain upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other limbs such as the face, the neck, and the throat/gullet, and the shoulders and the armpit, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the thigh, and the leg, and their similar limbs, are saved according to their general disposition when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may learn from this about your treatment/practice.
Which case is this:  for in a man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye at the root of his nose, and I extracted it for him from the other side, below the crease of his ear, and he is healed, and no damage happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into the corner of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I could only grasp a small tip with which it was attached to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a broad iron head which did not have barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill effect happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arab arrow, and it was one which had barbs; and then I made an incision over it between two tendons, and it was already submerged in his throat; and so I worked delicately with it, and then extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and so, after thirty days had passed and no change occurred in his condition, I made an incision over the arrow and grasped it, and he was healed, and no ill effect occurred to him.
And I saw yet another man in whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound closed over it, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the base of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound closed within her, and there was no change in her condition at all, and the woman experienced no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had fallen into his face and the wound closed and the arrow remained, finding no great pain there, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man, one of the king's chiefs, which had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already deviated a little to the right side, and the entire arrow was hidden.
And I was called to treat him about three days after the arrow struck.
And then I found the arrow's wound very narrow; and then I probed it with a thin probe, and I didn't feel it (the arrow); but he felt a pricking sensation below the right ear, and then I realized that that pricking was due to the tip of the arrow.
And I plastered the area with a plaster that has attractive and digestive properties, trusting that the area would form an abscess so that the location of the arrow would become apparent to me, because I dried it (the wound) with that.
And no significant accident occurred in the area that the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound healed in the meantime, and the patient remained without its (the arrow's) extraction for the space of many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine on the wound for many days until it opened, and with the probe I examined it, and found the thin tip of the arrow, which was still attached to the shaft.
And then I continued with the opening of the wound using that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and I spent my time with this for a period of about four months.
And then, after the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and remove it (the arrow), and it did not yield to extraction, then I did not cease to refine and devise upon it with ingenious methods, until one day I succeeded with ingenious methods of grasping it with specially made forceps, the form of which will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I healed that wound.
And some doctors believed that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I restored it and the wound is healed, and the patient is cured with complete health, and no harm came to him in any way.
And I recounted to you the nature of extracting certain arrows so that you may establish this as a rule and guideline regarding what I have not said, because the details of this art and its definitions are not grasped by narration, nor does the book contain them.
And the wise artisan understands much from little; and from what is present, what is absent; and finds new operations and new instruments, according to the nature of the unusual cases of the art when they occur.
And so I say that arrows are not removed from the limbs in which they are embedded except in two ways: either by pulling from the place at which it entered; or by the opposite from the other side (where the arrow point emerged).
And that which is removed through the place where it entered, is an arrow lodged in a fleshy place, and it is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield in this way from the moment you grasp it, then you must leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrefies, and its extraction and removal is made easier.
And similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to you thus, then leave it for some days and attempt to remove it with pulling and movement each day, and it will be removed.
And if it does not yield to you thus after some days, then you must drill around the arrow in that bone on all sides with a fine drill until the space around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body, and concealed from feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with some instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it due to the narrowness of the wound and the length of the probe, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then you should make an incision over the wound until it is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it has barbs with which it is held, then you should free the flesh in which it is lodged on all sides with all the skill that you can; and if you cannot avoid tearing the flesh when breaking or twisting the barbs until it gives way, work on that [freeing the flesh].
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then you should use twisting motions of your hand with forceps on all sides until you make it yield; and use the utmost care so that the arrow is not broken and that the extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it immediately, then you should leave it for a few days until the parts around it decay, and then try again for at that time it will be easier. But if you encounter troublesome bleeding, then you should administer what we have said about its treatment in its own chapter.
And be careful of cutting a vein or nerve, or tendon, and strive with all possible skill to avoid this when removing the arrow; and do this with ease and calmness and slowness and firmness as I have explained to you.
And then have the patient position themselves to match the posture they were in when the arrow struck them, for this is more helpful. But if this isn't possible for you, then you should do what you can using the illustrations.
But for arrows that are shot through the opposite side, or where some part of it already protrudes, or where the tip of the arrow makes a bulge because it has hit the part just under the skin close by and you see this bulge; then you should cut down upon it; and the cut should be of a size to fit the forceps; and then pull it out, for in this way it is made easier to remove.
But if it is stuck in the bone, then you should turn the blade against it until the arrow presses into the bone, and widens the space for itself, and then you can pull it out.
And if not, leave it for some days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still in it, then you push it through.
But if the shaft has fallen out and you want to push it through, then insert, if it is blunt, a hollow instrument so that it goes between the hollow of the instrument and the tail of the arrow and then push it through.
But if the arrow is hollow, then you push it through with a strong instrument inserted into it, because the arrow is made lighter by that.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you must remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow lands in the feet, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the side, and it is close enough that you can touch it with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut down on it, then cut down on it and be careful of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if you need to, and treat it until it is healed.
The way forceps are made with which arrows are pulled:
Let their ends be like a bird's beak that are made like a saw when they grip onto the arrow so that they don't let it go.
And of these, many kinds are made, large, small, and medium, according to the size, smallness of the arrow, and the width of the wound, and the narrowness of the place.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased, and it is pushed with that.
On phlebotomy of the veins. (or  Bloodletting of the veins.)
The veins from which phlebotomy is customarily performed are thirty, of which sixteen are in the head: two pulsatile veins near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins in the two apparent temples; and two veins in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called aspicientz; and the vein located in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi that are in the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip (these are the veins that are called algelic); and two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five: for of these there is the cephalic, and it is on the outer side; and the common people call it "the vein of the head", and the median cubital vein, which is composed of a branch of the basilic and another branch of the cephalic; and the common people call this one "the vein of the whole body"; and the basilic vein, which is located on the inner side, and is called "axillary", and the common people call it "the vein of the abdomen"; and the brachial vein, which is located above the elbow, and is the one in which phlebotomy is performed, and is the one that appears prominently above the pulse; and the salvatella, which is the vein between the ring finger and the little finger, and it has two branches.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one of them is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to these.
The benefit of [phlebotomy of] the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrh, migraine, dizziness/vertigo (sahafati), and chronic malignant ulcers of the head.
And the way to bleed these [veins], as I have explained to you, is as follows: the patient's head should be shaved, and the patient's head should be rubbed on the occiput at the location of both veins with a large cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two [veins] and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places on the head; and you should search for them with your finger, and if you feel their pulse with your finger, bleed there with a lancet.
Then you take the cutting lancet, which is the sharp one, and insert it under the vein into the skin until the phlebotomy reaches the bone. And then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And the length of the incision should be the width of two fingers joined together, or thereabouts; and let out as much blood as you wish.
And then bandage it with compresses and leave it until it is healed.
And the incision of these [veins] and their cauterization has already been discussed at the beginning of the book.
But for the two arterial veins that are at the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy (blood-letting) is for chronic and severe migraine, persistent eye inflammation (ophthalmia), and [cases] of sharp excess fluids effused to the eyes.
And the way to phlebotomize these is as I recounted to you: have the patient strongly bend their neck until the two veins appear visibly to the touch and their pulse can be felt beneath your finger; and then you mark them with ink.
And then you lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the lancet underneath, and raise the vein up; and incise it as you have done with the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to the amount needed.
And then loosen the binding on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, then place cotton and powder on it and tighten it firmly with a bandage, and leave it until it is healed.
And the description of them, and their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has already been given at the beginning of the book.
And so the benefit of phlebotomy of the forehead vein is similar to the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for long-standing illnesses of the face and for migraines, redness, and foul-smelling ulcers.
And the method of his phlebotomy is as I related to you; that is, that the patient tightens his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is called a *fossori* of which this is the form:
Place the pointed tip which is at the end of the *fossori* on that vein and strike it with a hammer or other object in the manner described, and let the blood flow according to the desired quantity; then loosen the bandage on the patient and bind the wound until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet, but it is not necessary that the lancet have a sharp point like other lancets, but rather a broad tip, and phlebotomize it transversely, because the bone is nearby and a thin lancet would very easily break on it.
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the tear ducts of both eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and mattering in the eyes, and for illnesses of the face.
And the method of phlebotomy is that the patient tightens his neck with a bandage, and then you phlebotomize them, standing above his head.
And let the phlebotomy be done somewhat transversely and lengthwise with a small lancet, because there is no flesh in that area; and if the lancet is thin, its tip will be broken very easily.
And then let the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton over them, and bind them for one night, and then loosen them.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and severe heartburn, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that occurs in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the method of its phlebotomy is that the patient tightens his neck and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long thin lancet and insert it in the middle of the tip of the nose vertically, because the vein cannot be felt there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
Be sure to remove your hand with the lancet little by little and let the blood flow according to your need; and then bandage it for one night, because it heals quickly/easily.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the two jugular veins helps with constriction of the breath/shortness of breath and the beginning of leprosy and many diseases and impetigo and bad corrosive ulcers as well as herpes.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on them is that the patient should compress each one at the neck with a bandage/tourniquet; and the practitioner stands above the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a chair/stool.
And then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide lancet little by little; and then draw blood in a moderate amount or according to what you see is needed for it; and then do the same to the other vein.
And then untie the bandage/tourniquet and compress the two veins with a moderate bandage/dressing so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning, because the wound will be healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the Algerian veins [likely a specific vein, perhaps in the arm],  is helpful for small abscesses and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and bad ulcers and fissures, and for bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on these is that you make the patient sit before you, and tighten his neck with a bandage/tourniquet, and then you turn back his lips and look at two veins of which one is on the right of the lip and the second on the left; and the identification of each is by the blackness of each of them, and this is more so because around them are small black veins; and then you incise each one with a lancet.
But if you are in doubt, and you do not know which one it is, then you should make an incision in the larger of them, and the more visible of them.
And do the same for the two veins that are in the upper lips.
But of the two veins that are below the tongue, the benefit is closer to the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for choking/suffocation that are in the throat, for diseases of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the way to phlebotomize them is as follows:  place the patient between your hands facing the sun, lift their tongue, and look beneath the tongue; on one side of it [the underside of the tongue] a vein, and on the other side a vein, and the color of each is tending toward black.
And then phlebotomize each one; and be careful not to move your hand deeper when phlebotomizing them, because arteries are located beneath them.
And your veins that are phlebotomized in the elbow are those in which it is customary to perform phlebotomy on all men, and their phlebotomy is done in two ways, either pricking them with a myrtle-leaf-shaped or olive-leaf-shaped lancet with a subtly sloping point; or cutting with a knife-like lancet, which is called alnessil, and this is its form:
Let its width be as you see, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, protruding, apparent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser width and with a more subtle point, and it is suitable for phlebotomy of thin veins that contain thin, clear blood.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilic vein, which is one of three veins: for it draws out blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and from that which follows [affects?] the feet and belly.
And it is appropriate for the phlebotomist, when performing phlebotomy on this vein, that they take great care and be extra cautious with it, for there is an artery beneath it, and if they err and are imprecise in the puncture of the phlebotomy, they will incise that artery and cause a flow of blood, therefore it is necessary that they not phlebotomize this [vein] with a pointed lancet, but rather with a cutting one like a small knife/lancet.
And if the basilic vein does not appear manifestly, then you must leave it and move to another, or look for one of its branches; or phlebotomize instead at the end of the arm, for there it is manifest; and lance it with a knife-like lancet as we have said.
But if you wish to perform phlebotomy in the same place [presumably, the basilic vein area], then before tying the arm you should touch the area until you find the location of the pulse, and then mark it with ink; and then tie the arm and lance the vein with a concave cut using a knife-like lancet as we have said; and be careful that the incision does not fall within any space near the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the bandage, swelling in the place you have marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid it.
And if you see the blood flow in the phlebotomy like the urine of a child, and the blood is a thin red, then know that it is blood from the artery; and then cover and place your finger over it for a long time; and then remove your finger, and if the blood has stopped, for many times it stops, then you bind the arm and leave it.
And protect the patient from moving [the affected limb], and let him be cautious and not eat too much for some days until he is healed.
But if the blood has not stopped, and you see that you have no medicine at hand for this, then you should incise the artery, if it seems appropriate to you; because its end is constricted, and the blood flow is stopped; or you can take bark of pistachio, and grind it, and take half of it and press it on the area of the vein with tight bandaging and with powders until the next day.
And then if the blood flow has stopped, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which was mentioned before in the section on powders that stop the flow of blood.
And stopping the bleeding is difficult in many situations because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of binding the arm.
But from the dark vein, phlebotomy (blood-letting) is beneficial, which draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is made up of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we have said.
And the bloodletter should be very careful during phlebotomy of this vein, because beneath it there is a nerve; and therefore if he errs in the puncture of the phlebotomy and hits the nerve, numbness will occur, and its healing will be difficult, and very easily it will not be completely healed. And this nerve is often apparent to the touch; and if it is hidden to some men and is subtle and is not visible, then you should perform the phlebotomy with which you let blood by the method of lancing, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
And if the vein is between two nerves, then you should cut the vein lengthwise.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and provides relief for diseases of the eyes.
And it is fitting in this particular case, if you wish to phlebotomize, to puncture with an olive-shaped lancet or a myrtle-shaped lancet; for it is the safest of all the veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
But it very easily swells up in some men, when it is not phlebotomized on the first incision, but this swelling does no harm.
The first of these is that you must know that phlebotomy is not administered except for the preservation of health and for the hope and precaution of the occurrence of diseases, and that phlebotomy should be done in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, median cubital and basilic; and that phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of spring, before breakfast in spring and autumn, and in winter close to mealtime, when signs of repletion appear.
And phlebotomy should be performed on the third day or the first after three hours of the day have passed.
But phlebotomy can be administered, when necessity and appropriateness call for it, either by night or by day, at any hour and at any time.
And a child should not be phlebotomized until they are fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month until the seventh, and the elderly who have already passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized.
And when anyone wants to be phlebotomized in any way, it is necessary to cleanse their bowels before phlebotomy with a light enema, if they have much retained excrement in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities from the cleansed bowels during phlebotomy, and so that they do not harm the principal members.
And patients with nausea, vomiting, or indigestion should not be phlebotomized, nor those recovering from illness or drunkenness until they have recovered, nor those ill with dropsy; and further, phlebotomy should be avoided near the time of colic, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, work, exercise, fasting, vigils, and any cause that weakens the body, be it physical or mental.
And consider beforehand the thinning of humors, if the blood is thick, with foods and drinks, and with medicines if this is possible.
And then enter a bath if nothing prohibits it, or exercise in some way so that the blood is thinned.
And perform his phlebotomy at the end of the day, that is, around the hour of terce (mid-morning, approximately 9 a.m.), as we have said.
And ensure that the patient's foot is protected on that day from all harmful mental events, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and from all physical events, such as excessive work and exertion, and sexual intercourse, and things similar to them.
And in his chamber, ensure the presence of things commonly used by men for aroma and fragrance, with balms and similar things according to the extent of possibility.
And the phlebotomist should sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one who is to be bled.
And then he should extend his (the patient's) arm, and the phlebotomist should rub it with his hand two or three times; and then tighten the ligature with a strap and wrap it twice.
And the tightness should be moderate, because when the tightness is not even, or the tightness is excessive, it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, it likewise prevents the proper flow of blood.
And after the tightening, it is necessary that the one who is to be bled rub his hands together until the veins are swollen, and become apparent to the touch.
And then the phlebotomist should anoint his lancet with a little old oil (specifically); and then place the index finger of his left hand on the vein, below the place where he intends to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein moves a little, and the incision is precise.  For there are veins which you find like a cord fleeing from the lancet, and these are full of air, and when you place the lancet on them, they contract away from the lancet, and the phlebotomist is deceived; and the lancet does not open the vein, and if it does, the opening is narrow.
And therefore, it is necessary that the phlebotomist be steady and attentive to all these things.
And then insert the lancet, and if the vein is opened in one stroke, then it is good; and if not, you repeat this a second time a little below that spot, or above it quickly, if the area is not infected/inflamed. (apostemat could mean either abscessed or just generally inflamed/infected)
And if the area is infected/inflamed, or the patient is afraid, then you leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, for it will very easily attract hot infection/inflammation, and do not let him enter a bath.
And then repeat the bloodletting if it is needed/desired. (ama - could mean loves/desires/needs - depending on the exact nuance intended, "if it is necessary" might be better).
And if the opening is small and thin blood flows, and you fear that it will not yield the quantity of blood that you want, then you repeat the phlebotomy in that hole following straightness and adjusting the opening a little, and do this quickly before the area is infected/inflamed, for the area is easily infected/inflamed in many people by a small opening.
But if you see it already infected/inflamed, then do not repeat on it, for it will not help you at all; and place on it some split/cracked olive, for it lightens/relieves the body of blood. (This probably refers to a poultice or some other application of olives). And it is better in this place than [plain] oil and other oils.
And similarly administer cracked/split olive in all phlebotomy with which you bleed veins because of the prohibition of thick blood, and do this [also with] theriac, al-fadoq (possibly a type of plaster/poultice), and sagapenum (a gum resin) when one of these is placed on the area, because the blood is thinned and resolved when it is thick.
And if in the place of the phlebotomy a large swelling/abscess is engendered, for it happens many times, and especially to the one who has not been bled except that one time, or in the opening of a small vein, then you cover and place on the area a sponge soaked in water and salt, slightly warmed, and tighten it for an hour, for it is dissolved/resolved.
And it is necessary to do this near the exit of the blood of that vein with its completion/full draining or of another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains in the place for some days, do not fear this; and if you wish, place on that some aloe and dissolved myrrh, or some juice of wild mint or calamint or similar.
And many times swellings and protrusions occur because of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And then place your hand upon it, and if then you find softness beneath your compression upon it, then that swelling is bad and dangerous; and therefore be careful that you place upon it something of what we have said, for blood will flow very easily from the artery.
And make a plaster with something that is styptic, so that the place is hardened, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you draw blood from every man according to the amount of his strength and according to the dominant color over the blood as it appears.
For if the blood is black, then you let it out until it is red; and similarly if you see it thick, then you let it out until it is thinned; and similarly if it is sharp (thin/acrid), until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full and strong and needs bloodletting at once, that the phlebotomy of the vein be amplified and the lancet be wide.
And in one who is frail, the opposite should be done, and it is necessary to extract it in several instances, and that the opening be narrow/small.
And the best that is administered in phlebotomy of a vein is that the incision be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safe from blood flow and nerve incision/damage, and is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy along the width and length (of the vein).
And for him who is accustomed to faint during phlebotomy, and you must treat this person before the phlebotomy with a little bread infused in pomegranate wine or warm simple oxymel (vinegar and honey); and extract his blood in three or four instances/stages.
And if it is coldness of complexion/constitution, then before the phlebotomy take bread infused in syrup of myrrh or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger and the like, or in aromatized and very fragrant syrup.
And if fainting occurs during the phlebotomy, and the cause is the loss of a lot of blood, then it is necessary that he drink meat broth and very fragrant subtle syrup; and use aromatics with rosewater, and his feet should be bathed with it.
And make use of the other measures that we have mentioned in the section in the chapter on syncope due to evacuation.
And he who wishes to bandage his arm and dissolve/reduce his blood a second time, must, if the phlebotomy is for a large evacuation, and his constitution is cold, evacuate the blood little by little, that is, according to the strength of his constitution, on consecutive days.
But he who wants to bandage his arm, and let his blood [flow] a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do this about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy.
But he who wants to draw the blood from his body away from the part to which it flows, must be bandaged on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much heated blood and is acute and causes fever, must have its blood let all at once and a large quantity should be drawn and the opening enlarged until syncope occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and you should place your hand on his pulse on the side away from the bleeding, so that death does not occur instead of syncope, because this happens many times when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not appropriate when you want to untie the arm and evacuate the blood a second time, since the opening of the vein has already closed and the outflow of blood is difficult, that you press upon it with vehemence and that which is bent is extended forcefully, for this is very bad.
Leave it alone until it is phlebotomized a second time; or remove what is coagulated from the blood at the opening of the vein with the sharpness of the lancet; or place on it some al-faruq theriac, or dragon's blood, and compress it lightly until the blood flows out.
And if the vein is infected/abscessed, then leave it alone and do not touch it until the infection/abscess has subsided.
And if necessity compels you to evacuate the blood a second time, and there is no other option, then you bleed the patient on that area or in the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "rope of the arm" is phlebotomized in place of the cephalic or basilic vein, when they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on him is that the patient puts his hand in warm water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly.
And then it should be tightened above it with a bandage with moderate pressure; and then phlebotomize according to the small transversal, not latitudinally nor longitudinally.
And let the opening be wide and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize it over the joint of the hand a little.
And if the flow of blood is stopped, then you put the hand back in the warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve the required amount.
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from putting the hand back in the warm water, although it is better to place the blood vessel in warm water in winter time.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because there is no pulsating vein, nor nerve, underneath it.
And phlebotomy of the cephalic vein of the right hand is beneficial for diseases of the liver.
And the proper way to perform phlebotomy is that you tighten the hand with a bandage or with your hand, after you have placed it in warm water, until the vein is swollen and clearly visible; then phlebotomize it transversely; and if you cut it completely, this will not harm anything.
And be careful not to damage your hand with the phlebotomy, because underneath there are the nerves of the fingers, bare of flesh.
And then return the hand to the warm water and let the blood flow into it, because if you don't return it to the warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and its flow will be blocked.
And when the blood has been extracted according to the necessary amount, then you put oil and salt on the vein, so that it is not closed up too quickly; and similarly you must do with any narrow branch (vein).
But the benefit of phlebotomy in the left hand is that it is suitable for diseases of the spleen.
And do likewise in his phlebotomy as you have done in the other.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein applies to diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the uterus, and retention of menses, and diseases of the kidneys and ulcers of the thighs and old legs, and similar ailments.
And the procedure for his phlebotomy is that the patient puts his foot in hot water and rubs it until the veins appear well; and tie above the ankle with a strap; and the place of the vein to be cut appears above the said thumb, and from it are branched many small veins on the surface of the foot.
And then phlebotomize the largest branch either below the leg above the heel or below its confluence, because it is better and safer.
And if you phlebotomize it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot, and position your lancet with which you phlebotomize across, as if you intended to incise it.
And let the phlebotomy be ample/generous. / And let the bleeding be substantial.
But if the flow of blood is restricted; then you immerse his foot in the hot water again and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated/drained.
And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then repeat with the lancet a little above, because the location is safe; and do not fear to harm him, but beware of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the same with the saphenous vein of the other foot.
And concerning the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is behind the heel below the ankle on the outer side. / And as for the sciatic vein, the place, as we have said, is behind the heel bone beneath the ankle of the left leg.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy (bloodletting from it) is for hip pain when caused by hot blood.  / And the benefit of phlebotomy there is for pain in the hip when it is caused by hot blood.
And the proper way to perform phlebotomy on it is that the patient enter a bath, and quickly and tightly bind his thigh on the side of the hip down to above the heel with a long, thin bandage, for it (the vein) appears only by doing this.
And then when it appears, you then phlebotomize it according to whatever method is possible for you and across (transversely), and that is better; or incise it by making an incision or cut it by sectioning, because the area is safe. / And when it appears, you should then perform phlebotomy on it by any method you can, preferably transversely; or make an incision, or sever it completely, because that location is safe.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it does not appear clearly to the touch, then phlebotomize a branch of it, which is the one that appears on the top of the foot near the little toe and the ring toe. / And it is very hidden in many people; and if you find it, but it is not readily apparent to the touch, then you should bleed a branch of it, which is the one that appears on the top of the foot towards the little and ring toes.
And beware of the nerves, and let flow the quantity of blood that you desire.
And then loosen the bandage and place cotton over the site of the phlebotomy and compress the area, for it is easily healed.
Concerning cupping/venesection and the properties of their use. /  On cupping therapy and the characteristics of its use. (Ventosacio refers specifically to a cupping procedure, not just general venesection, though bloodletting is often a component).
Cupping glasses are made of horn, or brass, or glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification (dry cupping).
And this cupping that is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping that is administered with scarification and blood extraction, there are fourteen locations on the body, one of which where cups are placed is on the inion (back of the head), and it is the rear part of the head; and the middle location of it [the head?] between the two sides of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the mandible, further below the mouth; and cupping of the two shoulder blades; and cupping on the alohoster, and that is above the end of the tailbone (coccyx); and cupping of the two armpits, which are below the two arms; and cupping of the two ankles; and cupping of the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins dispersed in the flesh, and because of this, the strength does not fall through them, as is the case with phlebotomy (venesection); nor should cupping be done, with fire or without fire, in any illness caused by repletion (excess) until the whole body has been evacuated.
But if necessity compels cupping because of illness or for maintenance, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at its end, and in the middle of it and at whatever time it may be.
And so when you see any of these signs, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we recommend that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping on the nape of the neck is that it helps with heaviness in the head and that which affects the eyes; but it is necessary that this be near a time of complete evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted for someone of a cold complexion, or someone who has a catarrh, to use it, because it causes them great harm.
And for this reason, old people, and those whose heads suffer from cold ailments, should not use it.
But for those on whom it is continuously used, it causes forgetfulness; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to pause his hand a little with the cup at the lower parts, for fear of causing forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkali is in the place of phlebotomy of the median cubital vein and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, constriction of the breath, damage to the respiratory system, coughs, and fullness/plethora.
And the alkali cup should be applied lightly, because if it is placed heavily, it generates weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (possibly a type of cupping material) is beneficial for accidental pains in the head, ophthalmia (eye inflammation), migraine, suffocation, and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it replaces phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand with the scarification tool lest he incise an artery and cause excessive bleeding.
And cupping under the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar oral diseases; and it replaces phlebotomy of the veins (aliheric) that are in the two lips.
But cupping done over the two shoulder blades is beneficial for headache (caphatan), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on both sides within the armpits effectively replaces phlebotomy of three veins, namely the basilic, median cubital (fusca), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and those smaller veins from larger ones until the drawing reaches the three main veins.
And instruct the cupper not to go too deep in the scarification, because the area [the armpit] is lacking in flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass which is used on the hips, and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcerations.
And you should instruct the cupper to use a large cupping glass, and that it be made of bronze, because the location requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses break easily and it should be scarified with a scarificator.
But cupping glasses on the legs lessen manifest plethora, because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old aches of the kidneys and womb, and of the bladder; and provoke menstruation, and benefit buboes and swollen glands; and they take the place of phlebotomy of two saphenous veins, thus relieving them, and of two ankle veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And the relief from cupping glasses on the groin is similar to the relief from cupping glasses on the two legs.
And the proper way to apply the cupping glasses is to place the cupping glass initially empty and draw with moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the location with a gradual arrival. And do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of the blood appears.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction little by little, and then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For a person who is of tender flesh with sparse pores, it is necessary that you scarify him only once and no more, so that the place does not ulcerate.
And order the cupper to widen the scarification and deepen it a little, and moderate the suction gently.
And if there is thickness in the blood, then you must scarify twice, because on the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its wateriness. But on the second, to the careful examination acquired from the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very polluted, then let scarification be done a third time so that it attains the rest (of the polluted blood).
And lastly, when we want to extract the blood little by little, or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the limit of scarification is only as deep as the skin.
And if the person whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and their pores are narrow, it is necessary that the locations for the cupping glasses be anointed with softening, opening, and resolving [ointments]; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or hip seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and things similar to these.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balsam mint oil, or aloe oil, or dill oil, and things similar to them.
But if the person to be cupped has wide pores and tender flesh, then it is necessary to avoid oil, and it is necessary for these people that the locations for cupping, close to the time of cupping, be washed with rosewater or with cold nightshade water, or with hip water, or with borage water, and things similar to these.
And for the one whose blood has much humidity, wash their cupping locations with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and things similar to these.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping locations with old wine, or with marjoram water (which is called "sansuc"), or with a decoction of dill, or of chamomile, and things similar to them.
And cupping should be avoided before a bath; for those having it, it should be administered about one or two hours after exiting the bath, and they should sleep after the cupping.
What is the necessary regimen for one who is to be cupped or bled before cupping/venesection? (Alternatively: How should one who is to be cupped or bled manage themselves before the procedure?)
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and experiences sharp blood and inflammation, then they should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive with sour wine and lettuces, hemp, julep, and things similar to them.
And include in their meals things like chicken, and the meat of wild birds, and foods seasoned with sour flavors, and similar things.
And for one whose complexion is cold, they should take syrup of marshmallows, or syrup of hemp seeds, and sumac, and a very fragrant medium-dry wine (between old and new); and it is recommended that they partake of little drink and food, and include in their meals chicken, hemp, fish, small birds, and pigeon/dove.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping or bloodletting, the drink be greater than the food (i.e., they drink more than they eat).
And it is necessary to give some men Theriac Al Faruq, or medications of musk, that is nutmeg, or alcelita (a kind of confection) to drink at certain times before cupping or bloodletting or immediately afterwards, so that the principal members are strengthened and the blood is thinned; and they should not take those (medications) warm/heated.
But the cups that are without scarification (dry cupping) are cups that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and for gout, and on the navel, in the areas of the knees, and the hip socket, for these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not sought by these (cups without scarification) to draw blood from the limb, as when we place a cup on the breast in the case of nosebleeds; and we use it (cupping) so that cold, ingrained wind/flatulence is dissolved from the limb, as when we place it on the belly and navel; for it rarefies the limb and warms it and reduces the pain because it dissipates flatulence.
And it (cupping) is also placed on the kidneys when blockage or pus occurs in them, for by the power of suction it very easily opens the blockage and eradicates the pus from its location.
And it does similarly when placed on the liver and spleen because of flatulence enclosed in them.
And these cups are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire (fire cupping).
And sometimes they are administered filled with warm water in diseases of the spleen.
And this is so that the cup may be filled and be larger, that is, with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
The way to make a cup administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have made it; and the amount of the grip be half a palm; and let it be on its side, in the place that is on the circumference, the middle of it, the hole should be small according to the size that a needle will enter it.
Let it be made of red or yellow brass, with thick, smooth, even and polished edges so that it does not harm the limb where it is placed.
And let there be in the middle of it a transverse rod of brass or iron, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cup be much larger than the one we have described; and somewhat smaller than that according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cups for children and weakened individuals are different than the cups for young people and those with large bodies.
And the way to place this cup with fire on the limb is to suitably extend the wick with a wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place it on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cup, so that the fire is lit around the lower part so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be over the hole that we have mentioned, until, when we are holding the cups and do not need to uncover the hole and let the vapor escape through it, then the cup will be immediately released.
And then light the wick according to the usual manner, and repeat it if you need to.
But in a cup that is administered in the disease of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod nor hole, it is only administered when it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb just so.
And this is its form: And this cup, the larger it is, the better, so that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered on many limbs but on those where the placement of cups is not possible, due to their small size, such as the wrist and gums and similar areas; or on a limb devoid of flesh, such as the finger and the nose and their like.
And the appropriate way to use them is that if you have found leeches that are in the fresh waters of clean springs...
And then they should be left for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies; and then the body should be evacuated initially by phlebotomy (blood-letting) and with cups; and then the patient's limb should be rubbed until it reddens; and then the leeches should be placed upon it.
And when they are full, they will fall off.
And if suction of the location with a cupping glass is possible, it is more helpful afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with sour wine and then with hot water; and let it be rubbed and pressed.
And if the flow of blood from the body is prolonged after the application of leeches, and it oozes, then you should soak a linen cloth in cold water, and place it over the area until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is profuse, then sprinkle over it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the blood is staunched; or place the halves of cooked broad beans on the area, and leave them there until the beans adhere to the area, and the blood is staunched.
And it is necessary, if repeated application of leeches is required, to change them when possible.
And if the leeches refuse to attach, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick it with a needle in several places until blood comes out; and then apply them, for when they sense blood they will attach immediately.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle on them a little crushed aloe, or salt, or ash, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it concerns the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish and vulgar physicians, and those who have never consulted the books of the Ancients nor read a letter of them, boast about this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of the learned is hidden in our land; and I have not at any time completely forgotten it; and I have not used what follows, except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their meaning, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
And then I applied myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience has passed; after having abbreviated it for you and freed it from the burden of lengthiness; and I have shortened it to be concise; and I have demonstrated it clearly; and I have formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as is done by adding clarification, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summaries and collections on the nature of bone fractures, of those whose premise is necessary, before we begin the discussion of fractured limbs and the different [treatments] of each one. It is fitting, then, that we state in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which the artisan is forced at the beginning and to dwell on their truth; that is to say, you and those of you who are eager to know or learn this noble art [along with] others.
And so I say that when someone experiences a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall, then you should attend to at the beginning with phlebotomy (blood-letting) or purging him, or both together, unless some condition prohibits it, such as weakness of strength, or if it happens to a child, or an old man in decrepitude, or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
And next, let his food consist of cool herbs and the meat of birds and river crabs; and we forbid rich meats and filling himself with foods, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are safe from hot swelling, and if you do not fear an outpouring of matter into the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen, with which he is accustomed.
And so when the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be nourished with nutritious foods of much substance, rich and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and fat meat and things similar to these, and with this regimen the knitting of the fracture is achieved more easily and better.
And know that broken bones when they are in men who are in their first old age (i.e. beginning to age) and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be joined and consolidated according to their nature because of the dryness of their limbs.
But still, nature adds and applies over the broken bone on all sides a certain glue-like substance, in which there is a thickness that adheres to and binds it together until they are glued and bound together, until they reach their full strength and firmness, as with fire at the beginning, until something prevents it from completing its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you provide the patient with foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and richness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the skull; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come to a consideration of every kind of fracture, explained in its own chapter, distinct from the others.
Much diversity of fracture in itself.
