And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there are arched fractures other than those that occur in the ankles; and sometimes a fracture is made along the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the sagittal, and splint bones, and additions proceeding and not proceeding, as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is fracture with wound and disruption in the head, and there is small fissure fracture.
And to each type there is a proper device or torment in its restoration according to what will be discussed distinctly in its place.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is recognized: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and this when you compress it with your hand.
And thus when there is no apparent crookedness in the place nor is it made by your touch when you touch the bone with movement and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture there.
However, it is possible that there may be crookedness or a small fissure, then you should not press it with your hand and with complete compression; rather, place upon it the medicines of which there will be discussion shortly, which are suitable for the place; and then it should be bound with a light bandage.
And know that the place when it is arched and not split in two or otherwise not seriously injured, such as when any part is removed from its counterpart, then you must begin immediately to straighten it, lest a hot abscess occur there.
And if a hot abscess occurs there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then adjust it in whatever gentle and ingenious way you can.
And know that its restoration and healing are easier than for a bone in which a serious injury has occurred.
And then bind it as will be discussed later.
But if the bones are seriously injured, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two sides, if it is the hand or the foot; either with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or with one rope and your hand.
And position yourself so that you place the limb evenly according to its natural shape, while the parts of the broken limb are being extended, then you must return those additional pieces gently and in whatever way you can to their places.
And take care, according to your strength, that no pain or injury occurs to the patient because of your work; and you must, according to your ability, apply one bone to its companion according to their best forms.
And it is necessary at that time that you skillfully touch it with your hand, and if you see anything amiss, rectify it and adjust it to the best of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do, who often cause an abscess because of their work, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen this happen because of their work many times.
And the manner of binding the injured limb is what I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and in their greatness and in their shape.
And for those that are small like the arm and the finger and the radius and similar to those, then the wrapping should be of a very fine soft cloth.
But those that are large like the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that the wrapping on them be wide and strong, because the wide bandage joins the large limb and tightens it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish occurs on it.
And then begin your reduction, setting, applying a suitable bandage for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandaging.
And then go with it to the part most above the location of the fracture, and tighten less than you did the bandaging with which you tightened the place of the fracture itself. And then move away with the bandaging from the site of the fracture a little, and loosen the tightness little by little, until you take in a large area around the site.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with bandaging, and then go with the wrappings to the part below the fracture; and your work in the tightening of the bandaging and in the loosening of it should be according to what we have said about the first bandaging above.
And then place between the turns of tow flax or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, don't place anything there.
And then place another bandage on it, and then neatly place freshly made splints on top of these bandages immediately, if there is no hot swelling or abscess in the place, and in that case, apply something which settles the abscess and removes the swelling to it; and leave that for some days; and afterward you may tighten the splints on it.
And let the splints be of half of a smoothed peeled cane, and skillfully formed; or they may be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over that fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint be according to the size of the limb, large or small; and then tighten another bandage over the splints according to your first tightening; and then tie it up neatly with thread as we have said regarding tightening, which is that your tightening should be greater at the location of the fracture itself, and the further away it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of flax; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen through the practice of some fools placing their threads of twisted hemp cords, that is a great error, because the tightening is uneven.
And likewise, thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve what you want by tightening with them, and there should not be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints near the bandaging in those places, then put some tow flax or carded wool underneath them so that this does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, and I will come back to this point in the second chapter with a detailed explanation.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason being that when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it until after five or seven days or more, depending on your certainty regarding the development of a hot abscess/swelling.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients performed restorations, which are placed on fractures and dislocations and sprains.
Method of a common abbreviated plaster for the restoration of a fracture, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, because it is something on which neither heat nor cold comes: and it is that you take mill dust, which is the marrow of the flour that hangs from the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And prepare it as is, only sieve it with egg white, and make the mixture neither thick nor thin; then use it.
Another method of plaster restoring fracture and dislocation and sprain: take of asafoetida, of labdanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and migrach, which is the root of wild pomegranate, of all ten drams; of myrrh and aloe each five drams; of aloe vera twenty drams; let all be ground and sieved and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is heated; then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores broken bones easily, and suits many people.
Another method of plaster which suits fracture and sprain: take migrach (wild pomegranate root) and marshmallow root, each ten drams; of myrrh and of aloe each five drams; of acacia six drams; of Armenian bole twenty drams.
Let all be ground in a mortar with much grinding, and be sieved, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be administered.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and which soothes pain occurring in them and in fissures and fractures: take dirty wool and submerge it in sour wine and cooked oil and place it on the area/location.
But in this plaster there is no power of restoration, of hot abscess and it expels pains properly/effectively.
Another method of plaster that restores broken bone: take leaves of a wild fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; grind it all and make a plaster from them.
A shortened method of plaster administered after the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remainder of an abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile and violet flowers, and orach flour, one part of each; let all be ground and mixed with wine, if the limb is not heated/inflamed.
But if it is heated/inflamed, then mix it with juice of coriander or with water and use it.
Another method of plaster, stronger in resolution than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs after the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and pomegranate rind, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, one equal part of each; let all be ground and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the condition of the limb and the return of its color.
But Hippocrates did not say in his book that anything be placed on the broken limb after its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommended that it be medium in thickness, between thick and thin.
But in Gaul it is seen that things are placed on the broken limb after its restoration, things in which there is desiccation with a little heat, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar things which we have mentioned.
Method of plaster that you will make for injury and pain: take breadcrumbs, wax, shaved human hair or bird feathers, marshmallow, and salt, equal parts; let them be ground and sieved and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the length of time that the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened is as follows: consider if there is no pain or itching and if the broken bone has not moved from its place, then you do not loosen it for many days. And if severe pain or itching or swelling occurs at the site, then you hasten to loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster from it.
And take a linen cloth and a wet sea sponge, and submerge it in warm water, and with that wash the area until the itching stops and the pain is soothed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place upon it clean wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on overnight until you are sure of a warm abscess, and that the swelling of the limb is reduced, and the pain from it is removed.
And then repeat that light binding and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as your first binding, and continue with this until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and the redness and the pain and the swelling and all the symptoms are completely gone, and there is no need for plaster and bandaging, then you repeat the procedure as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the things we have mentioned happen to the limb, then do not loosen it for about three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left for twenty days, depending on what you observe with the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh coagulates over it.
And then tighten the bandaging more than your first bandaging, and increase the richness of the patient's food, as was previously mentioned concerning him/her.
But if you see that the site of the fracture has dried and thinned out more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore bathe it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it; and do this every three days; and loosen the bandaging a little, and through this procedure nourishment will flow to the limb, and it is easily healed.
But what foolish bone-setters do, setting a fractured limb a second time when it wasn't set correctly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is a mistake in their practice, and a great risk, and if it were correct the Ancients would have emphasized this in their books and would have practiced it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, so it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and the blow drives the entire bone into the brain, which is below the bone, just like an axe does in wood, and therefore this type is called a depression fracture ["ascial" likely relates to axe/adze]; or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts the flesh there but the incision does not pass through to the inside of it; and this type of fracture is called a simple incision/superficial wound [literally, "absolute lifting/raising"].
And the damage of these two fractures is either great or small, as is a fracture that is a contusion or bruise; and its cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a rock, and things like them.
And this fracture is either penetrating, which then approaches the membrane which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the damage of these two fractures is either great or small.
And there is a fracture that is a hairline crack in the bone, as fine as a hair, and therefore this type is called a hairline fracture [literally "capillary fracture"].
And there is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow from a stone or similar things, which causes some layer of the bone to go into the inner parts, and a concavity is made in the place, just as happens to copper pots when they are struck and part of them is driven inwards.
And this happens especially in the bodies with damp bones, such as the heads of children.
And there are also all these kinds of fractures [or: And common to all these kinds of fractures are], which are fragments like separated stars/splinters. And I will return with an explanation [or: further discussion] of all this in its proper place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by touching them and by examining them with probes and [by the presence of] damaged flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is recognized because it is uncovered over the bone, and [the bone] is cleaned, and wiped over with a probe, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's condition, and then if you see, because of these conditions, manifest signs indicating danger, such as vomiting of bile, and distension, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and similarly [swelling] of the eyes and redness of them, and similar major symptoms, without doubt he will not be healed.
And if you see no unfavorable symptoms, you may hope for recovery, then you may begin his treatment.
The reason for this is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning of the injury and this is during the winter, then you should attend to the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to the specific situation.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must take care to set the bone before the seventh day, provided that what is under the bone is not corrupted from inflammation, and that the complications that we have mentioned do not occur.
And if the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and it is with small fragments and attrition, then you must excise the comminuted and damaged part, as I will explain to you.
And you should shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone as is customary and possible for you, according to the shape of the wound, and be gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter, after the uncovering of the bone, blood flow or a hot abscess, then you should treat it appropriately.  Fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess has subsided, and you are sure of the cessation of bleeding; then begin with the scraping around the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow chisel, of this shape: [referring to a missing illustration] And then use another chisel, a little wider than the first; and this is its shape: [referring to a missing illustration]
And then use the widest chisel.
And you should have a number of different chisels, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than the others and should be sharpened at their ends, and be made of Indian or hardened steel.
And use them with ease when striking the chisel so that you don't damage the head [of the chisel, presumably] and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you should drill around it before you use the chisels with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are so named because they do not reach from the surface of the skull bone to what is beneath it, because the drill has a round extremity or margin above which is a sharp head resembling a margin or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting that you take a large number of these drills, each one appropriate for the thickness of each bone, until you have for all skulls a [drill whose] end is sharp, and whose length is according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the way of [using] three kinds of drills: The method of drilling around the fractured bone is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; and then move the drill to another spot, placing it between each hole a distance about the thickness of a spoke of a wheel or about that.
And then cut with chisels what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can for lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other means or some instruments which you will have prepared for this, such as tongs and fine forceps.
And you must take every precaution that the chisel does not touch any part of the brain covering (dura mater).
And then when you have circled the perforated bone, and it is separated from the dura mater if it is connected to it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth the roughness of the bone remaining with another instrument similar to a chisel, but it should be thinner and finer than the other chisels.
And if any part of the small, fractured bones remains, then remove it carefully with the instruments you have; and then treat the wound with bandages and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is less invasive, far from fear, which Guy de Chauliac praised and lauded greatly; and this is his explanation:  It is necessary for you in the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most obvious, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the end of this slow and gentle chisel, of which this is the form:
And the lenticular (lens-shaped) part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is on the other two sides extending lengthwise, as you see; so that the lenticular part is placed next to the dura mater with the sharp side cutting into the bone.
And then strike the chisel on one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone gently as it is rotated, and you are certain about the membrane (dura mater) that no accident will happen to it in any way that you fear, even if the physician were the most foolish and ignorant of all men; certainly, even if he were sleeping.
Then if anything remains attached to the bone from the dura mater in any place on the bone, you will cut it away from it with the end of that lenticular chisel, and free it gently, for it is freed without harm and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate to the dura mater, and what is cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and fine splinters remain, then you must scrape that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps that you have prepared in number, of various sizes so that you can use the most appropriate ones in every place and the most suitable ones according to how your operation leads you on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must manage your scraping so that at the beginning you scrape the bone with the widest of those rasps, and then gradually use the finest ones, and don't stop doing this continuously until you get to using the thinnest and finest of them all.
But in each of the other small, hair-like fractures and fine breaks, you must administer care as required to correct it/them.
And it is a topic that is not sufficiently addressed by all those who have studied this art. And stay with what we have shown written about large fractures.
And if the membrane is retained when you scrape the bone, then you must take a cloth according to the size of the wound and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another cloth, folded double or triple, and submerge it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth, and do this as lightly as you can so you don't burden the pericranium; and then apply a broad bandage over that, and do not tighten it except enough to just hold the wound covering in place.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a warm abscess; and then untie it and apply medicines with which excess dryness is removed, such as linseed roots and orris-root powder, and powders of incense and birthwort, and things similar to these.
And you should make a powder of these medicines and sprinkle it on the wound, just as they are dry; and generally, apply any medicine whose property it is to cleanse and not to irritate.
And be careful, according to your ability, that the wound in your care is clean; and that there is no filth of ointment or other [substance] in it; nor should you allow virulence to gather in it; for when it is next to the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and a malignant sickness soon falls upon the patient.
And it happens in the pericranium of the head, when the bone is cleaned of it, and especially when its treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and then if those accidents we have mentioned happen to the patient, you should know that it is deadly without any doubt.
And if the blackness doesn't occur except from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is the effect of that medicine that causes that blackness, then you must take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and smear a cloth with them, and then place it on the pericranium; and then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two uppermost parts of the nose are broken together, or sometimes one of them, because you have two bones there, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken and only suffers bruising, twisting, and curvature.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into his nose, and align that fracture from the inside with your index finger, and with the thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this gently, and be careful not to cause the patient pain through your work.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be adjusted with the end of a probe which is small in thickness.
And if the fracture is in both parts, then you do the same.
And attend to its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should beware of hot swelling/inflammation.  (The word "apostema" generally refers to an abscess or swelling caused by infection.  In this context, it seems to warn against increased inflammation arising from the fracture around a week to ten days later.)
And then insert into the end of the nose a linen cloth wick, if the fracture is in one part, or put in two tubes/plugs, if the fracture is in two parts, and the tube/plug should be thick enough to fill the hole of the nose. (The word "calgua" is likely related to modern words like "calque" and refers to something inserted, perhaps a small tube or plug.)
And some of the ancient healers said that the tubes/plugs should be soaked in butter and changed every day, but this is not my opinion; on the other hand, it is necessary that the wick be soaked in egg white mixed with ground flour; and then leave the tube/plug in until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a tube/plug, a goose feather is inserted into the nose, after it has been wrapped in fine linen cloth; and it may be that you have a more severe nasal fracture and that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this [the goose feather method] isn't necessary for you; if you want, do it this way, and if you want, use tubes/plugs.
And if a hot abscess sometimes occurs, plaster the nose with wax or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon plaster.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you must plaster it externally with sesame flour and incense powder mixed with egg white; and then place fine lint on it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted (splintered), then you must make an incision over them, and extract them with suitable instruments; and then sew up the incision, and treat it with something that promotes flesh growth and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you must treat it with plugs/tubes and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the mandible when it is broken/fractured.
When the mandible below is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, see then if the fracture is only external, and it is not broken in two and curved inwards, then its recognition is easy/straightforward.
And it is necessary then, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and with that, push the bump/protrusion of the inwards fracture easily to the outside, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; gently aligning it with that (other hand/the outside).
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated in two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from both sides along a straight line until its alignment is possible.
And if loosening/displacement of the teeth occurs with the break, then tighten those which you hope will remain with gold or silver or silk thread; and then place a plaster/bandage on the broken mandible, and then place folded cloth over it, and place over the cloth a suitably made large star (shaped bandage), or a piece of leather equal to the length of the mandible; and then tie it on top according to how you have prepared the bandage and as the application requires, until it (the fracture) is healed.
And order the patient to rest and be tranquil, and have him take his food in sips/small amounts.
And if you judge that its shape has been altered in any way, then take care and untie it on the third day; and then correct what has been altered in it (the bandage) and plaster it with powder of melilot, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the plaster/bandage from it, and place soft wool over the plaster.
And then when that plaster is attached and adjusted on it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, and do not untie it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; for many times this fracture is set in three weeks.
And if again a hot abscess/swelling occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its reduction until it is healed, and the abscess/swelling is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered, then you should carefully remove those splinters with what is suitable for this purpose among the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with the lancet according to the extent of your need. (Fleubotomi is likely a variant of phleubotomy - the act of opening a vein to let blood, but seemingly used here for any incision).
And then when you have removed those splinters, and nothing of them remains, then you should sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for this and allow it to heal, until it is cured.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken. (Furcula refers to the "little fork," which is an anatomical term for the clavicle.)
Many times the clavicle is broken in the front part near the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For either it is broken, or separated, or it is separated into two parts without any splinters; and it is easy to restore; or it happens that splinters occur, and it is difficult to restore; or it is the case that the fracture is with a wound.
And the procedure in this case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the patient and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that side.
And then align the fracture with your fingers until its proper form is restored, so that there is neither a small protrusion nor a concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the ball with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards, because it has reached the throat, then it is necessary that the patient lie supine with their neck bent back, and you place a moderately sized cushion on their shoulder muscle.
And the assistant should press the shoulder muscle downwards so that the bone of the clavicle that is near the throat is raised, and then you adjust the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel bone fragments of the clavicle that are loose, then you should make an incision over it and extract those fragments with care/ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision on it with one of the incisors that you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that supports the nape of the neck; and it is an instrument of wood or iron, of which this is the form/shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the cavity/concavity; and its width should be according to your need for it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as long as possible for the operation; it should have two ends as you see, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound, which you have incised when you extracted the bone fragment, is large, and there is risk of a hot abscess/infection, then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess/infection, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound opening/tear.
And if a hot abscess/infection occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it over the area.
And ensure that the splint is not moved from over the broken bone; and therefore you must see the patient every day, so that when the bandaging is loosened or you see that the splint has moved, then you correct it and tighten the bandage.
And then arrange the patient's sleeping position on his neck (meaning, have him sleep somewhat upright), and place a small cushion under his armpit on the side of the injury during the night; so that with this the injured side is raised, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the elevation of the muscle; or else his arm should be tied to his neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless itching or an abscess occurs at the site, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need for it, and adjust the bandaging and restore it and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and healed.
And it is strengthened and restored within a month, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
On the restoration of a fractured shoulder.
The shoulder blade is broken in its broad part and only the edges are broken.
And so when its location is broken — and it is broken in its middle — and it is known only by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, endeavor to prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape in every ingenious way that is possible for you.
Then apply powder of ground spelt with egg white and tow [flax fiber] to the area; and then apply a folded cloth powder; and then apply a thin, tin-plated sheet of metal over it, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade, or a little more.
But if beneath, the plate is concave in any of the places of the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with tow, and then place the plate evenly.
And then tightly bind it above with long bandages, with appropriate constriction and firm placement of the plate so that it is not moved from its place.
And check the bandage every day; and whenever it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it and adjust the plate if it is moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty or twenty-five days.
And then when this number of days is complete, you dissolve/remove the bandage, and you should know for certain because this concerns bones whose joining is not feared nor their dissolution/separation.
And if you proceed from the location of the weighty bone and place the chain underneath, then you cut above it, and remove it, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the collarbone from the evacuation of an abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration/treatment of the fracture of the feet.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle and this heals slowly; but its extremities are often crushed and comminuted (splintered/broken into small pieces).
And among the accidents/complications when the middle of the foot is broken are that it deviates/bends inwards and causes great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and very easily one vomits blood; and a cavity/gap in the broken bone is evident and is not hidden to the touch.
And its treatment/restoration is that the patient should be laid on their back, and a cushion placed between their shoulders; and then press on their muscles, and adjust their ribs with your hands on both sides, and be subtle/gentle in your operation/treatment and in the setting, according to every way that is possible for this and that is appropriate to it, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
And then place a plaster and tow/padding on it and place on top a splint of thin willow or boxwood or similar light material, after you have wrapped it in cloth; and then be careful in tying it over the broken bone so that it is not moved; and go with the bandage sometimes around/circling the back/top, and tighten it with a suitable tightness; and then check the bandage constantly, and when it is loosened, tighten it.
And if necessity requires its untying, whether from chafing or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should carefully untie it and remove the plaster and correct whatever occurs from this with a method of healing it; and then reapply the plaster, if you see the need for it; and adjust the tightness until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration/treatment of ribs when they are broken/fractured.
Know that fracture of the ribs only occurs in the thick places that follow the back; and at their front extremities, no crushing occurs except because they are cartilaginous. And recognizing it is not hidden from feeling/touch through examination with the fingers.
And the restoration/treatment should be done as follows: align the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as it should be, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint, if you need one.
And if the rib fracture is pointing inwards, then the patient experiences intense pain and a piercing sensation, similar to the pain experienced by someone with pleurisy, because the bone pricks the membrane/pleura; and the patient has difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting a lot of blood; and this is difficult to treat/cure.
And the Ancients were indeed ingenious with that, with many devices/inventions.
And about them, I say this: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that cause swelling and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this because it is a considerable cause for creating/generating a hot abscess, unless it occurs naturally; and if it does occur, then it is already helping the situation.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed over it and then strongly sucked; and it seems reasonable, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass will attract superfluous fluids/humors to the area due to the weakness (of the tissues).
And some of them said: it is necessary that the area be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and that cushions/pads be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandaging is equal when you wrap it around according to the roundness/contour; and then the patient should be treated with the care/treatment for pleurisy, with respect to food and medicine.
And if a severe cause constricts the patient which he cannot bear, and the bone punctures the membrane with a damaging puncture, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary that we cut over the area, uncover the broken rib, and then place over it the instrument that preserves the membrane, the description of which has been given previously, and that we incise the bone easily/gently, and extract it.
And then we should join the two lips/edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess/internal ulcer develops inside, then you should take and soak pads/cushions in rose oil and place (them) over the area, and the patient should be treated as is appropriate for an internal abscess/ulcer.
And he should lie on his back, on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
On the restoration of a fracture of the spine of the back and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and subsequently suffers attrition/wearing away, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: for if you see his hands already softened, numb, and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor clench them, and when you squeeze them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most cases, his condition is fatal.
And if he moves them and feels the squeezing with your fingers and the pricking, then know that the spinal cord/nape of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the same thing occurs in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then examine his feet.
Then, if you see that they are already softened, the same thing happens to them as happened in the hands. Further, when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and bowel movements involuntarily, and sometimes when he lies supine on his back and wants to urinate, he cannot. Then, you should know that his case is fatal, and therefore, do not work on his treatment/cure.
But if none of these things occur, the case is better and easier.
And his treatment is that you strive for the reduction of the hot abscess that you place on the injured vertebra with rose oil alone, or with enough egg yolks. Apply this to it several times a day until the hot abscess is reduced.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and tighten it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to rest and be tranquil, and not to sleep on the affected side/part until he is healed.
And if it happens that due to the bone fracture, a fragment or something is separated from it, then it is necessary that you cut to the bone and remove that bone fragment; and then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat that with some incarnative (flesh-generating) ointment until it is healed.
But if the back of the tailbone is broken, and it is the end of the coccyx, then you should insert the little finger of your left hand into the anus, and align the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible by a second setting/reduction; and then apply a plaster and a splint, if you consider them necessary, and then tighten it.
And if you feel broken bone fragments in it, then operate on it, and remove that, and treat the wound with the aforementioned treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they break, then their fracture is only a splitting at their extremities, and they are incised lengthwise and deviate inward; and the patient experiences pain and pricking in the area, and the leg is numb when fractured.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture which has the form.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you adjust it according to what is apparent to you for proper alignment until it resembles its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or deviates downwards, then you have the patient lie on their stomach so that the restoration of that fracture can be prepared. And then when you have aligned it, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or leather on top of that, and tighten it firmly so that you don't fear puncture from the fracture or movement of the splint.
And adjust the hollow area by filling it in so that it takes the binding evenly, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease extending and restoring it until the hot abscess has subsided, and apply something that soothes it as described before; and then return to its restoration and bind it as is necessary.
And if there are cracks or comminuted fractures in the bones, then you treat them. (Or perhaps, "then you extract/remove them," depending on the precise meaning of "tratz" in this context.)
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of a fracture of the clavicle (or possibly "shoulder blade," depending on the precise meaning of "adjutori" in this medical context – it could refer to a bone that "aids" arm movement).
The *adjutori* is that which is between the elbow and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is done in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat piece of wood of moderate thickness according to this shape:
And at the two ends two ties should be attached, and then it should be suspended from a raised location/place.
And have the patient sit on a chair; then extend their broken arm over the wood until it reaches its end, in the middle of which the curve of the wood should be placed. (Implying the curve accommodates the elbow.)
And then suspend some heavy object over that, or have the assistant extend it downwards; and then the physician should align the fracture with their hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other method is that the patient should lie on their back and suspend their hand from their neck with a tie; then instruct two assistants, one of them to hold the part above the fracture with their hands; and the other to hold the part below it; and each of them should extend it towards themselves.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten above the fracture with a tie and above that with another tie, and each of the two assistants extends toward their respective side.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the tie above the elbow and the other below the fracture towards the shoulder.  (Or possibly "towards the inside of the elbow" depending on the exact meaning of "coyde.")
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the tie be placed above that place and above that elbow.
Align it with ease and without force, and then when it is aligned as it should be and applied with a good application, then tighten it unless a hot swelling (abscess/inflammation) occurs.
But if a hot swelling (abscess/inflammation) occurs, then loosen the bandaging until the seventh day, and place upon it clean wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the swelling subsides, and then tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place his hand open on his muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm against the support so that the arm is in a star-shaped position, unless something about the broken bone prevents this.
And then use splints.
And place over the fracture the splint that is the widest and strongest of the other splints; and leave between each splint the width of a finger, and the length of the splints should be according to the break with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten over the splints with the bandage that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And your bandaging over the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the further away the bandaging is from the fracture, the looser it should be.
And so, if you see the placement of the splints and the bandaging as we have described at the moment when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do [as follows/proceed].
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandaging and the splints in place for seven days, as we have said.
And then examine the bandaging on all sides for three days so that itching or swelling does not occur in the area; or [so that] food is not prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandaging; and so correct all of this according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage for about three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their hands on their stomach.
And place a cooked dressing filled with wool evenly over it, and ensure at all hours of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted or dissolved, or that the bandage is softened, so correct all of this with your skill.
And administer the patient's food according to the order that we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until [the bone] begins to knit together; and then you should increase the richness of their food.
For it is usual for the dressing and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then they should be untied, and a bath administered, and medicated with ointments that heal it.
And if the fracture is severely bruised, then you do not remove the bandage and splints from it for fifty days or for two months.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two *focils*; of which one is small, and it is the one located by the thumb, and the other is large and is the one placed under the smaller one, in the lower part.  (Note: "focils" likely refers to the radius and ulna.)
And it is very easy to break the larger *focil* only, or the smaller one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small *focil* is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is faster.
And when the *focil* is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small *focil* above, then it is fitting for the physician, after its restoration, to apply a small extension gently until it is aligned.
But if the large *focil* is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both *focils* are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the hand (in a specific position) after its reduction and extension, laid out on a cushion, and the wrist should be at the uppermost part, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a square chair/stool.
And there should be a cushion in front of him at his height or behind him so that the patient does not experience discomfort/difficulty.
And then the assistant should extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant should similarly extend from above.
And then the physician should adjust the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if there is bleeding in the bone fracture, then you must reduce every piece to its place with your skill.
And if separated bleeding appears there that punctures the skin and there is no hope for its restoration, then you should act upon it and remove it according to the method we have described above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for this, so take the treatment from that.
And if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then you should line a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be middling between thickness and thinness, and tightly wrap the cloth over it with gentle pressure until the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from ground flour with egg white, and then apply splints; and the splint that is placed over that fracture should be the widest and strongest.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in the most serious situations, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
And then apply your bandaging over the location of the fracture most strongly and firmly, and as the bandaging gets further from the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the bandaging a little more loosely according to what was mentioned previously at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft, damp cloths, and they should not be hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tied should itself be of a middling thickness between thin and thick, as we have recounted.
And examine the limb and the bandaging after a few days. And if any issue arises which needs correcting, such as itching which occurs on the limb, then you need to bathe the limb with warm water until that itching stops.
And leave the limb unbound until it rests, and then reapply the bandaging.
And if the dressing is now softened and the bone has moved again and suchlike, then correct all of that with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandaging, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it.
But if none of the things we have mentioned occur to the patient, then it does not need to be untied for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own power of voluntary movement, and have him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the strength of the constitution.
Chapter .xiii. (or Chapter 13)
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the thumb and the knuckles of the fingers, and what happens to them more often is bruising.
And when a fracture or bruise occurs in the hand, then it is necessary that the patient sits down and in front of him you arrange a pillow evenly, and then place his hand on it, extended.
And then the assistant extends the broken bones and the physician aligns them until they are joined with good alignment/joined well.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and padding, unless a hot abscess/inflammation occurs; and then apply a splint over it according to the size of the area, after you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the break is on the inner parts/towards the palm of the hand, then make a ball-like shape of cloth, and tell the patient to squeeze it with his broken palm/hand; then have it tightened/bound with a long cloth.
And there should be a splint made of leather with cloth in/on which it is raised, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts/back of the hand, then it is necessary to apply a splint over it and another splint from below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, just as the hand is tilted towards the small finger among the fingers with bandages/ties.
And if a fracture happens to any of the finger joints/knuckles, then if it is the thumb, it should be made even/aligned as needed and then be bound/held against the palm and, if you wish, apply a small straight splint to it so that the fracture is elevated and cannot be moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, or the index finger, or the little finger, or the ring finger, then it should be aligned and bound with the finger next to it; or they can all be bound together in a row, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and therefore apply it, the characteristics of a hot abscess and treat it — with your work — with what is needed when any of these things occur, as its description/explanation has been repeated.
Chapter 14.
On the treatment of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken, and it is evident to the touch depending on whether it is displaced towards the front or the upper part/outer surface.
And its treatment is done thus: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient should be lying prone (on their stomach) and face down.
And then the practitioner should extend the entire bandage in its respective direction, according to the nature of the injury, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then the bandage should be near the groin towards the pubic area, so that the extension pulls upward, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is close to the knee, then the bandage should be made close to the knee, and the extension should be downwards/toward the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bones with both his hands until it is returned to its natural form and the bone is joined in proper alignment; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess develops on the limb. But if an abscess develops there, then you leave it for a few days until the heat of the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done like this: it is necessary to wrap a firm bandage over the fracture, and double it two or three times, and remove the excess; and then wrap the leg until you reach the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee downwards, and bring the ends of the splint from the highest part on both sides, and then wrap over the leg and thigh that which is superfluous from the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one of these splints on the bones of the leg.
And then fill the empty spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandaging is padded.
And then begin with tight bandaging in the middle, over the location of the fracture, with three or four wraps, and the further you move with the bandaging from the location of the fracture, your bandaging should be less and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandaging is not moved from its place.
And then leave the bandaging on it, but in such a way that neither corrosion nor hot swelling or inflammation and things similar to these occur to the limb; and if any of these things occur, then quickly undo the bandaging, and correct what has happened immediately, according to what we have said many times.
But if in the bone there is a piece of bone which protrudes, then you must straighten it as much as is possible for you; and if not, work upon it and replace it, and treat it with that which has been mentioned before until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened other than by splints being adjusted to it, as we have described in regard to the shoulder and the arm.
But however, in the restoration of it done in such a manner the patient doesn't develop lameness; but if it is restored alone, without the other leg being applied to it, then it is inevitable that he will always limp.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in forty days or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap.
Know that fracture of the kneecap happens late/rarely, but contusion often occurs to it.
But if a fracture happens to it, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and over all of this there has been sensation/feeling.
And its restoration is thus: that which is broken of its parts should be aligned with the said (instruments/methods) until it is joined and connected as much as possible through equalization, facilitation, and fabrication/making.
And then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and tighten it from above with a bandage suitable for this.
And then consider all the arrangements that we have recounted in the other fractures, such as a hot abscess and things similar to them, so that you may oppose every complication with what is suitable to it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is large, and is called by the name of the leg (tibia); and the other is slender, and is called fibula.
And it happens with each of the types of fracture that which happens to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done just like the restoration of the arm, and the procedure for it is the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is turned in all directions (or distorted).
And if the more slender bone is broken, the leg is turned towards the front.
And if the larger bone is broken, you will find that one underneath, and it is among those cases that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension and setting (or alignment) and binding of the splints in the same manner.
But it is necessary, however, if the fracture of the leg is large and there are many injuries, that the extension be less and lighter, and ease it with your skill.
And in the leg there is some similarity of procedure to the arm, and it is when you align the splints and complete all of your work, then you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the thighs which are placed on sheets, and are made likewise of palm branches; choose those of small thickness, and not at all from the knotted parts, and their length should be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the heel.
And then wrap each one in cloth with two windings along its length, and place one on the side of the leg on one part and the other on the other part, and they should be from the knee to the lowest place of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places, at the two ends and in the middle, for with this binding the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left side, and it remains in a good position.
And a wooden splint has already been prepared according to the length of the leg; and let the leg be placed on it so that it keeps it from movement.
And especially you should do this in a fracture, and when with that there is a proper wound.
And then examine the leg every day and study it with due diligence, and watch for abscesses and swelling or other similar things; and if any of these things occur, then attend to it with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
On the fracture of the heel and the bones of the foot and the toes.
The heel bone does not typically fracture completely; but the bones of the foot may fracture sometimes; and the toes rarely fracture, experiencing only contusions in most cases.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see that these bones are displaced, then have the patient place their foot on the ground as if walking.
Then place one foot on the elevated part of those bones, and then press down and even them out until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and padding on top, and place a small splint with two flat ends under the arch of the foot.
And then tighten it against the foot with a suitable bandage after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary things.
And when three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones aligned, whether they were broken or dislocated, and the procedure for them is as you know.
But if someone says they are broken, then restore them and align them as I have explained to you in the restoration of the said hand. Then apply a splint to the broken toe, according to the length of the toe, and let it be a little wider than it; and then place under the foot this board which I have mentioned to you, and tighten it with a suitable bandage.
But if two, three, or more bones are broken, then you place a splint of the appropriate size, wrapped in linen cloth, on each toe, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, and let one end of the board extend beyond the sole of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is fitting for you not to forget to consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
On the fracture of the woman's vulva and the pubic bone and the man's penis.
When the woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit in a square chair, then incline her a little to one side, and let her be held from behind.
And then let the midwife fill the vulva with cotton, and let it be done in her vulva like a ball; and then let the woman be shaken, and raise her buttocks little by little, because everything is thrown out until it is in front of the vulva like a ball and the broken bone returns.
And then place cushions on her buttocks, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton with ease and then she urinates, and replace and return it in the manner in which you placed it at the beginning, and then return to her first cushion.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tighten a reed cannula over its opening; and insert the entire bladder into her vulva, and then blow forcefully into the cannula until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture will return (to place).
And then let it be filled with cotton and remain for some days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and splinting what we have described for the hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby fractures which rarely occur, especially for him who has some study and use and understands these words of my book; for that fracture indicates to you in most cases the correct way in its restoration and binding, and therefore understand well.
But when the penis of a man is broken, take the windpipe of a goose, and place the penis in it; then place a wrap of cloth over it, and let it be bandaged, and then let it be left for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is a plague/infection/disease.  (The word "plagua" can have several meanings, so context from the upcoming sentences will refine it).
It is fitting for him who suffers a fracture with infection (plagua seems to mean infection here), especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone or the hip bone, or similar to it, that you attend to and phlebotomize (bleed) him immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy so indicate, as we have said above.
And if the infection discharges blood, then it is necessary that you attend to stopping it/its retention thus: you should sprinkle ground burnt alum over it, if no other remedy is available to you.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong/delay this, unless an abscess occurs/develops.
But if a hot abscess occurs/develops, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for you will cause bad complications to arise.
But if the broken bone is protruding and exposed above the skin, then it is necessary that you endeavor to reduce it and align it with your hands gently and with little extension/pulling.
But if you are not prepared for extension and its alignment with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and it is an instrument which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers and the width of which is according to the size of the wound/affected area; and for this it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these depending on the amount needed for this treatment in all sorts of fractures.
And let it be round, and let it have little thickness so that it may not bend under pressure during the operation.
And let it be of sharp extremity, which should have a margin at its ends/edges; and let its top be tending towards thickness, and from its middle towards the ends/parts.
And this is its form/shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", that is, small spatula/blade.
Because it is necessary that its sharp elevated extremity be placed on the extremity of the protruding bone.
And push it in one go until the bone returns and is leveled with some balance/alignment.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture with each other, and if the end of the broken bone is thin, and the instrument does not grip it well, then you must incise the end of that bone until the instrument can grip it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have described, then incise it with what is suitable for it from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient experiences intensely sharp pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do this for you would confer great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then submerge cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound a cerate or other thing containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then apply bandages at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered until you cut the hole with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution not to tighten the wound with the fracture, for foolish doctors have often done this, and caused death or corrosive illness or suppuration, that is, pus, in their patients. And if your bandaging is light, soft, different from the bandaging of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad complications which we have mentioned, and you find pain in the place, then you should not put splints on it, but make a wrapping of hard cloths instead of splints and tighten it with them.
And when, after a day or two, the wound begins to generate proud flesh, then you remove the cloth that you had placed on it with force, and then apply some ointments, with which we customarily treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And you should untie the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and the limb should be positioned in such a way that the pus drains from it to the lower parts easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not consolidated nor is the proud flesh cut away from it, then know that there are small sinuses present, therefore you should explore the wound with a probe. And of those sinuses, those that are separated [from healthy tissue], remove and extract [them]; those that are not separated and which put pressure on the limb and cause pain, you must necessarily incise them as much as possible.
And if there occurs in the wound ichor or sanies, that is, a foul discharge, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you must treat each of these with what is appropriate for it, the treatment of which has been previously described in its chapter.
And regarding this, which should be the subject of my discourse, pay close attention: when a large bone is broken, such as the bones of the thigh and upper arm and similar large limbs, do not attempt to set it nor extract it because death often occurs from this;  leave it until it rots, for it will fall out very easily on its own after about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if it seems appropriate to you, and if not, leave it.
Chapter 20.
On the treatment of the nodule that occurs in the trace/site of a fracture.
This nodule often occurs at the site of fracture healing, especially near the joints, and the shape of the limb is deformed by it; and it very quickly hinders the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the nodule is recent and administer astringent medicines such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, dragon's blood, acacia, and similar ones; thus, take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and place it on the nodule with a cloth, and bind it tightly over it with a good bandage.
And leave this dressing on, not removing it for many days; and then remove it and apply another, until the nodule is reduced; or tighten a lead plate suitably on it, because lead has the property of reducing any swelling in the limbs.
But if the nodule has already hardened into stone and is tight and necessity compels its careful removal, then cut it at its highest point, and excise the excess and protruding part, or scrape it with a scalpel until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is healed and yet the limb remains thin and cold, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is healed, and yet the limb remains thin and cold, this happens for many reasons: one of which is an excessive dissolving of the ligament and improper bandaging; or by excessive tightness of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or by an excessive amount of superfluous embrocation (ointment/liniment); or by excessive movement at the wrong time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient and soften their heart until the blood is increased in them; and administer baths; and introduce joy and happiness to them.
And then place pitch on the limb, so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or continually apply embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and the limb returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are healed crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
When a limb, already healed, exhibits crookedness and a protrusion or knot at the broken bone near its healed point, and there is therefore deformity of the limb's shape, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then it is not necessary for you to accept the advice of someone who recommends that the limb be re-broken.
But there have been many foolish doctors and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this practice is very reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.  (Implying the fear can be so great it can cause death, or that the procedure is risky and can be fatal.)
But if the lameness and knot are recent, then they should be fomented with warm water in which softening herbs have been cooked, such as marshmallow leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a plaster should be made with a softening plaster like a well-made diachylon; or mucilage from marshmallow root can be taken and mixed with chicken fat or turpentine oil, and plastered with that; or ripe figs can be taken and ground with pigeon droppings, and similar medicines which are called consolidatives/strengtheners.
And sometimes it is dissolved with frequent light rubbing, done with moistened hands, and movement of the limb should be encouraged in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has already progressed and become hard and turned to bone, and the necessity for its treatment with iron (surgical instruments) is apparent, then the pus above must be drained and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and the excess knot along with the bone should be cut away with fine chisels/incision instruments, and care and attention should be applied to this with diligence and study.
Chapter 23.
Regarding/Concerning dislocation.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, whereby movement is hindered, and the shape of the limb is distorted, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to immediately ensure that it is reduced, and do not delay this reduction, because if it is delayed, the area will become abscessed and thus the reduction of the dislocation will quickly become difficult.
And for this reason it should not be moved or stretched during the time of abscess formation, because often this causes spasm and severe pain.
But when this occurs, then you must attend to the patient's phlebotomy (blood-letting), and then allow some time for the abscess to subside; and then the limb should be rubbed with warm water and oil, and then it can be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will be mentioned in its proper place. (i.e. the appropriate treatment will be described later in the text.)
And I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what was previously done for fractures; from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two mandibles are dislocated thus if not treated quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved from their places by a slight movement, and are only slightly softened; or they are dislocated by a complete and total dislocation, to the point that they are softened at the part of the joint so that saliva runs from the patient and they cannot hold it or close their mouth, and their tongue is impeded in their speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns to its place by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then it is necessary that reduction be administered gently, and not delayed at all.
And the assistant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the mandibular dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside with which he levers; and he instructs the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move in every direction; and the physician levers the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
And if its reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer fomentation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then when they are returned and leveled, and the patient's mouth is closed and not loose, then place cloth compresses with a cerate [ointment/plaster] on them, made of wax and rose oil; and then bandage gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head rest between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left side; and do not force them to chew anything; let their food be like broth, until when the pain is relieved and the jaws are healed, then they may chew what they want.
And this should be administered gently, and do not force the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then often fevers and persistent hiccups occur, and the patient's belly is very easily purged, and they very easily vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is fatal; and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side due to its connection with the chest/ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated on the outer part, and this is evident to the touch/sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then squeeze the area with your palm with strong pressure, for it will return (to place).
And then place a plaster and powders on it and bind/bandage it.
But the extremity of the one that follows the elbow and is joined with it, is not dislocated in any way; therefore it must be reduced and matched as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
And then place a plaster, and powders, and a bandage on it, and order the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
