Know that a muscle is dislocated only in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated at the part of the navel(?) downward.  (The word "titillic" is unclear and could be a misspelling or a specific anatomical term not easily translated.)
And the second is that it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this is done late/slowly(?); and it is not dislocated at the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the parts in front because of the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and comes out at the lower parts below the navel(?), and especially in those whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it very easily happens to some men by a blow or a fall, that their muscle becomes infected with a hot abscess/infection, and it is thought because of this that it is dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you examine this until you are absolutely certain about it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And its dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts below the navel(?); so that you can distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the navel(?) under the touch of the head of the muscle something like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all directions.
If it is dislocated under the foot or above, then you will find a noticeable lump, and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the practitioner lifts the patient's hand upwards; and then places his thumbs under the patient's navel(?), and strongly lifts the joint upwards to its place, and the practitioner raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it down, for it returns easily.
But if it does not return by what we have said, and it is from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days ago, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in hot water and an emollient and softening embrocation be administered, such as boiled roots of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and melilot in water, and let it be administered.
And then the patient should lie supine on their back and a ball of wool, which is balanced between softness and firmness, should be placed under their navel(?). And then the physician should place their heel on the ball, and forcefully lift the head of the muscle, and pull the patient's hand so that it is not moved downwards, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not moved downwards, for then it quickly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this way: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side of the affected area, and put his arm under the patient's navel(?) and lift it upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another practitioner pulls the patient's hand from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with them to weigh them down, because the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another way: a long stick is fixed in the ground, the top of which is round in shape, like a pestle, that is neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; then linen cloths are placed over the top of the stick, and the patient stands lengthwise along the stick, and then their hand is extended downwards on the other side; and their body is extended forcefully on the other side, because the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a stick the length of which is two cubits, and its width is four fingers, and its thickness is eased at the tip for the navel(?) according to this form:
And then linen cloths should be tied over the rounded end so that the stick does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the patient's navel(?), and the hand or arm is extended over the stick downwards, and the stick is tied to the helper and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a stair rung horizontally, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint will return immediately.
And when its reduction is complete according to whatever manner is possible for you, then it is necessary to place under the navel(?) wool stuffing of equal quantity; and then place a plaster prepared from ground mill-dust with vinegar and egg white over the whole muscle, thus covering it over, and then tightly bind it under the navel(?) with a suitable bandage; and the binding is wrapped over the plaster, and their hand is tied to their neck, and left like that, and their hand may not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be kept meager, until the limb is strengthened, for it is thus easier for them to heal.
And then more food may be given after five or seven days and some movement undertaken; and so if it is firm and not soft, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to excess moisture or for another reason, then it is necessary in that case to administer the cautery with three burns, as was previously said in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the binding is loosened and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandaging are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and so they cannot raise that arm upwards, then know that their nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are now incised or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation of that which is made below the feet and the breast or the back parts, the reduction is done by pushing and extending with the hands, until it returns (to its place).
And another bandaging should be applied to it, and treat it until it is healed.
And if heaviness occurs in the limb near the healing and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original state.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward, and its dislocation is not hidden from you for it falls under sight or touch according to how it is dislocated, and when you compare the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, this is clearly revealed to you, because the joint is concave and the patient cannot bend their arm, nor touch their shoulder with it.
And you must be diligent and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot swelling comes to it, because if a hot swelling comes to it, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is located towards the back, because it is worse than all the other types of dislocation, and more painful, and many times death follows from it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand be extended with two hands, and their arm be spread out, and the physician's hands be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint together with his thumbs, or with the heel of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, then return it thus: bend the patient's hand with your hand, until you strike the elbow with the heel of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to the reduction, then apply vehement and very strong extension.
And two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it has been wrapped with a long folded cloth, or bandage, over the hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that it may facilitate the lubrication of the joint; and then push the joint vehemently until it returns (to its place).
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed on it, which has astringent and drying properties with egg white, and it be tightened with a downward binding, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left (like that) for some days.
And then it should be untied; and then if the joint is set in its place, you untie its bandage and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not well tightened, then you repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and then untie it.
But if, after the reduction of the joint, heaviness occurs, and there is movement (instability) in it, then administer moistening with a bath and gentle rubbing, and compression, until it is relieved; or place emollients, that is, a fatty substance, on the joint; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bones day and night. And afterwards remove it, and put him/her in a bath; and when he/she sweats, then rub the joint with a moderate friction, and afterwards repeat the application of the emollients, that is, pork lard, once, and a second time, and a third time. with baths in between, until it is softened/supple.
And if you wish, place on it a piece of moist cow dung warmed with butter and press upon it; and do this several times, for it is moist and restores it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
The wrist is sometimes dislocated, and its reduction is easy, unlike other joints, but it is necessary that the reduction be performed quickly at the moment it is dislocated, before the area becomes swollen or develops a hot abscess.
And the reduction of the dislocation is done by placing the patient's wrist joint on a table and having an assistant extend his hand, while the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns to its place.
But it must be considered whether the palm has been turned towards the belly of the hand.  In this case, the patient places the back of his hand on a table during the extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inwards on the table, so that the physician's hand falls upon that prominence of the joint.
And thus if it returns quickly, good; and if not, then bandage it with a softening plaster to reduce the swelling and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise it will not be tolerated nor will you be able to reduce it after several days have passed.
But even if the joint remains in its twisted state, and the patient feels no pain, except that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything; then know that the nerves are broken or torn, and then there is no remedy except to cauterize it near the affected area, and this will soon improve matters.
Then when the sickness has returned, apply the plaster we have recommended, and let it be bound and tightened for five days; then it should be loosened and left off, and the hand exercised.
But if its movement is hindered and some heaviness occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction a few times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every direction.
And when any finger is dislocated towards the back of the hand or the palm, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns (to its place).
And then support the tip of the finger, and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned on that same day. (Meaning the second day).
And when night comes, bind it according to that description, and don't stop unbinding it during the day and exercising it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is healed/firm.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand, and bind it on that side and perform your initial treatment with it until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other directions.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck occurs, and many vertebrae are moved from their places, then there is no cure in that case, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and often some of their limbs are paralyzed, either both legs, or both arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is moved from its place, for often it is moved and often the displacement of these occurs in all four directions.
Thus, when it is displaced towards the back, it is called a hump/gibbosity.
But the treatment for it is that you consider whether the hump occurred from a childhood ailment, for then there is no cure or complete healing.
But for that which comes from a fall, or a blow, or something similar, the Ancients have already described the methods of treatment in lengthy discourse, most of which is beneficial; but I have abbreviated it, a little of which excuses its excess, for the sake of conciseness in my intended exposition, with which I have explained and demonstrated this using different instruments from those with which I will show and elaborate on this.
And of these, only the one that occurs on the back is treated properly with what I have described.
And the patient should be laid face down on a level surface near a wall, with a soft covering placed underneath so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the surface near his head, and another pole opposite his feet at the other end of the surface.
And an assistant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another assistant should hold the other pole in the same way.
And then wrap a strong linen bandage over the patient's head and under his armpits firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is behind his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage over his hips, his buttocks, and behind his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is behind his feet.
And then each assistant should extend the bandage towards the end of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be moved from their places where they are fixed, but they are not fixed as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms strongly upon the vertebra until it returns (to place), or he should place a board over it, and then place his feet upon the board until it returns (to place).
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three cubits, and dig a place in the wall (which we have mentioned is near the patient) into which the end of the board can enter. And then place half of the board over the vertebra, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the vertebra is reduced and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a windlass which is turned by hand; that is, you fix in the ground behind the patient's head two poles, the length of which should be one cubit, and likewise two poles behind his head [this seems repetitive, perhaps "feet"?]; and the distance between each pole should be a palm, and a hole should be made in each pole through which a rope runs, and each one should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it cannot be moved.
And let a round pole, on which the windlass is, be placed and turned in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end there is a hole in which the pole is inserted, the length of which is a palm, along with the part that is turned; and in the other two poles there is a similar arrangement.
And then tighten the bandages that you have tied on the patient's feet in the hole which is behind his head, and the bandages that you have tied on his thighs in the hole which is behind his feet. [Or perhaps, "the windlass which is towards his feet."]
And then next to each windlass there should be an assistant who turns the windlass with his hand, and the physician straightens the spine as we have described.
And then after the vertebra is returned, and the place is straightened, then it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then tow; and then place on the plaster a splint of wood whose width is three fingers or about that, and its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump, and over the part of the protruding vertebra.
And then it should be bound with the bandage that we have described, and the patient should use/have a light diet until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that softening and cleansing medicines be administered with the application of the board for a long time, which we have recounted; and then a sheet of lead is applied to that place.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the dorsal vertebrae, and it is thought to be due to that dislocation; and it is that bone which is already prominent, so no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very easily cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint suffer only dislocation, and they do not experience what happens to other joints, namely slight displacement and concavity/subluxation.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you compare the patient's healthy leg with the injured one, it is longer and the buttock is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the groin, and the place near the groin is swollen with a hot swelling, because the head of the femur has moved there.
And the sign of the one who has an outward dislocation is that their symptoms are opposite to this.
And a sign of the one who has a forward dislocation is that they can extend their leg fully, but they cannot bend it without pain in the buttock, and if they force themselves to walk, they cannot do so forwards; and their urine is retained and their groin is swollen, and next they walk on their heel.
And a sign of the one who has a backward dislocation is that they cannot extend the buttock, nor can they bend it forward without bending the groin, and their leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is soft, and the head of the femur is near that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and longstanding, that it is already fixed by having it near; you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not completely curable; and then no presumption should be made in that case.
But in the one whose dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, then you should strive to return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it returns very easily and does not need other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend their leg from the lower part, either with their hands or with a bandage which is tied on the leg above the buttock; and another assistant should extend their hand from above so that they place their hands under their armpits; and then tightly tie a linen bandage over the base of the thigh, and have another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be either from the inside of the groin towards the fork (perineum/pubis), or from the back towards the back.
And let the extension of all these be at once, until the patient is lifted with their body off the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And so if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then for each type it is necessary to describe the specific treatment.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the patient be lying on their healthy side; then place the bandages on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the highest part of the perineum/pubis.
And then another strong assistant should take his arms and grasp the thick part of the diseased thigh and extend outwards with strong extension, for it will return to its place.
And this type, with which this limb is reduced, is of easier treatment than the other types.
But if it is difficult for you and you cannot manage entry with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary that the two feet of the patient be tied together with a strong, light bandage, above both ankles and both knees.
And the length of each of its parts should be the amount of four fingers, and let the diseased leg be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended over the top of the wood, and it should be in the house; and his distance from the ground should be the amount of two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong youth to pull down on the top of the thigh, and another youth should be suspended with the patient, and the other youth pulling down on the thigh with strength should push, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when there is dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back according to what we have recounted in the chapter on humpback; and the bandage should be tightened on the diseased leg specifically and upon his feet.
And then two pieces of wood should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then fitted pieces of wood should be placed in the middle of the mound placed on which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed by the wood being between his thighs, so that he should not be pulled by the extension downwards.
And then each assistant should extend their part, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the forward parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and this is according to the same description, upon a mound; and the physician places the palm of his right hand over the diseased groin, and then presses it with the other hand, and with this pressure pushes it downwards toward the painful area.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be lowered, and he is raised above the ground; he should be placed on a hard surface, just as the leveling is necessary if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips; and he is on his face and the ligaments are extended, according to what we have said, not long ago.
And compression must be administered with a board over the place where the joint protrudes.
Then when the reduction is complete as it should be—and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient stretches his leg and spreads it without hesitation, then you know that the limb has returned as it should—and then you arrange two cushions and apply a plaster and bind it with a bandage so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and the patient should adhere to rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is already healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening (inflammation/swelling), then you return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him delay walking on it for some days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, and more to the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that when you command the patient to bring his leg up to his thigh, then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all its forms is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can; then you put your two hands on his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then make his knee adhere with your palms, and hold them with your fingers strongly upon his knee, and another assistant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg will then adhere to the thigh with easy, unimpeded movement. (literally: with non-laborious lightness)
And then apply a plaster to it and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then untie them.
And the patient should not walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the bindings, of which there was previous mention in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of dislocation of the ankle/heel. ( _talo_ likely refers to the talus bone, hence ankle or heel)
The ankle/heel is sometimes moved slightly, and sometimes it is completely dislocated, and its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle/heel swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight movement is easy reduction, and it is that it be extended gently with the hands and adjusted until it returns (to its place).
But in its treatment when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind his back, in the middle of his back.
And then you take with your right hand the foot from the highest part/top, and with your left hand the lower part of the foot in the area of the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left twice, and then extend with your left; and push the toes of the foot with your right towards the leg without exertion/force.
And do this twice, as we have described.
And then push the toes of the foot towards the leg the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then return (the foot) once or twice in this manner, and if you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return (to normal).
But if you are prevented from reducing it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and firmly fix a very strong cloth to the ground, which should be placed between their thighs, and you have already wrapped cloth over them so that you do not hurt the patient.
And then have their assistant hold their thighs, and then have another assistant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied above the ankle; and then let each assistant extend it against the other's pull, and let the cloth be stretched between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that their body is not pulled downwards during its extension.
And then the physician sets the dislocation with their hands, and another assistant holds the healthy thigh downwards, so that the dislocation returns easily.
And when the dislocation returns (to its proper place) and the success of its reduction is apparent to you, then apply a plaster and secure it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg.
And you should be wary of/take care with the tendons that are behind the heel, so that the bandaging on it is not too tight and does not injure it severely.
And then leave it like that for two or three days; and if the bandage loosens, tighten it.
And then untie it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not become loose and disrupted again, and then it may not be curable.
And if a hot abscess develops, then you must administer to soothe it that which we have described previously, in another place, regarding treatment and embrocation, until it is removed/reduced.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated toe be straightened with little extension without force; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the back of the foot, then you must have the patient place their foot on a level spot on the ground or on a table, and stand as if they were walking.
And then you stand near their foot and place your foot on the part beyond the affected joints, and press on it with your foot forcefully until it returns (to place), and you see that it is straightened and no protrusion appears at the location.
And then place a splint under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot, which has two ends, and then tighten it with moderate and firm bandaging for three days; and then loosen it and let them rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and healing is assured.
Chapter 35.
Regarding the types of dislocation that are caused by a blow or by a fracture or by any of these together.
When you encounter any of these cases, and you exert yourself in its treatment and in the patient's recovery, death often occurs.
And therefore, no one should presume to treat it except someone who is wise in the art, with long experience, merciful and compassionate, cautious, not rash nor presumptuous; and who uses, in the beginning, medicines that only reduce hot swellings/inflammations, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for the one in whom you hope for salvation from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, and in which some hope appears. And then strive to immediately perform the reduction at the beginning of the event, before a hot swelling/inflammation develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that reduces hot swelling/inflammation and treats the wound with appropriate drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and you find separated bone fragments, then you must secure their union/setting, and do in this what we have said about simple maladies of this type that has been previously described in their places.
And watch out and avoid, according to your ability, entering into dangerous procedures as my preaching has forewarned you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary for your true purpose in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which God be praised. Amen.
